                                 Books by the Author
1. 3D Unconditional Security—graphical overview of principle perspectives regarding unconditional security.
2. 4D Forgiveness—examines four levels of biblical forgiveness and explains how it is possible for God to
    forgive and forget our sin in one stratum while not forgiving or forgetting our sin in another stratum.
3. Breaking the Rocking Horse—introductory book regarding the distinction between having eternal life as a
    gift versus obtaining it as a reward in relation to the outer darkness and the parable of the soils.
4. Carnal Corinth—an affirmation of the categorical existence of carnal Corinthian Christians.
5. Destined for Salvation—evaluates five major models for interpreting 1Thess 5:9-10 and takes the passage
    as promising an unconditional rapture (and thus unconditional security) to all believers in Christ, even to
    those who are morally asleep. However, an incentive for being morally awake is provided by making
    salvation (in the form of millennial and eternal rulership) contingent upon being morally alert.
6. Fallen from Grace but Not from Perfection—an advanced defense of unconditional security which affirms
    that believers can fall from grace but not fall from perfection.
7. Is God a Communist?—by comparing Paul’s teaching of the workmen with Jesus’ parable of the laborers,
    this book refutes communistic views of Heaven which think that Jesus is building heavenly mansions in
    New Jerusalem for all believers regardless of the way they live.
8. John in Living Color—a color-coded translation and reference manual of the Gospel of John that focuses
    on the grammatical and aspectual indications of unconditional security.
9. Like a Tree Planted by the Water—expounds upon the OT background for the outer darkness by examining
    the precursor texts in Psalms, especially Ps 1-2.
10. Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity—a more comprehensive discussion regarding perseverance (and
    other performance-related themes) as being needed for rewards in Heaven rather than for entrance into
    Heaven. This book provides both introductory material and advanced treatments.
11. Monogamous Sex in Heaven!—at the same time in which the present author was reading books by Marrow
    and Kreeft posing that sexual intimacy continues in Heaven, he discovered that his wife had stage-3 breast
    cancer. As a therapy to help him deal with this news, he poured himself into research to see if Marrow and
    Kreeft might be correct, thereby enabling him to believe that no matter what happened, the best was yet to
    come. This is the conclusion and comfort of this book.
12. New Birth by the Sovereign Wind—refutes the Calvinistic interpretation of Jn 3:16 that limits God’s love
    to the elect. An excursus deals with the Calvinistic claim from Jn 3:3 that one must be born again before
    one can see (i.e., believe) the gospel. An additional excursus entertains a possible duality to what it means
    to see the kingdom.
13. Predestination and the Open View—a discussion of predestination that focuses on free will and the book
    of life from an open view.
14. Rewards are Eternal—is a defense of the eternal nature of biblical rewards against the proposition that
    Rev 22:5 indicates that all believers will rule in the eternal state and against the premise that Rev 21:4
    proves that misthological death will no longer exist in the eternal state.
15. Sealed and Secure—a thorough defense of a popular argument for unconditional security that also defends
    the premise of unconditional promises.
16. The Race of Grace—introductory book in which performance-related themes (such as perseverance) are
    considered essential for rewards in Heaven. Entrance into Heaven, in contrast, is regarded as free. A strong
    defense of unconditional security derived from believers being given to Jesus is a strength of this book.
17. The Outer Darkness—a magnum opus that ties together the author’s arguments for unconditional security
    and conditional rewards.
18. Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep—a rejection of the notion that profession of salvation is proven genuine
    by the way a person lives. This book focuses on the parable of the sheep and the goats and shows the
    inadequacy of conditional security in interpreting this parable.

                     For books by the author, see http://misthology.org/?page_id=35.
                      For additional material, see www.UnconditionalSecurity.org.

                 Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies
                                                     Introduction
        Will the bodies of all believers shine in resplendent glory? Some misthologists (i.e., experts in the doctrine
of rewards) anticipate that all resurrected believers will have soteriologically glorified bodies, but only faithful
believers will have misthologically co-glorified bodies.1 Other misthologists conclude that all resurrected believers
will have redeemed bodies, but only faithful believers will have glorified bodies. The latter position would deny
that all believers will shine in glory. Although I have favored the former position, I have been reluctant to describe
unfaithful believers as having glorified bodies that would shine. Until now, I anticipated that they would have
spiritual bodies that should be able to shine but would fail to shine. They might be compared to a light bulb that
lacks the wattage necessary to shine. Thus, I took a mediating approach.
        Based on four primary texts (Col 3:4; 1Cor 15:43; Phil 3:21; 1Jn 3:2), my assessment, heretofore, has been
that all resurrected believers will have transformed, glorified bodies since they will all be transformed and revealed
with Christ in glory—in terms of having an imperishable spiritual body. Yet only overcomers would have co-
glorified bodies that actually shine (Rom 8:17; 1Cor 15:41). Even so, posing unglorified glorified bodies is not the
most satisfying solution.
        During my research for MMP5, Scott Crawford, a friend of mine, author of a misthological book entitled
Perseverance Pays, taught a series of lessons on glory that challenged the notion that all believers will have glorified
bodies. A mutual friend of ours, James (Jim) Hollandsworth, had written a book, Christ Magnified, which likewise
limits glorified bodies to faithful believers and contends that unfaithful believers merely have redeemed bodies.
Scott and I had both assisted in proofreading Jim’s book, but I had not yet been fully persuaded that Jim’s position
was superior to mine, though during my misthological series I had acknowledged that Jim’s misthological position
was an attractive alternative to my own.
        As a result of Scott’s series, I conceded that the context favors the misthological view of glory in Col 3:4,
Phil 3:21, and 1Jn 3:2, which left 1Cor 15:43 as the solo passage for a baseline soteriological view of glory. Was
this verse really singing a solo that countered the biblical chorus of practically every other verse on the subject? A
more careful examination of the Corinthian context was certainly in order. Was Paul affirming a soteriological level
of radiant glory for those believers who lived unfaithfully in this context? If so, he would be writing contrariwise
to the broader biblical context and counter intuitively to his own broader context of the Corinthian epistle. Could
something in his immediate context resolve the tension? Yes. Adopting the Majority Text (MT) for 1Cor 15:49
would strongly tilt the evidence in favor of the point that Jim and Scott had made: All believers will have redeemed
bodies; only faithful believers will have glorified bodies.2 The question now needed to be explored, Is the MT
the most plausible reading of 1Cor 15:49? Win this battle, and you win the hermeneutical war.3
                                      Broader Context from 1Cor 15:50,58
       Naturally, we want to use clear verses to help us understand unclear verses. Before examining 1Cor 15:49,
which is a highly disputed verse, let us note the clearer verses that follow it: “Now I say this, brethren, that flesh
and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God; nor does the perishable inherit the imperishable” (1Cor 15:50).
Accordingly, “this perishable must put on the imperishable, and this mortal must put on immortality” (1Cor 15:53).
“The dead will be raised incorruptible, and we shall be changed” (1Cor 15:52; NKJV). “We shall not all sleep, but
we shall all be changed” (1Cor 15:51). All resurrected believers are potentially qualified by physical transformation
to inherit the kingdom and thus rule since they will have sinless imperishable/immortal bodies. However, as Paul

1
  Soteriology is based on two biblical Greek words, soteria (salvation) and logos (teaching) and correspondingly
refers to the biblical teaching about salvation. Although the biblical concept of salvation includes many dimensions
other than deliverance from Hell, it is this aspect of salvation to which writers frequently refer when they use the
word soteriology. Thus, salvation from Hell (or, more specifically, from eternal damnation in the Lake of Fire) will
be referred to as soteriological salvation. In contrast, misthology is a coined term derived from the Greek word
misthos (reward) and logos (teaching) and refers to the doctrine of temporal and eternal rewards but is used
primarily in reference to eternal rewards. Misthological themes pertain to eternal rewards.
2
  Cyan will be used, as a general rule, in the present chapter to highlight my misthological summational remarks.
Yellow will be used in the present chapter for emphasis to highlight the quotes of others.
3
  For an online video that summarizes the material in the present chapter, see https://youtu.be/YVPftMGwljk.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                        Page 2
pointed out earlier in this epistle, this transformation into immortal bodies is not sufficient to inherit the kingdom.
A moral transformation is also necessary:
           •   8
                 On the contrary, you yourselves wrong and defraud, and that your brethren. 9 Or do you not
               know that the unrighteous shall not inherit the kingdom of God? Do not be deceived; neither
               fornicators, nor idolaters, nor adulterers, nor effeminate, nor homosexuals, 10 nor thieves, nor
               the covetous, nor drunkards, nor revilers, nor swindlers, shall inherit the kingdom of God.
               (1Cor 6:8-10)
This assessment is consistent with Paul’s warnings to believers elsewhere in his epistles:
           •   9
                 Now the deeds of the flesh are evident, which are: immorality, impurity, sensuality,
               20
                 idolatry, sorcery, enmities, strife, jealousy, outbursts of anger, disputes, dissensions,
               factions, 21 envying, drunkenness, carousing, and things like these, of which I forewarn you
               just as I have forewarned you that those who practice such things shall not inherit the kingdom
               of God. (Gal 5:19-21)
           •   3
                 But do not let immorality or any impurity or greed even be named among you, as is proper
               among saints; 4 and there must be no filthiness and silly talk, or coarse jesting, which are not
               fitting, but rather giving of thanks. 5 For this you know with certainty, that no immoral or
               impure person or covetous man, who is an idolater, has an inheritance in the kingdom of Christ
               and God. (Eph 5:3-5)
         From the broader context of the Corinthian epistle, as well as from the broader Pauline corpus, we must
deduce that those believers who fail to demonstrate a moral transformation here and now on Earth will be excluded
from the inheritance of the heavenly kingdom. Paul does not say in 1Cor 15:50-53 that all believers will inherit the
kingdom. Within the body of the epistle, he already has ruled out the possibility of all believers inheriting the
kingdom since he warned that immoral believers will not inherit the kingdom. According to Paul, all believers
will be eschatologically changed (i.e., transformed) and have immortal bodies, but only faithful believers who
have undergone a moral transformation during their lives on Earth will inherit the heavenly kingdom.
         The implication of Paul’s epistle is that believers must (A) undergo an eschatological transformation (per
the immediate context of 1Cor 15:50) and (B) have undergone a moral transformation (per the broader context of
1Cor 6:8-10) to (C) inherit the kingdom (A & B  C). That both A and B are equally necessary to inherit the
kingdom is easily demonstrated from Paul’s double negations. Restated in chronological, logical, and contextual
order, the syllogism would be:

    1. If you are immoral, you cannot inherit the kingdom (1Cor 6:8-10).
    2. If you are mortal, you cannot inherit the kingdom (1Cor 15:50-57).
    3. Therefore, you must be immortal and moral to inherit the kingdom.

Inheriting the kingdom is conditioned upon being both moral and immortal. If you are moral and immortal,
then you will inherit the kingdom. If, despite your regeneration, you are missing either requirement, then you will
not inherit the kingdom. The heavenly inheritance is conditioned on earthly performance. Hence, it makes sense for
Paul to conclude this section in 1Cor 15:50-57 with a moral imperative: “Therefore, my beloved brethren, be
steadfast, immovable, always abounding in the work of the Lord, knowing that your toil is not in vain in the Lord”
(1Cor 15:58). The imperative command to be steadfast and immovable would be incompatible with an
unconditional promise. Paul would not exhort us to work if the context is not provisionally promising us a benefit
conditioned on that work. Inheriting the kingdom would be one such benefit. Is it the only one?
                                         Moral Imperative of 1Cor 15:49
Textual Evidence
       What is not apparent to the English reader is that the majority of the Greek manuscripts introduced this
passage regarding inheriting the kingdom with a moral imperative. This imperative is hidden by most English
translations since they follow the CT (Critical Text) or TR (Textus Receptus), using a future indicative, and thus
translate the verse as what would seem to be an unconditional promise: “And just as we have borne the image of
the earthy, we shall also bear the image of the heavenly” (1Cor 15:49; NAS). This type of translation can give the
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                   Page 3
impression that Paul is saying that we will bear the heavenly image regardless of our earthly performance. Even so,
this understanding of the passage raises the question, Why would Paul say that our work is not in vain (in
1Cor 15:58) if our bearing the heavenly image is not impacted by our work (in 1Cor 15:49)? Is not our inheriting
the heavenly kingdom (in 1Cor 15:50) conditioned on our bearing the heavenly image (in 1Cor 15:49)? Paul
certainly seems to be making that very point by embedding the statement about inheriting the kingdom in 1Cor
15:50 in the middle of a context demonstrating the necessity of the heavenly image (1Cor 15:40-57).
       Two English translations are commendable exceptions and follow the MT: “Therefore as we have borne the
image of the earthly, let us bear also the image of the heavenly” (DRA). “And just as we have borne the image of
the man of dust, let us also bear the image of the man of heaven” (NET). This first-person plural subjunctive is the
reading of the MT. This hortatory exhortation provides a moral imperative and misthological condition for bearing
the image of the shining heavenly bodies (1Cor 15:41) and for inheriting the heavenly kingdom (1Cor 15:50).
Remarkably, this superior textual reading is rejected by most translators and commentators because of their
mistaken contextual prejudices. Lenski (TIO) is a prejudicial case in point:

      The textual evidence is preponderantly in favor of the subjunctive as a glance at the codices shows.
      But this is a case where the textual evidence, which is otherwise so decisive, cannot be accepted. It
      would be exceedingly strange that a paragraph which is so entirely didactic as this one is should reach
      its close with a final contrast that begins again in simple didactic fashion and then suddenly continues
      with hortation. It would be strange, indeed, that by means of the aorist Paul should place us at the
      resurrection moment at the last great day and then with a hortative subjunctive should force us back to
      the present moment in which the Corinthians and Paul are living as he writes these words.
Contextual Objection
       Lenski acknowledges that the textual evidence is decisively in favor of the subjunctive exhortation, but he
rejects it in favor of the future didactic because he does not see how the present force of the exhortation fits the
contextual focus on the future resurrection. His objection backfires if the present exhortation is misthologically
linked contextually with the future resurrection. For, in that case, we would expect a present moral exhortation that
makes it possible for us to participate misthologically in that immortal resurrection. In other words, (1) if the didactic
material suggests that Paul is noting differences in the resurrected state that are associated with varying degrees of
rewards and (2) if in addition to the textually probable subjunctive hortation calling for present moral conformity
there is an indicative exhortation calling for moral stability in our work for the Lord for the sake of future reward,
then Lenski’s argument implodes. A subjective would be the natural reading; an indicative would be the strange
reading. The conditions just described, as imploding Lenski’s argument, are certainly present in the context. Indeed,
several verses in the context necessitate perceiving a misthological aspiration (1Cor 15:41,50,58; cp. 15:32-34).
Contextual Support
       Not even explicitly factoring in misthological considerations, Fee (NICNT, 2014), contrary to commentators
like Lenski, presents a compelling case for the hortatory understanding:

             But in this case the question remains as to whether their sharing of the “heavenly” existence has
      not already begun, that is, whether their corresponding to the man of heaven refers only to the
      resurrection body that they are to assume at the Eschaton, or whether Paul is here intending also a
      broader sense, including behavioral implications, involved in their sharing in his likeness now. Under
      ordinary circumstances, one would see the latter as a secondary idea at best, brought in circuitously by
      way of the earlier passage (vv. 21–22) through the later discussion in his letter to the believers in Rome
      (Rom. 5). But in this case the question arises on the basis of the final verb in the final sentence. If Paul
      had written the future, “we will bear,” the case would be closed; the whole discussion would then
      involve the heavenly body and have to do only with the Eschaton. One can well understand, therefore,
      why the majority of those who have written on this passage opt for this reading. Nonetheless, the future
      is found in only a few disparate MSS and is easily accounted for on the very same grounds that it is
      now adopted by so many, while it is nearly impossible to account for anyone’s having changed a
      clearly understandable future to the hortatory subjunctive so early and so often that it made its way
      into every textual history as the predominant reading. At this point good textual criticism must prevail
      (the reading that best accounts for all the others is most likely the original); thus the admonition “let
      us” must be the original (since it is nearly inconceivable that a scribe would have created this), and
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                         Page 4
      if original, it must be intentional on Paul’s part as a way of calling them to prepare now for the
      future that is to be.
             If so, then the exhortation is not that the Corinthians try to assume their “heavenly body”
      now; both the earlier argument (vv. 23–28) and that to come (vv. 50–57) insist that such happens only
      at the Eschaton. Rather, they are being urged to conform to the life of the “man of heaven” as those
      who now share his character and behavior. As before (vv. 33–34) and later (v. 58), the concerns over
      their denial of the resurrection with its heavenly body and over their unchristian behavior merge. The
      implication is that not only are they not fully pneumatikos [spiritual] now, but they will not be
      fully pneumatikos [spiritual] at all if they do not presently also “bear the likeness of the man of
      heaven.” Thus we have another expression of Paul’s “already/not yet” eschatological framework.
      They are being urged to become what they are by grace; but as the rest of the argument makes plain,
      what they presently are by grace will not be fully realized until this mortal puts on immortality and the
      final defeat of death is realized. (Italics his.)

       Fee does a Judo flip on Lenski’s line of reasoning. According to Fee, a scribe following Lenski’s line of
reasoning would be motivated to change the subjunctive to an indicative to provide what the scribe thinks is the
clearer reading. Changing to an indicative is easier to explain than changing to a subjunctive. Scholars opposing
Fee offer counter arguments, of course. Such textual points aside, Fee’s contextual arguments are superior, as will
be demonstrated herein. Fee’s contextual line of reasoning leads to a misthological assessment. According to Fee,
Paul is calling upon the Corinthian believers to prepare presently for their future with the implication that they will
only realize this heavenly image in the future if they presently manifest it in the present. Being fully spiritual
in the future is conditioned on being morally victorious in the present.4 In other words, this misthological
realization is conditioned on moral participation. A fully spiritual transformation in the future (that entails a
glorified body that is suitable for inheriting the glorious kingdom because it has conquered death) requires moral
preparation. Prudently, the NET follows Fee:

             If the original reading is the future tense, then “we will bear” would be a guarantee that
      believers would be like Jesus (and unlike Adam) in the resurrection. If the aorist subjunctive is
      original, then “let us bear” would be a command to show forth the image of Jesus, i.e., to live as
      citizens of the kingdom that believers will one day inherit. The future indicative is not widespread
      geographically. At the same time, it fits the context well: Not only are there indicatives in this section
      (especially vv. 42–49), but the conjunction καί (kai) introducing the comparative καθώς (kathōs) seems
      best to connect to the preceding by furthering the same argument (what is, not what ought to be). For
      this reason, though, the future indicative could be a reading thus motivated by an early scribe. In light
      of the extremely weighty evidence for the aorist subjunctive, it is probably best to regard the aorist
      subjunctive as original. This connects well with v. 50, for there Paul makes a pronouncement that
      seems to presuppose some sort of exhortation. G. D. Fee (First Corinthians [NICNT], 795) argues for
      the originality of the subjunctive, stating that “it is nearly impossible to account for anyone’s having
      changed a clearly understandable future to the hortatory subjunctive so early and so often that it made
      its way into every textual history as the predominant reading.” The subjunctive makes a great deal of
      sense in view of the occasion of 1 Corinthians. Paul wrote to combat an over-realized eschatology in
      which some of the Corinthians evidently believed they were experiencing all the benefits of the
      resurrection body in the present, and thus that their behavior did not matter. If the subjunctive is the
      correct reading, it seems Paul makes two points: (1) that the resurrection is a bodily one, as
      distinct from an out-of-body experience, and (2) that one’s behavior in the interim does make a
      difference (see 15:32–34, 58). (Emphasis original.)5


4
  Fee is not clear as to the meaning of being fully spiritual now versus not being fully spiritual at all in the future.
This tension can be easily resolved by the thesis of this book. Paul means that to have fully spiritual bodies at the
resurrection, believers must have bodies that bear the image of Christ by having glorified bodies. Those believers
who fail to have glorified bodies and thus only have spiritual bodies will not be fully spiritual. They will not have
glorified bodies at all and thus have bodies that fall short of the full eschatological realization that Paul envisions.
5
  The NET Bible First Edition Notes (1 Co 15:46–49) (Biblical Studies Press, 2006).
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                Page 5
        What difference does it make if you live morally or shamefully? NET points out that Paul’s injunction in
1Cor 15:32-34 not to behave shamefully makes no sense if he proceeds to say that our behavior makes no difference
at the resurrection. NET also notes that Paul’s injunction in 1Cor 15:58 to work indicates that behavior makes a
difference pertaining to his preceding discussion, which is why Paul starts off v. 58 with the word: therefore. What
is the therefore there for? The answer is self-evident: The therefore calls attention to the fact that our behavior has
an impact on what happens at the resurrection. Our deduction should be, accordingly, that the context is leading
the Corinthians to perceive that the behavior of believers determines if they will have a glorified body that is
suitable for inheriting the kingdom. Comfort (NTTTC) also follows Fee in preference for the hortatory
subjunctive:

      The textual evidence for the variant [subjunctive] reading is far more extensive and earlier than
      for the WH NU [indicative] reading. Thus, it is likely that a few scribes changed the hortatory (“let us
      bear”) to the future (“we will bear”) to make for easier reading or to conform the verb tense to the
      prevailing future, as evidenced in 15:51–54. Therefore, Fee (1987, 787) argues that the second reading
      “must be the original, and if original it must be intentional on Paul’s part as a way of calling them [the
      Corinthians] to prepare now for the future that is to be.”

       Comfort sides with Fee on the textual analysis. Even more importantly, he concurs that Paul intends for the
Corinthian believers to prepare now for the future that is to be only fully realized, per Fee, if they do in fact
prepare now. In accord with various contemporary commentators, Fee should be commended for defending the
widely attested subjunctive against those commentators who adopt the poorly attested indicative. This debate is not
new. Thiselton (NIGTC) notes: “The debate began in the early centuries. Tertullian argues against Marcion: He
[Tertullian] says, ‘let us wear [or bear]’ as a precept; not ‘we shall wear [or bear] in the sense of promise.’
Chrysostom, Cyprian, and Basil appear to read the subjunctive.” Marcion, who preferred the indicative, and against
whom Tertullian was arguing, was a heretic. As to modern writers, Thiselton acknowledges that “Heinrici, Allo,
Sider, Collins, and Wolff stand with Fee” in understanding Paul issuing a subjunctive precept rather than an
unconditional indicative promise. Commentators are divided. Hermeneutical battle lines are drawn.
Subjective Necessity
       Commentators on the other side of the debate object. For example, Or (AYBC) fires a counter salvo: “The
likeness of the heavenly one is hardly to be achieved by exhortation.” An objection must be made to his objection.
If misthological glory is entailed, it must be achieved by moral exhortation. Such likeness to Christ in glory can
only be achieved by obeying the moral exhortation conveyed by the subjunctive. Although Nash (SHBC)
acknowledges the strong textual and patristic evidence supporting the subjunctive, he waffles in which reading he
prefers:

      Most modern versions read, “we will also bear the image of the man of heaven.” This fits with Paul’s
      contrast between the earthly body now and the resurrected body then. But nearly all the ancient
      manuscripts actually read, “let us bear the image of the man of heaven.” This does not seem to fit
      with Paul’s main point so well. If it is the correct reading, as is likely, then perhaps Paul was
      saying that though we have worn the image of Adam, we should now look to Christ and strive to
      conform our lives to his. Though we do not yet possess that resurrected body that shall fully
      reflect the image of Christ, we should live in such a way that Christ is reflected even in these
      earthly bodies. Both “we will wear” and “let us wear” stress that becoming like Christ is the glory of
      the resurrection. The exhortation “let us,” however, adds to the basic message of future hope an
      appropriate note of responsibility in the present. The full experience of that glory must await our
      own resurrection, but to the degree that we can reflect the image of Christ now, we already wear
      the likeness of resurrection existence. (Italics his.)

      Nash acknowledges that the subjunctive has the strongest textual support and “is likely” the correct reading.
Nevertheless, he thinks that it does not fit the context as well as the indicative. On the contrary, per Nash’s own
analysis, Paul’s analogy in 1Cor 15:41 practically demands the subjunctive. Degrees of stellar glory befit the
subjunctive. If Paul were using an indicative, promising that we will all fully bear the image of Christ
regardless of our moral conformity now, then we would assume equality, not variations among ourselves in
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                       Page 6
degrees of celestial glory. Contextually, the fact that Paul calls attention to varying degrees of stellar glory
should lead us to expect a subjunctive. A subjunctive fits naturally with inequality, an indicative would not.
Nash’s analysis is inferior to Fee’s and fails to deal with the full implications of Paul’s argument. Nash talks as if
fully bearing the image now is the only thing at stake. Through sophistry, he speaks as if bearing it fully in the
future is assured. His interpretation would have us believe that Paul is speaking nonsense: Bear the image now fully
because you will bear the image in the future fully even if you refuse to bear it fully now. To the contrary, Paul is
not saying that we will bear the image fully in the future regardless of whether we bear it now. Paul is not merely
talking about “the degree we can reflect the image of Christ now”! He makes a correlation between the degree we
will manifest it now with the degree we will radiate it in the resurrection.
Chiastic Support
       Constable (TCENB) does not waffle in his support of Fee on both the subjunctive and degrees. On
1Cor 15:49, he provides a lengthy quote from Fee that concludes by saying, “in Christ’s resurrection and their being
‘in him’ they have also begun to bear the likeness of the man of heaven. The urgency is that they truly do so now
as they await the consummation when they shall do so fully.” Fully does not happen automatically. The urgency is
due to the subjunctive. Fee was not clear as to what being fully spiritual now versus not being fully spiritual at all
in the future means. Thus, in footnote 4, I supplied my own clarification. But Fee was clear that you must bear
Christ’s image now morally if you wish to bear it fully eschatologically. For 1Cor 15:41, Constable provides a
confirmative analysis:

      The fact that there are different kinds of bodies among animals should help us understand that there
      can also be different kinds of human bodies. Some human bodies are mortal and some are immortal.
      Some are corruptible and others incorruptible.
             Likewise the fact that celestial bodies differ in glory (brightness) should help us realize
      that human bodies can also differ in glory. The glory of a perishable mortal human body is much
      less than that of an imperishable immortal human body. Also the differing glory of the heavenly
      bodies argues for differences among glorified believers.

       Commentators frequently note the chiastic structure of Paul’s discussion. As Constable illustrates, Paul uses
a chiasm to delineate the difference in terrestrial glory versus celestial glory:

        A Not all flesh is the same (i.e., earthly bodies).
           B Examples of different kinds of flesh: people, animals, birds, fish
               C There are heavenly and earthly kinds of bodies.
               C’ The splendor of heavenly bodies is of one kind and the splendor of earthly bodies is of
               another kind.
           B’ Examples of different kinds of splendor: sun, moon, stars
        A’ Not all stars (i.e., heavenly bodies) have the same splendor. (Italics his.)

Using the actual text and the implication supposed by those who reject Paul as indicating differing degrees of glory
would yield the following balanced chiasm:
             39
               All flesh is not the same flesh,
                      but there is one [earthly glory of] flesh of men,
                      and another [earthly glory of] flesh of beasts,
                      and another [earthly glory of] flesh of birds,
                      and another [earthly glory of] flesh of fish.
                               40 There are also heavenly bodies and earthly bodies,
                               but the glory of the heavenly is one, and the glory of the earthly is another.
                      41 There is one [heavenly] glory of the sun,
                      and another [heavenly] glory of the moon,
                      and another [heavenly] glory of the stars.
             41b
                Likewise, all heavenly bodies are not the same bodies.
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                 Page 7

Obviously, Paul is using the term flesh to refer to earthly bodies. So, we can make the appropriate substitution and
derive the matching ending. If Paul simply meant to conclude his chiasm without advancing the thought, he could
have concluded by saying, “Likewise, all heavenly bodies are not the same bodies.” Doing so would have paralleled
his opening phrase of the chiasm, “All flesh is not the same flesh.” If Paul only wanted to confirm the difference
between earthly bodies and heavenly bodies, the corresponding introduction and conclusion would be:

            All earthly bodies are not the same earthly bodies…
            All heavenly bodies are not the same heavenly bodies.

Paul does not use this corresponding conclusion. Yet many commentators erroneously interpret him as if he had.
Indeed, Paul should have made the corresponding statement if he meant what many commentators take him to mean.
Instead, he advances the thought in his chiasm, as Constable correctly notes, to show differences among glorified
believers. Nevertheless, Constable’s statement needs to be tweaked. Contrary to Constable, “the differing glory of
the heavenly bodies” does not argue “for differences among glorified believers.” These differences between those
three types of heavenly bodies (sun, moon, and stars) only show that the resurrected bodies of believers will have a
celestial glory as opposed to their previous terrestrial glory. Commentators who deny that a degree of glory is being
implied among resurrected believers are correct on this point. Such misthological implication cannot be derived
from that simple variation of the three types of heavenly bodies. Where their denial stumbles badly, even fatally,
nonetheless, is in failing to perceive that Paul has used an unbalanced chiasm.
         Just as an unbalanced butterfly can be used in stock trading to give the trader a directional bias, so Paul’s
unbalanced chiasm is not misthologically neutral. It shows the direction he wants to take this discussion. Paul does
not say that fleshly men vary in glory. Nor does he say that fleshly beasts vary in glory. Neither does he say that
fleshly birds vary in glory. He does not say that fleshly fish vary in glory. Although people, beasts, birds, and fish
do vary in physical glory, Paul completely ignores that fact. Notwithstanding, he calls attention to the fact that stars
vary in celestial glory. Why? Why single out the variation of the stars? This stellar variation of one class of the
heavenly bodies is not paralleled in his chiasm. He does not call attention to subvariations in subclasses of fleshly
bodies. However, in juxtaposition to the singular solar glory and singular lunar glory of the other two heavenly
bodies and in contradistinction to the unmentioned subvariation of fleshly bodies, Paul states the variation of the
stellar glory. By calling attention to this stellar variation, Paul advances his chiasm to a secondary implication,
namely, the one stated by Constable.
         Accordingly, Constable’s assessment needs to be restated with an adjustment that agrees with Paul’s
unbalanced chiasm: The differing glory of the heavenly stars argues for differences among glorified believers.
The variation among the stars is what argues for the differences in degrees of glory among those believers who
qualify for such stellar glory. Not only will they have the glory of having a resurrected body, per the primary strands
of Paul’s chiasm regarding the sun, moon, and stars, but, secondarily, they will also have varying degrees of glory
among themselves, per the concluding strand of his chiasm: star varies from star in glory. The gar in Paul’s
concluding statement is probably best rendered as indeed as done by the RSV in Paul’s unbalanced chiasm:
             39
               All flesh is not the same flesh,
                      but there is one [earthly glory of] flesh of men,
                      and another [earthly glory of] flesh of beasts,
                      and another [earthly glory of] flesh of birds,
                      and another [earthly glory of] of fish.
                               40 There are also heavenly bodies and earthly bodies,
                               but the glory of the heavenly is one, and the glory of the earthly is another.
                      41 There is one [heavenly] glory of the sun,
                      and another [heavenly] glory of the moon,
                      and another [heavenly] glory of the stars.
             41b
                Indeed, star varies from star in glory.

The glory of the stars represents the glory of resurrected believers who qualify for that glory. They will not qualify
equally. Hence, Paul uses this unbalanced conclusion and proceeds to use a subjunctive in 1Cor 15:49 and an
imperative in 1Cor 15:58 to match the misthological implication of his chiasm.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                       Page 8
Dual Subjective Implications
       Robertson and Plummer (ICC) acknowledge that the subjunctive is the best textual understanding: “If, with
the best editors, we follow the greatly preponderating external evidence and read φορέσωμεν rather than
φορέσομεν, ‘let us wear’ or ‘let us put on for wear’ rather than ‘we shall wear,’ the meaning will be that the
attaining to the glorified body depends upon our own effort: see Goudge.” Precisely. The point of contention
is whether attaining a glorified body depends on our effort. The subjunctive leads to the conclusion that not
all believers will attain a glorified body because they will not obey Paul’s subjunctive exhortation to live
morally. Present morality will greatly affect the state of our eschatological bodies. Robertson and Plummer defer
us to Goudge for this conclusion, so let’s see Goudge’s comment (WC):

        “Let us also bear,” is the more strongly supported by the MSS, In this case the meaning will be
        either (a)“Let us not throw away our prospect of bearing etc.,” with the same reference to the
        resurrection body; or (b) “Let us bear the image of the heavenly even here,” by conforming our
        lives to His. The former interpretation suits the context better, but the latter gives a thoroughly
        Pauline thought. Cf. 2 Cor. iii. 18; Col. iii. 10. The Christian by faith and obedience is
        transformed into the likeness of Christ in a true sense even here, and this prepares him for the
        future transformation of his body, Cf. Rom. viii. 23.

Goudge is torn between two very valid understandings of the subjunctive in this context. Actually, the subjunctive
most reasonably carries both meanings in this context: (A) We solidify our prospect of bearing the heavenly
image in glory via a body that shines like that of Christ (B) by morally bearing the image of the heavenly
now. Goudge anticipated such a misthological conclusion by conceding in 1Cor 15:41, “The thought may have
crossed the Apostle’s mind that the resurrection bodies of the redeemed will also differ in glory one from
another.” As deduced by various writers, surely Paul intends misthological distinctions in v. 41, where, in
comparing our resurrected heavenly bodies with celestial heavenly bodies, Paul states: “There is one glory of the
sun, and another glory of the moon, and another glory of the stars; for star differs from star in glory.” We will
discuss this verse more thoroughly shortly.6 For now, let us consider the primary background passage that was
probably in Paul’s mind.
OT Background
       As is widely acknowledged among commentators, Dan 12:2-3 is the backdrop for Paul’s discussion of the
glory of the stars as it applies to the resurrected body.

            •   2
                  And many of those who sleep in the dust of the ground will awake, these to everlasting life,
                but the others to disgrace and everlasting contempt. 3 And those who have insight will
                shine brightly like the brightness of the expanse of heaven, and those who lead the many
                to righteousness, like the stars forever and ever. (Dan 12:2-3)

Everlasting glory is juxtaposed with everlasting contempt at the resurrection. According to the OT background,
some resurrected believers will shine like stars; some believers will not. The distinction between those believers
who shine and those who do not shine will be everlasting because rewards are eternal (see RAE, 4DF, TOD). Given
such rudimentary premises from a number of such passages, Paul is best understood as expounding upon that basic
understanding in 1Cor 15:41 by indicating that, even among those believers who shine, a variation in radiance is to
be expected. Just as we see a difference in radiance among natural celestial bodies, we should anticipate a difference
in radiance among misthological celestial bodies. Not all rewarded believers shine with equal brightness. Indeed,
per the background of OT and the context of other passages, Paul is indicating more than that obvious degree of
misthological differentiation. He should also be understood as indicating that some resurrected believers shine while
others do not. Some believers will have glorified bodies, while other believers are disgraced with unglorified bodies.
Paul and Daniel concur. Daniel does not say that all resurrected believers will shine. Quite the opposite. Paul
likewise should not be misinterpreted as saying that all resurrected believers will shine. Daniel and Paul agree that
some resurrected believers shine like stars; some do not. Paul builds upon that basic misthological foundation with
the further acknowledgment that among those believers who shine like stars, they will not shine equally.

6
    See Misthological Glory in 1Cor 15:41, 13.
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                Page 9
Ethical Exhortation and Necessitation
      For purported contextual reasons, however, Morris (TNTC) seeks to counter the textual evidence and this
misthological conclusion with a soteriological presupposition:

      The majority of the more ancient MSS; read ‘let us bear’ instead of we shall bear in the second part of
      the verse. If this reading be accepted (as it is by Findlay, Williams and others), then Paul is
      exhorting the Corinthians to put on their heavenly state, progressively to make it their own (‘let
      us also try to be like the man from heaven’, Goodspeed). This sounds as though it were something
      people can do by their own efforts. But this is so far from Paul’s usual approach that we are justified
      in regarding ‘let us bear’ as a primitive corruption of the text by scribes interested in ethical
      exhortation, and not quite certain what the apostle meant. (Emphasis his.)

Contrary to Morris, an ethical exhortation is not a deviation from Paul’s usual approach. To be sure, it takes effort
to live morally. Yet, earlier in this very epistle, Paul calls for maximum effort in view of possible reward at the
resurrection:
      24
        Do you not know that those who run in a race all run, but only one receives the prize? Run in such
      a way that you may win. 25 And everyone who competes in the games exercises self-control in all
      things. They then do it to receive a perishable wreath, but we an imperishable. 26 Therefore I run in
      such a way, as not without aim; I box in such a way, as not beating the air; 27 but I buffet my body and
      make it my slave, lest possibly, after I have preached to others, I myself should be disqualified.
      (1Cor 9:24-27)

The uncertainty that Morris seeks to ascribe to the early scribes should be laid at his doorstep instead. Morris is the
one who is “not quite certain” as to what Paul means since Morris thinks that an ethical exhortation is contextually
out of the question. Perhaps Morris should have considered his own comments on 1Cor 15:58: “Arising out of all
this comes an exhortation to Christian stability.” Yes, indeed, an ethical exhortation reasonably arises out of the
misthological possibility of resplendent regality.
       Commendably, the comment by Jamieson, Fausset, and Brown (JFB) acknowledges the reasonableness of
the exhortation in 1Cor 15:49: “An exhortation, and yet implying a promise (so Ro 8:29). The conformity to the
image of the heavenly Representative man is to be begun here in our souls, in part, and shall be perfected at
the resurrection in both bodies and souls.” Yes, the basic point is that present moral conformity to the image of
Christ results in perfected misthological conformity at the resurrection. They cite Rom 8:29 as a cross-reference. A
corresponding misthological understanding of Rom 8:29 is plausible, perhaps even probable. God provisionally
“predestined to become conformed to the image of His Son” temporally, morally, and misthologically.
       Robertson (WPNT), citing Ellicott, seeks to counter, “Paul hardly means to say that our attaining the
resurrection body depends on our own efforts!” In doing so, Robertson misconstrues the entire point. Paul is not
talking about merely having a resurrected body but about having a resurrected body (1) that can inherit the
kingdom and (2) that shines like the stars, both of which are reward concepts. Paul is not enjoining us to earn
a resurrected body but a glorified resurrected body. Nicoll (EGTC) properly deduces the misthological ramifications
of 1Cor 15:49:

      Is this a purely physical distinction, between pre- and post-resurrection states of the same men (cf. 44)?
      or is there a moral connotation implied, as Hf. and Ed. suggest? The latter seems likely, esp. on
      comparison of Phil. 3:18 ff., Col. 3:1–4, Rom. 6:4, and in transition to the exhortation of ver.
      49….Admitting the larger scope of ver. 48, we accept the strongly attested hortatory φορέσωμεν of
      ver. 49: “Let us wear also the image of the Heavenly One”….we are exhorted to “put on Christ”
      (Rom. 13:14, Gal. 3:27), realising [sic.] that to wear His moral likeness here carries with it the
      wearing of His bodily likeness hereafter: see vv. 20–23, Rom. 8:11; 1 John 3:2 f. (Italics his.)

      Nicoll is correct. A moral connotation with an eschatological implication is implied. Wearing Christ’s
bodily likeness in the hereafter, in a glorious manner that shines like the stars and qualifies one for inheriting
the kingdom, is conditioned on wearing His moral likeness now. Such an assessment is misthologically and
contextually prudent and correctly perceived by various commentators. Various older writers seem to have been
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                         Page 10
just as perceptive or even more so than some of our newer writers on this point. Chrysostom (NPNF), for example,
as has been noted, prefers the hortatory subjunctive in 1Cor 15:49:

        Paul’s meaning is such as this: “as we have borne the image of the earthy,” i e., evil deeds, “let us also
        bear the image of the heavenly,” the manner of life which is in the heavens. Whereas if he were
        speaking of nature, the thing needed not exhortation nor advice. So that hence also it is evident that
        the expression relates to our manner of life.

Ellicott (ECER) concurs:

        Better, let us bear also the image of the heavenly. Such is the reading of the best MSS. The words
        transport the thoughts of the reader to the future glory, and, at the same moment, show a light on
        present duty. The resurrection life is to be begun in us even now. “If by any means we can attain to
        the [out] resurrection of the dead” (2Corinthians 3:18; Philippians 3:21 [sic.]).

The scriptural quotation ECER provides is actually from Phil 3:11, where Paul seeks moral conformity to Christ’s
death so that he might achieve misthological immortal conformity in the out resurrection (a well-known
misthological concept which I discuss at length elsewhere). All believers will stand in resurrected bodies. But only
faithful believers will be outstanding at the resurrection in glorified bodies. Yet again, Paul conditions
eschatological misthological conformity on present moral conformity.
       Even those writers who fail to opt for the subjective interpret the passage frequently in a manner that requires
it. Gray, for instance, writes:

        A perfect moral resemblance to Christ will be attained at the last day.
               The nobility of heaven.—When Cineas, the ambassador of Pyrrhus, after his return from Rome,
        was asked by his master, “What he thought of the city and state,” he answered, “that it seemed to him
        to be a state of none but great statesmen, and a commonwealth of kings.” Such is heaven—no other
        than a parliament of emperors, a commonwealth of kings: every humble faithful soul in that
        kingdom is co-heir with Christ, hath a robe of honour, and a sceptre of power, and a throne of
        majesty, and a crown of glory.7 (Italics his.)

Gray affirms the truth of this passage as it pertains to faithful believers. They will have a perfect resemblance to
Christ and a crown of glory. What about every unhumble, unfaithful soul in that kingdom, though? Will the
unfaithful also attain a perfect moral resemblance to Christ at the last day and be co-heirs with Him? Of course, not!
Thus, this commentator’s comments are not fitting for an indicative but better suited for a subjunctive.
       Let us return to Tertullian, a very early writer who, with much better consistency on the passage than many
contemporary writers, states:

        We come now to the very gist of the whole question: What are the substances, and of what nature
        are they, which the apostle has disinherited of the kingdom of God? The preceding statements give
        us a clue to this point also. He says: “The first man is of the earth, earthy“—that is, made of dust, that
        is, Adam; “the second man is from heaven”—that is, the Word of God, which is Christ, in no other
        way, however, man (although “from heaven”), than as being Himself flesh and soul, just as a human
        being is, just as Adam was. Indeed, in a previous passage He is called “the second Adam,” deriving
        the identity of His name from His participation in the substance, because not even Adam was flesh of
        human seed, in which Christ is also like Him. “As is the earthy, such are they also that are earthy; and
        as is the heavenly, such are they also that are heavenly.” In like manner, those also who after Him
        are heavenly, are understood to have this celestial quality predicated of them not from their
        present nature, but from their future glory; because in a preceding sentence, which originated
        this distinction respecting difference of dignity, there was shown to be “one glory in celestial
        bodies, and another in terrestrial ones,”—“one glory of the sun, and another glory of the moon,
        and another glory of the stars: for even one star differeth from another star in glory,” although
7
    J. C. Gray, Biblical Encyclopedia and Museum, vol. 14 (Hartford, CT: The S. S. Scranton Co.,1900), 101.
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                 Page 11
      not in substance. Then, after having thus premised the difference in that worth or dignity which
      is even now to be aimed at, and then at last to be enjoyed, the apostle adds an exhortation, that
      we should both here in our training follow the example of Christ, and there attain His eminence
      in glory: “As we have borne the image of the earthy, let us also bear the image of the heavenly.”
      We have indeed borne the image of the earthy, by our sharing in his transgression, by our participation
      in his death, by our banishment from Paradise. Now, although the image of Adam is here borne by is
      in the flesh, yet we are not exhorted to put off the flesh; but if not the flesh, it is the conversation, in
      order that we may then bear the image of the heavenly in ourselves,—no longer indeed the image
      of God, and no longer the image of a Being whose state is in heaven; but after the lineaments of Christ,
      by our walking here in holiness, righteousness, and truth. And so wholly intent on the inculcation
      of moral conduct is he throughout this passage, that he tells us we ought to bear the image of
      Christ in this flesh of ours, and in this period of instruction and discipline. For when he says “let
      us bear” in the imperative mood, he suits his words to the present life, in which man exists in no
      other substance than as flesh and soul; or if it is another, even the heavenly, substance to which this
      faith (of ours) looks forward, yet the promise is made to that substance to which the injunction is
      given to labour earnestly to merit its reward. Since, therefore, he makes the image both of the
      earthy and the heavenly consist of moral conduct—the one to be abjured, and the other to be
      pursued—and then consistently adds, “For this I say” (on account, that is, of what I have already
      said, because the conjunction “for” connects what follows with the preceding words) “that flesh
      and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God,”—he means the flesh and blood to be understood in
      no other sense than the before-mentioned “image of the earthy;” and since this is reckoned to consist
      in “the old conversation,” which old conversation receives not the kingdom of God, therefore flesh
      and blood, by not receiving the kingdom of God, are reduced to the life of the old conversation. Of
      course, as the apostle has never put the substance for the works of man, he cannot use such a
      construction here. Since, however he has declared of men which are yet alive in the flesh, that they
      “are not in the flesh,” meaning that they are not living in the works of the flesh, you ought not to
      subvert its form nor its substance, but only the works done in the substance (of the flesh), alienating
      us from the kingdom of God. It is after displaying to the Galatians these pernicious works that
      he professes to warn them beforehand, even as he had “told them in time past, that they which
      do such things should not inherit the kingdom of God,” even because they bore not the image of
      the heavenly, as they had borne the image of the earthy; and so, in consequence of their old
      conversation, they were to be regarded as nothing else than flesh and blood.8 (Italics his.)

      How will there be many mansions in our Father’s house, if not to accord with a diversity of
      deserts? How will one star also differ from another star in glory, unless in virtue of disparity in
      their rays?9

Tertullian argues that those believers who live immorally, earthly, terrestrial lives are disinherited from the
kingdom of God. Disinherited believers will not attain the inheritance of the kingdom nor attain the future
heavenly glory associated with it because heavenly celestial glory is conditioned on heavenly moral conduct.
We must bear the image of Christ in the present if we are to bear His image in the future. If we fail to bear
His image in the present, we will not bear it in the future, thereby missing out on heavenly stellar glory and
thus not have glorified bodies. We must labor earnestly to merit this reward. Earthly immoral conduct
disinherits believers, whereas heavenly moral conduct qualifies believers for the heavenly inheritance.
Excellent! Although Tertullian has overstated his argument on a couple of points,10 nevertheless, his overall

582–583. Also see Tertullian, On the Resurrection of the Flesh, 49. Available at
https://www.newadvent.org/fathers/0316.htm. Accessed 12/16/2020.

   Tertullian probably overstates his case on two particulars. First, the term “flesh and blood” in 1Cor 15:50 probably
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                         Page 12
argument is superior to those of his critics on the thrust of the passage. In consistency with Paul’s clear misthological
understanding regarding inheriting the kingdom in other passages, Tertullian insists that Paul is saying that
1Cor 15:49 is giving a moral imperative that we live in moral conformity with kingdom objectives so that we
may merit the kingdom reward. This reward, we can deduce, is not only to inherit the kingdom but to have
glorified bodies that are fitting for inheriting this glorious kingdom, a body that is not only immortal and
imperishable but which shines with glory. Accordingly, while all resurrected believers will have redeemed bodies
that are immortal and imperishable, only misthologically glorified believers will have glorious bodies that shine
with radiance befitting inheriting the kingdom. Tertullian is consistent in his interpretation. Having perceived a
misthological understanding of 1Cor 15:41 dealing with degrees of glory, Tertullian advocates taking
1Cor 15:49 as an exhortation to achieve that degree of glory as one’s reward.
       Like Tertullian, Augustine associates 1Cor 15:49 with a reward conditioned on our present moral conformity
to the heavenly image:

      And at another place in the end of the epistle ‘As we have borne,’ he says, ‘the image of the earthly,
      we shall also bear the image of the heavenly.’ Virginity bears this image, integrity bears it, holiness
      and truth bear it; they bear it who are mindful of the chastening of the Lord, who observe justice and
      piety, who are strong in faith, humble in fear, steadfast in the endurance of suffering, meek in the
      endurance of injury, ready to pity, of one mind and of one heart in brotherly peace. And every one of
      these things ought ye, holy virgins, to observe, to cherish, and fulfill, who having hearts at leisure for
      God and for Christ, and having chosen the greater and better part, lead and point the way to the Lord,
      to whom you have pledged your vows. Ye who are advanced in age, exercise control over the younger.
      Ye who are younger, wait upon the elders, and encourage your equals; stir up one another by mutual
      exhortations; provoke one another to glory by emulous examples of virtue; endure bravely, advance
      in spirituality, finish your course with joy; only be mindful of us when your virginity shall begin to
      reap its reward of honor.”11

Augustine exhorts us to exhort one another to live morally so that we will reap honor as our reward.
Surprisingly, he quotes Paul as using the indicative: “shall also bear.” Adopting the subjunctive—“let us also
bear”—would be the more fitting selection for the point Augustine is making. After all, Augustine acknowledges
that bearing the image is not unconditionally assured since it is a reward conditioned on our morality. Yet,
although Augustine advocates this understanding of the context, he adopts the indicative translation. By making the
eschatological outcome conditioned on present morality, Augustine comes to the same conclusion that would be
derived from the subjunctive. Logical and contextual consistency suffices to lead Augustine to the proper conclusion
even though he assumes the indicative translation is implemented. His doing so illustrates that even those adopting
the indicative translation are without excuse if they fail to perceive a misthological implication of the passage. Even
if we accept the indicative translation, “we will bear the image of the heavenly [eschatologically],” the context and
its logical implications require its realization be conditioned on our bearing the image morally. Complete victory
over death and inheriting the kingdom are misthologically contingent results. Even if you adopt the indicative
text, context requires a subjunctive understanding and thus misthological implications. We learn this from
Augustine’s example. In fact, elsewhere Augustine quotes 1Cor 15:49 with the subjective understanding and
explains it as if it were an indicative: “Paul says elsewhere: ‘As we have borne the image of the earthly, let us also
bear the image of Him who is from heaven” (1 Cor 15:49). This means: Let us who were mortal according to Adam
believe with true faith and sure and steadfast hope that we shall be immortal according to Christ.”12 Augustine
equates the subjunctive, “let us also bear,” with the indicative, “we shall also be,” here in conjunction with the quote
above because, contextually, to have the latter you must have the former. The indicative is dependent upon the
subjunctive for its fulfillment.


refers simply to the physical composition of our earthly bodies, not to our moral disposition. Second, v. 50 begins
with “but” or “now” (de) not “for” (gar). Nevertheless, his overall argument remains valid.

Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                   Page 13
                                         Misthological Glory in 1Cor 15:41
Patristic Confirmation of Merited Degrees of Glory
       Augustine’s misthological understanding of 1Cor 15:49 befits his misthological understanding of 1Cor 15:41,
where he affirms that not all those who have eternal life and are saved will obtain the glory associated with the
brightest stars in 1Cor 15:41: “For the obtaining that glory, which shall not be of all, although they abide in eternal
life and salvation: “For star differeth from star in brightness; so also the Resurrection of the dead.” It is,
therefore, “good for a man so to be.”13 “Yet ‘star differeth from star in glory; so also the resurrection of the
dead.’ These are the different merits of the Saints.”14 Glory is a reward based on merit. Not all believers will
merit a glorified body.
       Although the patristic differentiation between soteriological and misthological dimensions is somewhat
blurred at times, nevertheless their rudimentary attempts to make such distinctions and to affirm both dimensions
can be appreciated. Chrysostom, for instance, associates the varying degrees of glory among the stars in 1Cor 15:41
with varying degrees of glory among believers:

      And these things are not merely suggested by our own reasoning, but declared also by the divine
      oracles; for He Himself saith, “He shall reward every man according to his works.” And not only in
      hell, but also in the kingdom one will find many differences; for He saith “in my Fathers house are
      many mansions;”2 and, “there is one glory of the sun, and another glory of the moon.” And what
      wonder, if in dealing with such great matters he has spoken with such precision, seeing that He
      declares there is a difference in that world even between one star and another? Knowing then
      all these things let us never desist from doing good deeds, nor grow weary, nor, if we should be
      unable to reach the rank of the sun or of the moon, let us despise that of the stars. For if only we
      display thus much virtue at least, we shall be able to have a place in Heaven.15

Chrysostom seems to make having a place in Heaven contingent on our performance. If this is his intent,
then his statement should be amended by saying that only if we display at least the glory of the stars will we
have a place in the Heavenly City. All believers will be in Heaven. Those who fail to attain to the glory of
the dimmest star will be in the celestial outer darkness, still in Heaven, though (see MMP4 and MMP5).
Chrysostom expounds further:

              Ver. 41. “There is one glory of the sun, and another glory of the moon, and another glory of the
      stars: for one star differeth from another star in glory.”
              And what means he by these expressions? Wherefore from the resurrection of the body did he
      throw himself into the discourse of the stars and the sun? He did not throw himself out, neither did he
      break off from his purpose; far from it: but he still keeps to it. For whereas he had established the
      doctrine concerning the resurrection, he intimates in what follows that great will be then the
      difference of glory, though there be but one resurrection. And for the present he divides the whole
      into two: into “bodies celestial,” and “bodies terrestrial.” For that the bodies are raised again, he
      signified by the corn: but that they are not all in the same glory, he signifies by this. For as the disbelief
      of the resurrection makes men supine, so again it makes them indolent to think that all are vouchsafed
      the same reward. Wherefore he corrects both. And the one in the preceeding [sic.] verses he had
      completed; but this he begins now. And having made two ranks, of the righteous and of sinners,
      these same two he subdivides again into many parts, signifying that neither righteous nor sinners
      shall obtain the same; neither righteous men, all of them, alike with other righteous, nor sinners
      with other sinners.
              Now he makes, you see, first, one separation between righteous and sinners, where he says,

      “bodies celestial, and bodies terrestrial:” by the “terrestrial” intimating the latter, and by the “celestial,”
      the former. Then farther he introduces a difference of sinners from sinners, saying, “All flesh is not
      the same flesh, but there is one flesh of fishes, another of birds, and another of beasts.” And yet all are
      bodies; but some are in more, and some in lesser vileness. And that in their manner of living too, and
      in their very constitution.
             And having said this, he ascends again to the heaven, saying, “There is one glory of the sun,
      and another glory of the moon.” For as in the earthly bodies there is a difference, so also in the
      heavenly; and that difference no ordinary one, but reaching even to the uttermost: there being
      not only a difference between sun and moon, and stars, but also between stars and stars. For
      what though they be all in the heaven? yet some have a larger, others a less share of glory. What
      do we learn from hence? That although they be all in God’s kingdom, all shall not enjoy the
      same reward; and though all sinners be in hell, all shall not endure the same punishment.16

       Chrysostom correctly perceives that Paul is indicating that not all believers will receive the same reward or
degree of glory even though he acknowledges: they will all be in Heaven; they will all be in God’s kingdom. Here,
he correctly states that all believers will be in the kingdom/Heaven. Although all believers will be in Heaven (in the
kingdom) and receive varying degrees of reward, not all believers will receive glory. Relative to such glory, Paul
had indicated that unfaithful believers might receive no reward (1Cor 3:11-15) and be castaway/disqualified
(1Cor 9:27).
       Basil also perceives a misthological differentiation, perhaps with even a clearer insight than Chrysostom:

      For who is so ignorant of the good things prepared by God for them that are worthy, as not to know
      that the crown of the righteous is the grace of the Spirit, bestowed in more abundant and perfect
      measure in that day, when spiritual glory shall be distributed to each in proportion as he shall
      have nobly played the man? For among the glories of the saints are “many mansions” in the
      Father’s house, that is differences of dignities: for as “star differeth from star in glory, so also is
      the resurrection of the dead.”17

       Basil perceives that the size of your mansion will be in proportion to the degree of your radiance in
glory. He limits these variations in mansions and glory to those who are worthy. This is my point entirely. Only
those believers who are worthy of having heavenly mansions in the Heavenly City will have various degrees of
radiant glory that corresponds to the varying degrees of brightness of the stars. Not all believers will receive the
crown of righteousness, which Basil links with having a portion in the glory of the stars. If he is limiting this
variation in glory to those faithful believers who will receive the crown, then he is dead on target.
       Jerome was of the same opinion:

      “In my Father’s house are many mansions.” I suppose there are also different degrees of merit.
      “One star differeth from another star in glory,” and in the one body of the Church there are different
      members. The sun has its own splendour, the moon tempers the darkness of the night; and the five
      heavenly bodies which are called planets traverse the sky in different tracks and with different degrees
      of luminousness. There are countless other stars whose movements we trace in the firmament. Each
      has its own brightness, and though each in respect of its own is perfect, yet, in comparison with one of
      greater magnitude, it lacks perfection. In the body also with its different members, the eye has one
      function, the hand another, the foot another. Whence the Apostle says, “The eye cannot say to the
      hand, I have no need of thee: or again the head to the feet, I have no need of you. Are all Apostles? are
      all prophets? are all teachers? are all workers of miracles? have all gifts of healing? do all speak with
      tongues? do all interpret? But desire earnestly the greater gifts. But all these worketh the one and the

   John Chrysostom, Homilies of St. John Chrysostom, Archbishop of Constantinople, on the First Epistle of St.

      same Spirit, dividing to each one severally even as He will.” And here mark carefully that he does not
      say, as each member desires, but as the Spirit Himself will. For the vessel cannot say to him that makes
      it, “Why dost thou make me thus or thus? Hath not the potter a right over the clay, from the same lump
      to make one part a vessel unto honour, and another unto dishonour?” And so in close sequence he
      added, “Desire earnestly the greater gifts,” so that, by the exercise of faith and diligence, we may win
      something in addition to other gifts, and may be superior to those who, compared with us, are in the
      second or third class. In a great house there are different vessels, some of gold, some of silver,
      brass, iron, wood. And yet while in its kind a vessel of brass is perfect, in comparison with one
      of silver it is called imperfect, and again one of silver, compared with one of gold, is inferior. And
      thus, when compared with one another, all things are imperfect and perfect. In a field of good soil, and
      from one sowing, there springs a crop thirty-fold, sixty-fold, or a hundred-fold. The very numbers
      show that there is disparity in the parts of the produce, and yet in its own kind each is perfect.18

      The fourth proposition of Jovinianus, that all who are saved will have equal reward, is refuted by the
      various yields of thirty, sixty, and a hundred fold in the parable of the sower, by the “stars differing in
      glory” of 1 Cor. 15:41.19

       Jerome rightly compares the variation in degrees of glory with the heavenly mansions as something we may
win according to our degrees of merit. In support of this conclusion, he alludes to Paul’s statement in 2Tim 2:20:
“Now in a large house there are not only gold and silver vessels, but also vessels of wood and of earthenware, and
some to honor and some to dishonor.” In this analogy, Paul uses vessels of gold and silver to represent variations
of honor, while variations in dishonor are represented by vessels of wood and earthenware. Putting the two
passages together, the most natural inference is that (1) there are two classes of believers and (2) subordinate
variations in those classes. Some believers will be vessels of honor, while others are vessels of dishonor.
Moreover, there will be gradients in both classes. Not all believers will be in the class that receives various
degrees of honor and glory. Paul immediately goes on in the next verse to condition being a vessel of honor on
present morality: “Therefore, if a man cleanses himself from these things, he will be a vessel for honor, sanctified,
useful to the Master, prepared for every good work” (2Tim 2:21). If you do not cleanse yourself, Christian, then
you will be a vessel of dishonor. In that case, you will not have a glorified body but a resurrected body associated
with dishonor and shame.
    Cassian provides a lengthy explanation, part of which will suffice:

             CHÆREMON: In accordance with the condition and measure of every mind Holy Scripture
      summons our free wills to different grades of perfection. For no uniform crown of perfection can be
      offered to all men, because all have not the same virtue, or purpose, or fervour, and so the Divine
      Word has in some way appointed different ranks and different measures of perfection itself. And
      that this is so the variety of beatitudes in the gospel clearly shows. For though they are called
      blessed, whose is the kingdom of heaven, and blessed are they who shall possess the earth, and
      blessed are they who shall receive their consolation, and blessed are they who shall be filled, yet
      we believe that there is a great difference between the habitations of the kingdom of heaven, and
      the possession of the earth, whatever it be, and also between the reception of consolation and the
      fulness and satisfaction of righteousness; and that there is a great distinction between those who
      shall obtain mercy, and those who shall be deemed worthy to enjoy the most glorious vision of
      God. “For there is one glory of the sun, and another glory of the moon, and another glory of the
      stars: for star differeth from star in glory, so also is the resurrection of the dead.”20

He affirms that a great distinction in glory among the celestial bodies of the risen saints is seen in the variation of
