the celestial glory. Origen likewise believes that Paul’s analogy of the variation in celestial glory is intended to
convey a diversification between our spiritual heavenly bodies based on our good works:

       “There is one glory of the sun, another glory of the moon, another glory of the stars, and star
       differs from star in glory.” Therefore those who seek for the glory of the resurrection and for
       honor and incorruption, shall assuredly attain what is written, “The body is sown in dishonor,
       it will rise in glory. It is sown in corruption, it will rise in incorruption.” And so the one who
       searches for this glory and honor and incorruption through perseverance in good works will
       attain to eternal life.21

Origen ties Rom 2:7 with 1Cor 15:41. Both Pauline passages deal with the possibility of earning glory if we
diligently seek it. Origen is correct.
Modern Contentions Regarding Patristics
       Yet some modern interpreters, such as Thiselton (NIGTC), are so resistant to the patristic affirmation of
reward in 1Cor 15:41 that they make statements that are flagrantly false: “Some patristic writers interpret this
allegorically of the different glories of different raised believers. But this is not Paul’s point, even if it suggests the
broad principle of dynamic diversified life in the resurrection mode of existence.” Thiselton admits that Paul’s
analogy suggests diversification of life in our mode of existence at the resurrection and yet denies that it is a
diversification in terms of glory even though Paul explicitly affirms that this is the diversification he is talking
about! Thiselton is doing nothing more than engaging in sophisticated doublespeak.
       Lange (ACHS) is really no better: “The allusion here might naturally lead us to think of the various degrees
of glory in the resurrection bodies, as compared with each other; but the context does not point to this, and all the
allegorical deductions, such as we find in Tertullian and others, must be pronounced erroneous.” Yes, Paul’s
analogy naturally leads to understanding him as implying various degrees of glory in our resurrected bodies. Why
not embrace this natural understanding? Because Lange says that it is an unnatural understanding of the context.
No, it is not. Per Paul’s earlier statements, some of the statements he is making in this context must be limited to
faithful believers, such as inheriting the kingdom. The context refutes rather than confirms Lange’s objection.
       Lenski (TIO) shows the same faulty reasoning: “Paul is not writing about the differences that will appear
between the saints and the fact that some will shine in greater glory than others. This difference is referred to in
other passages of Holy Writ, it is not mentioned in this verse.” True, this difference is not explicitly stated in
1Cor 15:41, but it is explicitly stated in other passages, as Lenski acknowledges. We have no compelling reason to
think that Paul does not intend this as a secondary meaning in this passage. Such commentators are blind to the
misthological understanding of the text and context. Patristic writers are often superior to their modern critics in
this regard.
       Other commentators, such as Robertson and Plummer (ICC), praise the patristic understanding:

       It is legitimate to apply these differences in the heavenly bodies to possible differences in the
       glories of the risen saints, and it is not impossible that the Apostle had this thought in his mind.
       See Tert. De Res. 49, 52. But his main argument is that God, who made all these known differences
       and connexions, may have made differences and connexions between our present and future bodies
       which are quite beyond our comprehension. Immense differences there are certain to be. See some
       excellent remarks of Origen in Jerome, Letter to Pammachius against John of Jerusalem, 26.

Robertson and Plummer are quite restrained when they say that “it is not impossible that” Paul had differences in
degrees of glory of the risen saints in mind when he used this star analogy. Actually, it should be practically
impossible to think that Paul did not have it in mind. In view of the passages Paul had in mind, even the generic
comment made by Lowery (BKC) requires a misthological differentiation: “The differences in splendor between
the earthly bodies and the heavenly bodies suggested to Paul the differences between a natural and a spiritual body
(cf. Dan. 12:3 where resurrected saints were compared to stars; also Matt. 13:43)” (emphasis his). Yet, if Dan 12:3
and Mt 13:43 are the backdrop for Paul’s analogy, then Paul absolutely must intend misthological implications

21
  Origen, Commentary on the Epistle to the Romans, Books 1–5, T. P. Halton (ed.), T. P. Scheck, (trans.), vol 103
(Washington, DC: The Catholic University of America Press, 2001), 115–116.
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                   Page 17
since these passages are strongly misthological.
       Yet Jamieson, Fausset, and Brown (JFB) cite these passages and assert:

      The analogy is not to prove different degrees of glory among the blessed (whether this be or not
      indirectly hinted), but this: As the various fountains of light, which is so similar in its properties, differ
      (the sun from the moon, and the moon from the stars; and even one star from another, though all seem
      so much alike); so it is no unreasonable doctrine that our present bodies differ much from our
      resurrection bodies, though still continuing bodies (cf. the simile, Dan. 12:3; Matt. 13:43). Also that
      of seed in the same parable (Matt. 13:24; Gal. 6:7,8). (Emphasis theirs.)

       By citing these misthological texts as cross-references, JFB undercuts its own denial of misthological
implications. Moreover, it is refuted by its own admission cited earlier that 1Cor 15:49 is an exhortation that implies
a promise. By the same token, 1Cor 15:41 should be taken as an affirmation that implies a misthological
differentiation. Indeed, part of the misthological defense that Exell (BI) uses, entitled Degrees of Glory in Heaven,
is an exposition of 1Cor 15:41-42 and cites such passages:

              II. THIS IS PROVED—1. By Scripture. (1) By all those passages which lay down, in general terms,
      the great rule of God’s proceedings with the children of men (2 Cor. 5:10; Rom. 2:6; 2 Cor. 9:6;
      Gal. 6:7–9). (2) From the account which Paul gives of the different rewards which will be given to the
      ministers of the gospel (chap. 3:12–15). In this representation there are persons who obtain salvation,
      and yet have not the recompense which the wiser administrators of the Word receive. And therefore
      we conclude that there will be the same difference between hearers, according to the manner they have
      profited. (3) From Dan. 12:3. As there is a difference between the general brilliancy of the firmament
      and the lustre of the stars, so there shall be a difference between those ordinary Christians who obtain
      felicity and those zealous persons who have been the instruments of the conversion of many sinners.
      (4) From the parable of the pounds (Luke 19). (5) From those passages where we find the patriarchs,
      the prophets, and the apostles represented as occupying a more conspicuous situation in glory than
      ordinary believers (Matt. 8:11; 19:28; Luke 22:30). 2. From analogy. Look at—(1) Nature. In what an
      infinite variety of methods do you see the Creator displaying His perfections! (2) The operations of
      grace. “There are diversities of gifts, though but one Spirit.” (3) Christians. How various their
      attainment, knowledge, holiness, and joy, though all beloved by God! (4) The heavenly host. Though
      all holy and happy, there are archangels, and angels, thrones, dominions, principalities, and powers.
      3. From the transactions of the judgment-day, and the nature of the future felicity (Matt. 25).

       Unless Paul has temporally forgotten all such passages, some of which are his very own from this very epistle,
it stands to reason that such passages inform his illustration and require misthological association. Seeking to
counter such claims, Calvin (CC) asserts disassociation with such passages:

             A mistake, however, is commonly fallen into in the application; for it is supposed that Paul
      meant to say, that, after the resurrection, the saints will have different degrees of honour and glory.
      This, indeed, is perfectly true, and is proved by other declarations of Scripture; but it has nothing to
      do with Paul’s object. For he is not arguing as to what difference of condition there will be among the
      saints after the resurrection, but in what respect our bodies at present differ from those that we will
      one day receive.

      Calvin’s rejection of the applicability of all such passages in his interpretation of 1Cor 15:41 is consistent
with his rejection of the subjunctive in 1Cor 15:49. One can hardly avoid smirking, however, when reading his
conclusion in 1Cor 15:58:

             Having satisfied himself that he had sufficiently proved the doctrine of the resurrection, he now
      closes his discussion with an exhortation; and this has much more force, than if he had made use of a
      simple conclusion with an affirmation. Since your labour, says he, is not in vain in the Lord, be
      steadfast, and abound in good works. Now he says that their labour is not in vain, for this reason,
      that there is a reward laid up for them with God. This is that exclusive hope which, in the first
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                         Page 18
      instance, encourages believers, and afterwards sustains them, so that they do not stop short in the
      race. Hence he exhorts them to remain steadfast, because they rest on a firm foundation, as they
      know that a better life is prepared for them in heaven.
             He adds—abounding in the work of the Lord; for the hope of a resurrection makes us not be
      weary in well-doing, as he teaches in Col. 1:10. For amidst so many occasions of offence as constantly
      present themselves to us, who is there that would not despond, or turn aside from the way, were it not
      that, by thinking of a better life he is by this means kept in the fear of God? Now, on the other hand,
      he intimates, that if the hope of a resurrection is taken away, then, the foundation (as it were)
      being rooted up, the whole structure of piety falls to the ground. Unquestionably, if the hope of
      reward is taken away and extinguished, alacrity in running will not merely grow cold, but will
      be altogether destroyed. (Italics his.)

        So, as Calvin rightly notes, Paul’s whole argument in this context is destroyed if Paul’s discussion does not
lead the Corinthians to conclude that their running and labor results in a differentiation of reward at the resurrection.
They must be able to perceive, per Paul’s discussion, that those who run and labor have a better life prepared for
them in Heaven than those who do not. Yet, according to Calvin, Paul did not mean to imply a differentiation of
heavenly reward in the resurrection between those who run and do not run! Calvin would have us think that Paul
intends for the Corinthians to understand that they all receive the same glorified body regardless of whether they
run or not! Per Calvin, no differentiation in degrees of glory is insinuated or anticipated by Paul’s analogies!
Calvin’s argument self-destructs. Paul’s misthological exhortation in v. 58 requires misthological implications to
be present in his discussion leading up to that point. In v. 58, Paul makes a rational, imperative exhortation that
draws upon his subjunctive exhortation in v. 49, both of which are befitting his luminary illustration. Calvin is
absolutely correct that the resurrection is the foundational truth that Paul is asserting, without which Paul’s entire
call to piety falls to the ground. Nevertheless, Calvin is mistaken to think that Paul is only laying a foundation! Not
so! Paul has laid the groundwork for misthological implications, and he has built upon the foundation with these
misthological implications.
        Barnes (BN) is much closer to the truth:

      The idea here is, therefore, not only that the bodies of the saints in heaven shall differ from those
      on earth, but that they shall differ among themselves, in a sense somewhat like the difference of
      the splendour of the sun, the moon, and the different stars. Though all shall be unlike what they
      were on earth, and all shall be glorious, yet there may be a difference in that splendour and
      glory. (Italics his.)

       He is correct that 1Cor 15:41 has a dual purpose: First, the foundational truth is to show that our
resurrected bodies differ from our earthly bodies. Second, the implicational truth built upon that foundation
is to show that our resurrected bodies differ in degree of glory. Barnes is mistaken, nonetheless, to assert that
this verse necessarily teaches that all believers will have glorious bodies. Until recently, I likewise thought that this
passage teaches that all believers will have glorious bodies, but I based that assessment on 1Cor 15:43: “It is sown
in dishonor, it is raised in glory.” Until now, like so many others, my prevailing opinion has been that this verse
indicates that all believers will be raised with glorified bodies. Even so, I could not bring myself to import that
meaning into 1Cor 15:41 because the OT and NT background argues strongly against doing so.
Passages Affirming Misthological Glory
      Misthological glory is affirmed in various passages: Dan 12:2-3; Mt 13:43; Rom 2:7,10; 5:2; 8:17-18;
1Cor 15:41-43; 2Cor 4:17; Eph 5:17; Col 1:27; Phil 2:16; 1Thess 2:12,20; 2Thess 2:14; 2Tim 2:10; Heb 2:7,9-10;
1Pet 1:6-8; 1Pet 4:12; 5:4,10 (cf. 1Enoch 39:7; 104:2).22 As frequently noted by commentators, Jesus’

22
   In Col 1:12, the Father has provisionally “qualified us to share in the inheritance of the saints [hagios] in light.”

acknowledgment that the righteous will shine like the sun in Mt 13:43 is an obvious allusion to the righteous shining
like the expanse of heaven and stars in Dan 12:3 and is prefigured in His transformation: “He was transfigured
before them; and His face shone like the sun, and His garments became as white as light” (Mt 17:2). This shining
glory is a misthological reward limited to believers who are morally righteous. Those believers who bear Christ’s
moral image now will bear His glorious image then. Surely, Paul is not completely ignoring all such passages and
indiscriminately applying 1Cor 15:41 to all believers regardless of the way they live, which would be contrary to
all such passages. Consequently, until now, I have presumed that all believers will be raised with glorified bodies
(1Cor 15:43), but not all believers will have sufficient glory to shine like the sun or stars (v. 41), so that the degrees
of glory explicitly denoted in luminous variations in v. 41 are expanded in v. 43 to include a subluminous range. I
am amending that stance in the present discussion by placing v. 43 exclusively in the misthological spectrum, which
aligns with the hortatory understanding of v. 49 and accords much better with Dan 12:2-3, where the misthological
shining of righteous believers is juxtaposed with misthological shame of unrighteous believers.
       Certain unrighteous believers are even considered by Paul to be enemies of the cross. Thus, I would amend
my summation in MMP1 by now postulating that those citizens of the kingdom who become enemies of the cross
are to receive immortal bodies, not glorified bodies. Their potential glory will be turned into misthological shame
(Phil 3:18-21). Their immortal bodies, which otherwise would have shined with glory, will not shine. When Paul
says that their glory is/becomes their shame (Phil 3:19), what glory is he talking about? He immediately supplies
the answer with an explanatory for (gar): Their glory, at the time when believer’s bodies should be transformed
into glorified bodies (3:21), will be the occasion and source of their shame. Their immortal bodies will be their
shame. For the prospects of a similar Pauline outcome, compare Col 3:4 with Col 3:23-25. John teaches the same
thing. When Christ appears and believers are transformed into immortal bodies, many of these transformed believers
will be put to shame. “When He appears” (1Jn 2:28; 3:2), they will all see Him, but some will fail to become like
Him (1Jn 3:2) and be put away in shame from before Him (1Jn 2:28; cp. Ps 1:5; Mk 8:38; Lk 9:26; Jude 1:24). The
imagery of this truth is most vivid in Rev 3:18, where transformed believers are ashamed of their misthological
nakedness because they are walking about in the raw—completely devoid of white garments, just as Jesus
anticipated (Mt 22:12). They do not shine at all. Therefore, their bodies would be described as immortal and
imperishable but not glorious. Harmonizing Paul’s statement in 1Cor 15:43 with numerous other passages, some of
which are his own, and with his own broader and immediate context in this epistle, requires this conclusion.23 For
instance, in conjunction with what Paul said in 1Cor 3:11-15, we can surmise that in the resurrection, all believers
will have foundational immortal bodies allowing them to live with the Lord in a glorified state/place, namely the
celestial outer darkness (see MMP5), but the immortal bodies of those who have had all their works burnt up will
not shine. They will not have glorified bodies: spiritual bodies, yes; glorified bodies, no.
       As explained in MMP5, having Christ in us is our hope of glory (Col 1:27). Yet this hope is seminal. Having
Christ in us gives us the capacity to become like Him in our character so that we may become like Him in our
radiance. If we grow to be Christlike in our character, we will shine like Him in splendor. If we are victorious, we
will have tremendous internal glory: “For I consider that the sufferings of this present time are not worthy to be
compared with the glory which shall be revealed in (eis) us” (Rom 8:18; cp. vv. 8:17b,21). This glory to be revealed
in us is the revealing of the glory of the beauty of faithful believers (GNTC). Having Christ in us gives us this
seminal capacity.
       This misthological understanding of glory in Col 1:27 increases the probability that glory is also

(Col 1:22-23). The articular form of the adjectival form of holy is used in Col 1:12 to make it nominal: The saints
(ho hagios—holy ones) of Col 1:12 who will be presented holy (hagios) at the Bema worthy of this inheritance in
the light are those believers who are presently meeting the moral requirements. We are presently qualified to run/live
in such a way that we may eschatologically qualify to inherit this saintly portion in light unless we disqualify
ourselves. Paul acknowledges that they could be disqualified (katabrabeuo) for this prize/reward (Col 2:18) and
that he could be disqualified (adokimos) as well (1Cor 9:24-27). Paul warned believers to be on guard against savage
wolves that would come among them and yet encouraged them that God “is able” to give them an “inheritance
among all those who are made holy [hagiazo]” (Acts 20:32, TM). Paul enjoins the verbal form of being made
saintly/holy with the contingent possibility of the inheritance in this verse, providing a close linguistic and
conceptual parallel to Col 1:12.

misthological in Col 3:4, as acknowledged in MMP3 in deference to Hollandsworth. Accordingly,
Hollandsworth’s terminology is preferable: unglorified immortals will merely have redeemed bodies rather than
glorified bodies. Accepting Hollandsworth’s conditional understanding of the glory in Col 3:4 also yields a better
sense for a moral imperative in Col 3:5. If you want to be glorified (v. 4), then it is imperative that you meet the
condition of practical sanctification (v. 5). In view of 1Jn 2:28, a similar argument could be made regarding hope
in 1Jn 3:3 and thus for a misthological understanding of 1Jn 3:2. All believers will see Jesus and be transformed
into His likeness in some capacity or another. But Jesus can be seen in a mere resurrected body, as He was on the
road to Emmaus (Lk 24:13-31), or in a glorified body (Rev 1:12-17), which invokes such a sense of awe that mortals
fall down as if dead before it. As noted in MMP3, everyone will stand before God in judgment at some point
(Rev 20:15), but only misthologically worthy believers will be able to stand it (Lk 21:36; cp. 1Jn 2:28). They have
the strength to stand (CT) and are counted worthy of standing (MT). They will be transformed into the same type
of glorious body as the Lord and thus be able to stand before the Lord’s glorious body. A marked difference must
be made between merely standing before God and being worthy of doing so. Accordingly, the transformation in
Phil 3:21 would be exclusively mistholic rather than dualistic (contrary to what I suggested earlier in that
misthological series). This assessment of Phil 3:21 is better suited for the previous comments about Phil 3:20 in that
series. The citizenship within Heaven affirmed in Phil 3:20 probably includes the eschatological destruction of
unfaithful believers, alluded to in Phil 3:19 (cp. 1:15-16; 2:21), within its scope of possibilities, thus providing
impetus for the moral exhortation to stand firm in the Lord in Phil 4:1. Therefore, the transformation into a glorious
body like the Lord’s is also misthological and misthopological, just as Hollandsworth has surmised in his chapter
dedicated to this subject.25
 Dual Foundational and Implicational Affirmations
      The OT and NT background leads us to anticipate that Paul would make the same distinction in 1Cor 15:41:
Only faithful believers shine. Many commentators assume that Paul is merely making a distinction between earthly
bodies and heavenly bodies, so as to indicate that all believers will have the same type of body—a heavenly one.
However, commentators such as Lias (CBSC) rightly point out that Paul makes this basic distinction and then
advances beyond it in v. 41:

      but the glory of the celestial is one, and the glory of the terrestrial is another] The celestial body is
      superior to the terrestrial. In like manner, and to a similar extent, shall the risen body surpass the
      present human organism.
      41. There is one glory of the sun, and another glory of the moon] The argument is pushed a step
      farther. The celestial bodies are not all alike. They differ in beauty and excellency. And so to all
      eternity it shall be true of men raised and in possession of their heavenly bodies, that ‘one star differeth
      from another star in glory.’ So Chrysostom on v. 38. “Augustine elegantly says, ‘splendor dispar:
      cœlum commune.’ ” Wordsworth. An erroneous interpretation of St Matt. 20:10 has led some to the
      conclusion that all rewards shall be exactly alike in the world to come. As the Apostle here shews, the
      analogy of nature makes against this in every way. And the passage just cited has reference not to the
      equality of rewards, but of the principle on which such rewards are given. The labourer is rewarded,
      not for length of service, but for the spirit in which that service has been rendered.
      42. So also is the resurrection of the dead The fact is now plainly stated that all shall not possess
      the same degree of glory in heaven. ‘So,’ i.e. as has been before stated. But St Paul goes on to deal
      less with the fact than with the manner in which the fact is accomplished. (Emphasis his.)

       Lias does much better than Calvin in realizing that Paul does not stop at foundational truth but pushes
beyond it a step further to imply misthological distinction. The OT background, the unbalanced chiastic
structure, and exhortations prove this to be the case.26 As in the case of Barnes, the only tweak that needs to be
added is to note that the variation regarding glory is so extensive that Tertullian is correct to clarify that believers
who have lived earthly lives will not have bodies that shine with heavenly glory. Worldly believers will merely

have immortal bodies. Dillow has an excellent two-page discussion on 1Cor 15:41 and cites additional patristic
sources who believe the verse is describing degrees of glory of resurrected believers.27 Like myself, Dillow sides
with commentators who perceive degrees of glory among resurrected believers in v. 41 as being the secondary
implication of Paul’s analogy. He cites Godet as an interesting hybrid in that Godet agrees with Calvin (on v. 49)
but nonetheless allows misthology as the secondary intention (on v. 41).
Hybrids
      Godet explains his hybrid view at length, but I will only interact with the key components. On 1Cor 15:41,
he comments:

             It has often been thought, that by stopping to describe so particularly this wide diversity
      of splendour, the apostle meant to allude to the difference of glory which will exist among the
      risen, according to the different degrees of moral perfection to which they have attained. The
      Fathers especially dwelt fondly on this view; see Ambrose, Chrysostom, Tertullian. This last
      makes the future body of God’s servants correspond to the flesh of men; that of pagans, to the flesh of
      beasts; that of the martyrs, to the flesh of birds; that of the Christians who have had only baptism with
      water, to the flesh of fishes; then the glory of Christ corresponds to the brightness of the sun; that of
      the Church, to the brightness of the moon; that of the Jews, to the brightness of the stars (De
      Resurrectione, c. 52). All this is evidently only a play of imagination. The context requires no such
      application; for, as is proved by the sequel, Paul proposes, by bringing as it were before the very eye
      the infinite resources of Divine power, to show that God can hold in reserve for His elect a body
      absolutely different from their terrestrial body. But, while holding exegetically by this application, the
      only one justified by the context, we need not deny the possibility of a purely secondary allusion
      to the diversity which God may be pleased to make between the bodies of the risen. As Holsten
      well says: “The way in which Paul emphasizes the diversity of the heavenly bodies implies the
      supposition of an analogous difference of glory between the risen.”28

       True, the resurrection is primary; misthological diversity is secondary. Moreover, I agree with Godet that
Tertullian overstates his case. I grayed out the overstated part of Tertullian’s material in the above quote from Godet
and did not include it in my quotes of Tertullian.29 Even so, the main thrust of Tertullian’s argument remains

   Joseph Dillow, Final Destiny: The Future Reign of the Servant Kings, 20th edition (Monument, CO: Paniym
Group, 2014), 1006-1007.
28
   Frederic Louis Godet, Commentary on First Corinthians (Grand Rapids: Kregel Publications, 1977), 837-838.
29
   Whipple’s treatment of the passage is decidedly misthological but reminds me of Tertullian in overstating his
case by appealing to various particulars which may or may not be valid. I disagree with Whipple on several of the
particulars he implements and would not want to base my case on such particulars. To a lesser extent,
Hollandsworth’s argument for 1Cor 15:35-42 is also based on a particular used by Whipple. Gary T. Whipple,
Shock and Surprise Beyond the Rapture! (Miami Springs: Schoettle Publishing Co., 1992), 209-251.
Hollandsworth, 96-99.
         Hollandsworth makes a strong probable connection between 1Cor 15:35-42 and Jn 12:23-25. Dying morally
is necessary to be raised gloriously. I strongly agree with him that a misthological differentiation in dimensional
essence is indicated: “Not all bodies will be alike in essence” (Hollandsworth, 97). Indeed, in MMP5 I infer that
unglorified immortal believers will have 4D bodies while glorified immortal believers will have 6D bodies. They
will differ in dimensional essence. Since the present study demonstrates that (1) inductive probability calls for
misthological differentiation in 1Cor 15:41 and that (2) deductive consistency demands it, we can be certain
that Paul intends a misthological differentiation in this passage. Therefore, the association Hollandsworth
makes with Jn 12:23-25 is valid. The question is, Is it true by way of application or by way of interpretation?
Hermeneutically, we base application on interpretation. I am not sure that Paul was thinking about or exegeting
Jn 12:23-25 when he wrote 1Cor 15:35-42 (though one might consult PNTC and NAC, for example, for a link
between the natural/soulical body of 1Cor 15:44 with the natural/soulical body in 1Cor 2:14-15 and then posit that
the soul must die to obtain misthological glory, which is an argument that Whipple makes at length). Even so,
Jn 12:23-25 certainly applies conceptually to 1Cor 15:35-42. Having exegetically demonstrated herein, even from
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                         Page 22
persuasive, so much so that Godet acknowledges the possibility of misthology as Paul’s secondary intent. With
Dillow, Barnes, and Lias, I would agree that this misthological implication from Paul’s discussion is secondary to
his primary argument regarding the resurrection. Where such writers could justifiably challenge Godet is when he
calls this misthological implication a possibility, not necessarily justified by the context. Better would be to call it
a probability, strongly implied by the context. Even better would be to call it a necessity, required by the implications
of the immediate context in light of the broader context.
        Taking the various degrees of glory as a necessity is consistent with adopting the subjunctive in 1Cor 15:49.
Godet rejects the subjunctive because he says that the context demands the indicative, that it will not allow an
exhortation because this requires taking image in a moral sense, which he claims is ruled out by 1Cor 15:50, since
he believes this verse is talking exclusively about eschatological/ontological necessity to inherit the kingdom. I
agree with him that 1Cor 15:50 requires an eschatologically ontological transformation. Paul’s immediate argument
is that we must have transformed bodies that are impressable to inherit the kingdom. However, in lieu of the broader
context, since unrighteous believers cannot inherit the kingdom (1Cor 6:9-11), Paul’s secondary presupposition
must be that inheriting the kingdom requires a present moral transformation as well. Accordingly, since 1Cor 15:50
requires (1) an eschatologically ontological transformation as its primary requisite and (2) a temporally moral
transformation as its preliminary prerequisite, we can, at a minimum, conclude that Paul must intend to infer various
degrees of misthological glory in 1Cor 15:41. Likewise, the imperative of 1Cor 15:49 would have both temporal
and eschatological dimensions. We must bear Christ’s image temporally if we are to bear it misthologically. Hence,
in 1Cor 15:58, we are to work temporally because our toil will not be in vain eschatologically. Only when this
context is read in isolation, and thereby taken out of context of its broader epistolary context, can Godet’s assessment
be taken seriously. For those who refuse to take the passage out of context, his argument against the subjunctive is
groundless.
        MacDonald (BBC) shows a slight preference for hybridization. To his credit, he has a decided preference for
a misthological understanding of 1Cor 15:41:

              Most commentators agree that Paul is still emphasizing that the glory of the resurrection body
      will be different from the glory of the body which we have on earth at the present time. They do not
      think that verse 41, for instance, indicates that in heaven there will be differences of glory among
      believers themselves. However, we tend to agree with Holsten that “the way in which Paul
      emphasizes the diversities of the heavenly bodies implies the supposition of an analogous
      difference of glory between the risen.” It is clear from other passages of Scripture that we shall not
      all be identical in heaven. Although all will resemble the Lord Jesus morally, that is, in freedom from
      sin, it does not follow that we shall all look like the Lord Jesus physically. He will be distinctly
      recognizable as such throughout all eternity. Likewise, we believe that each individual Christian will
      be a distinct personality recognizable as such. But there will be differences of reward granted at the
      Judgment Seat of Christ according to one’s faithfulness in service. While all will be supremely
      happy in haven, some will have greater capacity for enjoying heaven. Just as there will be differences
      of suffering in hell, according to the sins that a man has committed, so there will be differences of
      enjoyment in heaven, according to what we have done as believers. (Emphasis his).

Paul’s own writings, the necessity (not mere possibility or probability) of a secondary misthological interpretation
for 1Cor 15:41, the underlying misthological premise of the entire Pauline passage is proven. Accordingly, I
recommend Hollandsworth and Whipple’s arguments regarding various particulars, even though I have not pursued
them herein, as having homiletical application. In particular, Hollandsworth’s discussion appears flawless, at least
by way of application and conceptual association. Moreover, conceptually by way of application, I would concur
with much of Whipple’s discussion, nevertheless with several reservations.
         For one thing, based on 1Cor 15:51-57, Whipple erroneously assumes that all believers will be changed
into glorified bodies at the commencement of the eternal state when death is defeated. Not true. His conclusion errs
because it is missing my particulars regarding the eternality of misthological death. Rewards and lack thereof are
eternal. Dan 12:2-3, for instance, shows the misthological eternality of the radiance and lack thereof for those raised.
Misthological death is only overcome by believers who morally overcome. Notwithstanding, as a result of the
present study, I concur with Whipple that believers who fail to overcome will merely have redeemed bodies at the
Bema, not glorified bodies (p. 217). Where Whipple erred is in failing to perceive that this will be their eternal state
as well.
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                 Page 23

       Contrary to MacDonald, not all believers will be supremely happy in Heaven. Unfaithful believers confined
in the outer darkness will have their eternal joy tainted with eternal regret. That point aside, like Godet, MacDonald
favorably quotes Holsten and concurs with the misthological distinction in degrees of rewards as being indicated in
v. 41. MacDonald provides the typical translation of v. 49 with the indicative but cites the subjective translation in
his endnote. If this indicates his slight preference for the indicative, then it is a hybrid view. More consistent would
be to adopt the subjunctive. Regarding 1Cor 15:50, he simply concludes that the surety of the resurrection assures
us that our work is not in vain. But this is false. For our work not to be in vain requires both the soteriological
resurrection and misthological differentiation. MacDonald, like Paul, must presume that his affirmation that our
work is not in vain entails a misthological differentiation based on our works. Both MacDonald and Paul can safely
presume that their readers will make this inference because of their comments in the immediate and broad context.
                                         Misthological Glory in 1Cor 15:43
Misthological Glory in Rom 8:21
        Commentators almost always write as if 1Cor 15:43 applies to all believers. Even misthologically astute
commentators such as López (GNTC) and Hodges (GNTC2) make this assumption. Rom 8:21 is frequently paired
with 1Cor 15:43 as being soteriologically applicable to all believers. Until this study, I was inclined to do so as well,
at least regarding 1Cor 15:43. However, considering the above analysis, revisiting Rom 8:21 is prudent.
        For reasons explained at length in DOLF, we only have a very brief window of opportunity to develop a
beautiful, godly character through suffering and fighting. If we are victorious, we will have tremendous internal
glory: “For I consider that the sufferings of this present time are not worthy to be compared with the glory which
shall be revealed in (eis) us” (Rom 8:18; cp. vv. 8:17b,21). This glory to be revealed in us is the revealing of the
glory of the beauty of faithful believers (GNTC).
        Therein, I cited López (GNTC) in support of taking the glory in Rom 8:18 as misthological. Although López
thinks that two degrees of glory are entailed in Rom 8, so that 8:21 is unconditional for all God’s children, he rightly
acknowledges the glory to be revealed in us in Rom 8:17 “refers to faithful believers who will share in the honor,
prestige, and reward of co-reigning with Christ” (emphasis his.) One problem with his assessment of v. 21, though,
is that Paul limits this misthological honor to faithful believers and says that unfaithful believers will be dishonored
(2Tim 2:20). How would López reconcile unfaithful believers clearly being dishonored with their allegedly being
honored? A more detailed analysis is required. The fuller quote in López’s standalone commentary provides more
detail:

              8:18-22. For (gar) is an explanatory conjunction that expands the meaning of suffering of this
      present time (expressed in v 17). Hence believers ought to consider (cf. 4:3 for the meaning of the
      Gk term logizomai) it beneficial to undergo struggles now, because the future rewards for the faithful
      (cf. 2 Cor 4:17) in the Messianic age far outweighs present suffering. Sharing in Christ’s suffering is
      a precursor to being glorified. One should distinguish between two kinds of glory expressed in chapter
      8. Glorification corresponds to the third stage of Christian salvation that occurs at resurrection as a
      result of solely believing in Christ (5:2; 8:21, 30; cf. 1 John 3:2). Yet, glory here revealed in the
      mature sons of God (cf. v 17) refers to faithful believers who will share in the honor, prestige and
      reward of co-reigning with Christ over creation (Heb 1:8-9; 2 Tim 2:12; Rev 2:26-28). When a king
      was crowned (for his faithfulness to the kingdom for which he served; cf. Heb 1:5-9), he was glorified.
      So believers will be crowned (1 Cor 9:25; Phil 4:1; 1 Thess 2:19; 2 Tim 4:8; Jas 1:12; 1 Pet 5:4; Rev
      2:10; 3:11) and glorified (for their faithfulness; cf. Rom 8:18-27) with many rewards. But especially
      in this context, this glorification refers to the rewards of exercising dominion over creation with Christ
      (8:18-23; cf. Rev 2:26-27). Presently creation was subjected to futility (lit. a state of being without
      use or value, emptiness, purposelessness or transitoriness, BDAG, 621) since the curse of the fall (Gen
      3:17-19). However, the creation will be delivered from the curse (described as futility in v 20) into
      the glorious liberty of the children of God. Noticed how Paul carefully distinguishes the meaning of
      the latter phrase liberty of the children of God from the earnest expectation to be revealed by the sons
      of God (v 19). Both phrases have distinct meanings. The earnest expectation that awaits creation refers
      to the ones committed to Christ (vv13-19) revealed as the future ruling sons of God. That is creation
      waits to be under the control of these future rulers. Conversely, Paul does not state that creation awaits
      the liberty of the sons of God. That would elevate creation to the place belonging to its future rulers.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                       Page 24
      All children of God equally inherit the earth liberated from the curse of the fall. However, only the
      sons of God as committed believers who suffer for Christ will receive a double portion (vv13-15, 17-
      19) as firstborn sons to rule creation.30 (Bold and italics his; highlight mine.)
López is mistaken to say that all of God’s children will inherit the Earth, much less that they will do so equally.
Jesus says, “Blessed are the gentle, for they shall inherit the earth” (Mt 5:5). Many years before, David had made
the same affirmation (Ps 37:11). Inheriting the Earth is an exclusively misthological concept. López should have
known better than to make it a soteriological equality. To be sure, Rom 8:17 uses a soteriological/misthological
duality between being a child/heir of God versus a son/co-heir of Christ. This duality plausibly carries over into
Rom 8:30. Thus, one might suspect that Rom 8:21 is another case of soteric-mistholic duality. On the other hand,
López’s correct assessment that the glory described in Rom 8:17-19 is misthological leads one to suspect that the
glory of the children of God in Rom 8:21 is not talking about those who remain children (as in Rom 8:17a). Rather,
their temporary status as children in v. 21 coincides with the groaning of childbirth described in Rom 8:22-23,
which culminates in misthological adoption as sons in Rom 8:23, in conjunction with a misthological hope and
salvation in Rom 8:24-25 attained by their present sanctification. This induction would regard Rom 8:21 as an
exclusively misthological text. The passage is not describing the glory of those children who stagnate in their
development and thus fail to mature. Rather, the children described in this passage experience the pains of childbirth,
along with creation, and progress to misthological adoption31 that does not stop at simply having a redeemed body.
These adopted sons have glorified bodies, not merely redeemed bodies.
       Like López, Hodges (GNTC2) likewise thinks that Rom 8:21 and 1Cor 15:43 refer to the “body of glory”
every child of God will receive regardless of the way they live. Hodges would have done better, though, to follow
through on the implication of his statement on Rom 8:21 that “these children will at that time have fully entered
into the privilege of their ‘adoption as sons’ ([Rom] 8:15).” However, when commenting on Rom 8:15, he
acknowledges that it is talking about adult sons who are “‘led by the Spirit of God’ (v. 14).” Consequently, by
Hodges’ own analysis, this transition from childhood to adoption is only referring to faithful believers who
qualify as co-heirs (Rom 8:17). Contextually, Rom 8:21 is talking about the glory of children as they
experience their misthological adoption as mature sons, not the supposed soteriological glory of those
children who fail to experience misthological adoption as mature sons.

       Paul is not talking about the glory of the children of God who fail to mature and thus fail to become
co-heirs with Christ. Rather, his focus is on the children of God who suffer with Christ so that they may be
glorified with Christ when they move from their childhood status to son status when they are adopted at the
resurrection.

31
  For misthological adoption, see my books TOD and DSAS. The adoption/sonship (huiothesia) in Rom 8:23 is an
acknowledgment of our status as mature sons (huios). This legal status as adult sons will be necessary for rulership.
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                 Page 25
        The glory in Rom 8:18 is the co-glory of Rom 8:17, as Paul explains with for (gar) in Rom 9:19, because it
is for the sons of God, namely their adoption as sons. Rom 8:21-22 is simply describing their transition from their
status as children of God to being sons of God at the time of the resurrection. These two verses are not describing
believers who will remain children at the time of the resurrection but those children who by means of suffering with
Christ have qualified for adoption as mature sons who inherit the kingdom.32
        Granted, all of God’s children will be in a state/place of glory since even the outer darkness is in the glorious
third heaven; however, not all of God’s children will have an internal essence of glory that makes them ontologically
superior even to the angels in radiance. Not all believers will have glorified bodies that glow with the radiance of
Christ. For that reason, not all believers will have the power to stand before the Son (Lk 21:36). Only believers
whose inner man has been made an eternal weight of glory (2Cor 4:17) will be able to stand the weight of His glory.
Misthological Glory in 1Cor 15:43
      Not every commentator is obtuse to the misthological implication of 1Cor 15:43. Ciampa and Rosner (PNTC)
commendably tie in Dan 12:2-3:
             Even if, like much else in vv. 36–41, Paul’s distinction between earthly and heavenly bodies is
      dependent on Genesis 1, the tie between resurrection bodies and the lights in the heavens was
      probably also encouraged or inspired by the most explicit passage on bodily resurrection in the
      Old Testament, Daniel 12:2–3: “Multitudes who sleep in the dust of the earth will awake: some
      to everlasting life, others to shame and everlasting contempt. Those who are wise will shine like
      the brightness of the heavens, and those who lead many to righteousness, like the stars forever
      and ever.” The Old Greek of v. 2 speaks of those who will “light up like the luminaries of heaven,”
      while Theodotion’s version has “shine like the splendor of the firmament”272 in the same place (both
      translations from NETS). Both versions of the verse indicate that either the same or another group will
      shine like “the stars.” The theme of the splendor or luminescence of the righteous in the resurrection
      is quite clear. Since Daniel’s text is the clearest reference to the subject, one assumes that its
      influence on Jewish thinking on the matter would have been quite significant.
If Paul can presume that the Corinthians could be swayed by his OT citation regarding victory over death in
1Cor 15:54-55, why can we not presume that he would likewise anticipate the persuasive force of Dan 12:2-3 in his
star analogy in 1Cor 15:41 be carried over into 1Cor 15:43, as Ciampa and Rosner have done? Ciampa and Rosner
also point out another OT background passage for 1Cor 15:43 that should not go unnoticed regarding the honor
Paul is associating with the resurrection (PNTC):
             After being sown in dishonor, the body is raised in glory. Genesis 1 suggested, as poetically
      expressed in Psalm 8, that God had “crowned [humanity] with glory and honor” (Ps. 8:5). But
      humanity had lost most of its glory and would regain it again only in the resurrection. Jung Hoon
      Kim has pointed out the association between garments of glory and the resurrection or eternal life in
      early Jewish literature. 1 Enoch 62:15–16 reads: “The righteous and elect ones shall rise from the earth
      and shall cease being of downcast face. They shall wear garments of glory. These garments of yours
      shall become the garments of life from the Lord of the Spirits.” In 2 Enoch 22:8 the Lord tells Michael
      to “Go and extract Enoch from his earthly clothing” and “anoint him with my delightful oil, and put
      him in the clothes of my glory.” As a result he became “like one of the glorious ones” (22:10). This is
      the reverse of what happened at the fall. In the Apocalypse of Moses 20:1–5 Eve says that after she ate
      of the forbidden fruit, “my eyes were opened, and I knew that I was naked of the righteousness with
      which I had been clothed,” and recognizes, “I have been estranged from my glory with which I was
      clothed” (cf. Rom. 3:23). Kim seems to overlook the possibility that this motif may have found its
      source in Psalm 8 and its description of humanity being crowned with glory and honor. (Italics
      theirs.)

32
  The redemption of our body in Rom 8:23 occurs in relation to our misthological adoption as sons but is not a
misthological affirmation. Rom 8:23 is not saying that only overcomers will have redeemed bodies. All believers
will have redeemed bodies. Paul is affirming that at the time believers receive redeemed bodies via the resurrection,
the sons of God will experience their misthological adoption and transformation into glorified bodies. Those
believers who fail to qualify for misthological adoption will simply have redeemed/resurrected bodies. As heirs of
God, their unglorified redeemed bodies qualify them to live in the kingdom, not to inherit the kingdom.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                      Page 26

If Ciampa and Rosner are correct that Paul’s motif is informed by Ps 8:5, then we certainly should consult this
corresponding OT passage:

          •   4
                What is man, that Thou dost take thought of him? And the son of man, that Thou dost care
              for him? 5 Yet Thou hast made him a little lower than God, and dost crown him with glory
              and majesty! (Ps 8:4-5)

But if Paul has this OT passage in mind, we should consider its NT exposition:

          •   5
                For He did not subject to angels the world to come, concerning which we are speaking. 6 But
              one has testified somewhere, saying, “What is man, that Thou rememberest him? Or the son
              of man, that Thou art concerned about him?” 7 “Thou hast made him for a little while lower
              than the angels; Thou hast crowned him with glory and honor, and hast appointed him over
              the works of Thy hands; 8 Thou hast put all things in subjection under his feet.” For in
              subjecting all things to him, He left nothing that is not subject to him. But now we do not yet
              see all things subjected to him. 9 But we do see Him who has been made for a little while
              lower than the angels, namely, Jesus, because of the suffering of death crowned with glory
              and honor, that by the grace of God He might taste death for everyone. 10 For it was fitting for
              Him, for whom are all things, and through whom are all things, in bringing many sons to
              glory, to perfect the author of their salvation through sufferings. 11 For both He who sanctifies
              and those who are sanctified are all from one Father; for which reason He is not ashamed to
              call them brethren, 12 saying, “I will proclaim Thy name to My brethren, In the midst of the
              congregation I will sing Thy praise.” (Heb 2:5-12)

This passage is well-known in misthological circles as dealing with glory as a reward. The warnings of Hebrews
warn believers about missing out on all such rewards. If the OT forms the backdrop for Paul’s statement about being
raised in glory, then it would be exceedingly strange to think the glory he has in mind is not misthological. We
know that Paul is thinking in terms of OT thought about the resurrection since he quotes the OT for his finale in
1Cor 15:54-55 regarding victory over death. Therefore, we may reasonably pose that Paul has the OT background
in mind and expects the Corinthians to also since he quotes it in his appeal to them.
       The comments by Ciampa and Rosner (PNTC) on Paul’s contextual honor are also worth noting:

             When Paul says that the body is sown in dishonor, one is reminded of what he said earlier
      in this letter about his own experience of being dishonored: “You are honored, we are
      dishonored! To this very hour we go hungry and thirsty, we are in rags, we are brutally treated, we
      are homeless. We work hard with our own hands. When we are cursed, we bless; when we are
      persecuted, we endure it; when we are slandered, we answer kindly. We have become the scum of the
      earth, the garbage of the world—right up to this moment” (4:10–13; cf. 2 Cor. 6:8). Of course Paul’s
      personal experience followed the pattern established by Christ himself, whose passion consisted in
      large part of a series of events in which Christ was dishonored, whether by the religious or political
      authorities, by his own disciples, or by the others who were crucified beside him. Paul summarizes
      Christ’s experience in Philippians 2:7–8 as one of taking on “the very nature of a servant” and
      “becoming obedient to death—even death on a cross!” Paul’s catalogues of his sufferings (including
      the part just cited) suggest that he fully embraced the dishonor to which he was constantly
      exposed, knowing that it was the natural prologue to experiencing glory modeled after that into
      which Christ himself entered after his experience of dishonor (cf. Phil. 2:9–11). Of course the
      dishonor experienced by Christ, Paul, and every other human being is a result of Adam’s sin and the
      dishonor that sin brought on the whole human race and which is being overcome through Christ’s
      redemptive work. (Italics theirs.)

Did Paul envision the Corinthians who dishonored him as being honored by Christ with a glorious body at the
resurrection? Surely not. Not unless they repent and honor him would he expect Christ to honor them. If the dishonor
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                  Page 27
Paul suffered for Christ is a natural prologue to his experiencing the honor to which he refers, would it not be a
misthological honor? Though PNTC is not explicit, its comments require a misthological understanding of honor.
Its subsequent discussion leads to this impression as well:

      In 2 Corinthians 3:18 Paul says that we “contemplate the Lord’s glory” and “are being transformed
      into his image with ever-increasing glory.” In Colossians 1:27 Paul speaks of the mystery which is
      “Christ in you, the hope of glory.” In Romans 8:18 he says that “our present sufferings are not
      worth comparing with the glory that will be revealed in us.” In Romans 8:21 he declares that the
      creation itself will be “brought into the freedom and glory of the children of God.” Christ, the
      firstfruits, has already entered into glory via the resurrection. His people can look forward to
      experiencing glory through the resurrection as well.

        Based on its own quotation of Rom 8:18, the complete thought of the PNTC statement should be: “His people
can look forward to experiencing glory through the resurrection as well…if they suffer with Christ.” In any event,
this is Paul’s intent. This glory is contingent upon suffering. As Paul says in 2Cor 4:17, “For momentary, light
affliction is producing for us an eternal weight of glory far beyond all comparison.” No affliction, no glory for being
afflicted. If we refuse to suffer with Him, we should not expect to be glorified with Him. “We suffer with Him in
order that we may also be glorified with Him” (Rom 8:17b). The glory to which Paul refers in Rom 8:21 is the
glory associated with sufferings in Rom 8:18, which is a co-glory shared by those who co-suffer with Christ in
Rom 8:17b.33
        Even aside from all such considerations, a natural reading of 1Cor 15:43 in light of 1Cor 15:41 would be that
Paul is continuing his discussion about stellar glory in v. 41. Paul is applying the variation of glory among the
stars to the variation of those who are raised in glory at the resurrection. Since the glory in 1Cor 15:41 is
limited to those who shine like stars, per Dan 12:2-3 (etc.), the glory in 1Cor 15:43 would likewise be limited
to those believers who attain stellar glory. Paul is not asserting the glorification of those believers who fail to
shine like stars. Instead, he is insisting that those who shine like stars will be resurrected in glory. Assuming
that the glory of v. 43 applies to all believers runs counter to its context and the background of v. 41. Being raised
in glory is limited to those who shine like stars. The glory of v. 43 is the resurrected stellar glory of v. 41 applied to
the resurrection and thereby limited to those who will have the stellar glory of v. 41, namely, the sons of God
(overcomers).

33
  The online video that summarizes the material in the present book will help the reader visually see what is going
on in Rom 8:16-525. See https://youtu.be/YVPftMGwljk.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                        Page 28
       Paul explicitly supplies an all in 1Cor 15:51 and implicitly in several other places as well. Assuming that he
implies an all in all cases would be clearly wrong. When Paul says, “So also is the resurrection of the dead”
(1Cor 15:42), we naturally do not understand him as saying, “So also is the resurrection of all the dead.” An assumed
all should not be read into the verse. His statement does not apply to the unregenerate. We easily perceive him to
be talking about the resurrection of believers, not unbelievers. Yet some erroneously read Paul in v. 42 as if he says,
“So also is the resurrection of all the dead believers.” He does not say that either. We cannot assume, for example,
that he is affirming a glorified body for all believers in 1Cor 15:43 as a consequence of their resurrection in v. 42.
Likewise, when Paul says, “The dead will be raised imperishable (1Cor 15:52), he does not mean, “All the dead
will be raised imperishable.” He is only talking about all the dead in Christ (1Cor 15:22), not universalism, only
“those who are Christ’s at His coming” (1Cor 15:23). Similarly, even though Paul has been widely misconstrued as
using an indicative to say, “We shall also bear the image of the heavenly” (1Cor 15:49), he should not be further
misperceived as saying, if that were the case, “We shall all also bear the image of the heavenly.” As has been shown,
even if the indicative text is preferred, a subjunctive understanding could be entailed. That said, a subjunctive
reading is much more likely. Similarly, we should note that Paul does not say, “Thanks be to God, who gives all of
us the victory” (1Cor 15:57). One may reasonably pose, per his context and the OT background that he cites, which
will be discussed shortly,34 that the victory is only given to those believers who are victorious. Paul does not say
that we will all be changed equally. Per 1Cor 15:41, we know that he does not intend to imply equality at the
stellar level.
       Paul makes it very clear that all believers will be changed and have imperishable, immortal bodies at the
baseline level: “We shall all be changed…we shall be changed. For this perishable must put on the imperishable,
and this mortal must put on immortality” (1Cor 15:51-53). He also makes it clear that having a spiritual body is
only conditioned on having a natural body: “If there is a natural body, there is also a spiritual body” (1Cor 15:44).
Naturally, this statement applies to all believers. All believers have a natural body; ergo, all believers will have a
spiritual body. Therefore, Paul makes several affirmations that are applicable to all believers: They will all be
changed into imperishable, immortal, spiritual bodies. However, the assumption made by most, that the other
aspects of the resurrection within Paul’s discussion are applicable to all believers, is very presumptuous. It reads an
all into the text that is not there in several locations. We know for a fact that reading an all into v. 50 is erroneous
regarding the inheritance. Not all believers will inherit the kingdom. Only the faithful will inherit the kingdom, as
attested by numerous passages. Thus, we must not assume that Paul intends for all items to apply to all believers.

       A variety of other items in the list appear to be just as limited to the faithful as the inheritance. The analogy
of the celestial glory of the heavenly bodies being a misthological facet is strongly attested. Dishonor versus glory
