     See Misthological Victory in 1Cor 15:54-57, 30.
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                  Page 29
is also clearly a misthological theme. Since the unfaithful will still be subject to misthological death, absolute victory
over death, which is what Paul pictures in this context, must be a misthological affirmation. Although this
resurrection passage expresses a soteriological-misthological duality, the careful exegete should not find it
confusing, neither should the Corinthians, given the context of the epistle and the OT background to which Paul
appeals and alludes. TOD provided a chart of passages demonstrating where the assumed all is falsely read into the
text. Several texts from this Corinthian context should be added.
        In 1Cor 15:54, Paul quotes the part of Is 25:8 that affirms the swallowing up of death in victory as being
applicable when our bodies are changed via the resurrection. For church-age believers, this will be at the time of
the rapture. It would be presumptuous to think that this transformation will be for all the dead at this time. Not all
will be resurrected at this time. Unbelievers are not included. Believers from earlier dispensations will not be
resurrected until later. At a minimum, the victory over death affirmed in this passage is not universal. Those who
have this victory over death ≠ all the dead. Paying even more attention to the context, we must not assume that
Paul means all raptured saints experience this transformation at this time. Those raptured believers having this
victory over dead ≠ all saints. We need not assume that Paul means all church-age saints experience this victorious
transformation at any time. Unfaithful believers will never experience it. This victory is reserved for those believers
who are victorious.
        An online color-coded posting of an excerpt by G. H. Lang is excellent in arguing both for the subjunctive
in 1Cor 15:49 and limiting the all in 1Cor 15:51:

             It is often urged that this passage declares that though “we shall not all sleep,” but some be alive
      at the decent of the Lord, yet “we shall all be changed,” and surely, says the objector with
      emphasis, all means all. Truly; but in verse 22, “For as in Adam all die, so also in the Christ
      shall all be made alive,” “all” means all of mankind, for every child of Adam will at some time be
      raised by Christ (John 5:28,29). But not all at the first resurrection (Rev. 20:5). Therefore in this
      very chapter “all” means different things, and in verse 51 requires limiting, since it refers to a
      smaller company than in verse 22.
             The last and immediate context in verses 48,49, which speak of those who are to “bear the image
      of the heavenly,” that is, are to share with the Lord in His heavenly form, glory and sovereignty. Now
      the more difficult, and therefore the more probable reading here is in the Revised Version margin:
      “As we have borne the image of the earthly, let us also bear the image of the heavenly.” It is evident
      that one copying a document is not likely to insert by mistake a more difficult word or idea than is in
      the manuscript before him; so that, as a general rule, the more difficult reading is likely to have been
      the original reading. Moreover, in this case “let us also bear” is so well attested by the manuscripts as
      to have been adopted as the true reading by Lachmann, Tischendorf, Tregelles,
      Alford, and Westcott and Hort, and is given as the text in the latest editions of the Greek Testament,
      those of Nestle and Von Soden. Ellicott prefers the common reading, but on subjective and internal
      grounds only, and his remark on the external authority is emphatic: “It is impossible to deny that the
      subjunctive phoresomen is supported by very greatly preponderating authority.” Alford (on Romans
      9: 5) well says, “that no conjecture [i.e., as to the true Greek text] arising from doctrinal difficulty is
      ever to be admitted in the face of the consensus of MSS. and versions.” Weymouth gives the force
      well by the rendering “let us see to it that we also bear.”
             By this exhortation the apostle places upon Christians some responsibility to see that they
      secure that image of the heavenly which is indispensable to inheriting “the kingdom of
      God” (verse 50). In this Paul is supported by Peter, who also writes of that “inheritance which is
      reserved in heaven” (1 Pet. 1:4), which he describes by the latter statement that “the God of all grace
      called you unto His eternal glory in Christ” (v. 10). But Peter goes on to urge the called to “give
      diligence to make your calling and election sure” (2 Pet. 1:10), thus showing that this calling to
      share the glory of God has [by diligence on the part of the regenerate believer] to be made
      sure. He is not at all discussing justification by faith or suggesting that it must be made sure by works
      done after conversion. Justification [by faith] and eternal life are not in the least his
      subject. He writes expressly to those “who have [already] obtained like precious faith with us on the
      righteousness of our God and Saviour Jesus Christ” (2Pet. 1:1). The calling of grace is to share in
      God’s own eternal glory, or, as Paul expresses it, to share God’s “own kingdom and glory,” and he
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                        Page 30
      tells us that he exhorted, encouraged, yea, and testified, to the end that his children in faith
      should ”walk worthily of God” Who had called them to such supreme dignity (1 Thess. 2: 11, 12).
              Since therefore this most honourable calling must be “made sure” by “walking worthily,” in
      order that we may be “counted worthy of the kingdom of God, for which ye also suffer”
      (2 Thess. 1: 5), the reading “let us also bear the image of the heavenly” becomes consistent and
      important. Thus 1 Cor. 15: 41,52 is addressed to those who are assumed (whether it be so or not) to
      have responded to that exhortation, and it will mean that “we [who shall be accounted worthy to
      bear that heavenly image] shall not all sleep, but we shall all be changed.” Of that company it is
      strictly true that all means all….
              It has been accepted above that “all” means “all,” but what does “all” mean? It is not always
      used absolutely, in its universal sense. Thus the Lord, speaking of the last days of this age, said, “ye
      shall be hated of all men for My name’s sake” (Matt. 10: 22; Luke 21: 17); yet later, speaking of the
      same period, He showed that there will be then some, the “sheep,” who will befriend His persecuted
      followers (Matt. 24: 33-40). The explanation is found in the other report of His words: “ye shall be
      hated of all the nations” (Matt. 24: 10); that is, the hatred will affect all the peoples everywhere on
      earth, though not every individual as the other use of “all” might by itself suggest.
              Again; of the trial of Christ before the Council of the Jews it is said that “all the chief priests
      and the elders of the people took counsel (sumboulion) against Jesus” (Matt. 27:1); yet Luke
      23:50 tells that one of the Council, Joseph of Arimathea (a bouletees), had not assented to their
      counsel (boulee); and John 19:39 shows that Nicodemus dissociated himself from their act; and he
      also was one of the Council (John 7:50-52). Acts 1:1 speaks of Luke’s Gospel having narrated
      “all that Jesus began both to do and to teach,” yet we know that the world could not contain the
      books that would be required for such a full account (John 21: 25).
              These instances suffice to warn against rashly taking “all” in its fullest sense. They call for
      careful consideration of each use of the word. The [Holy] Spirit took up the natural habits of
      human speech! No one is misled when he hears one say that “all the world was there.”35 (All
      emphasis original except for the yellow highlighting which I added.)

        Lang, an outstanding misthologist, does a superb job of showing the superiority of the subjunctive textually
and contextually. Moreover, he provides cross-references from Peter to support his conclusion that Paul is exhorting
us to bear the image of the heavenly so that we can inherit the kingdom. Indeed, a book-long defense could be given
for that proposition. Since he and I and other misthologists have written such defenses, I will pass over adding any
further collaborating arguments in the present discussion to confirm his point, other than to note the obvious point
from Paul’s context. Paul makes it clear in this immediate context that we must have heavenly bodies that exhibit
all the characteristics he is describing in order for us to inherit the kingdom. A perishable body cannot inherit an
imperishable kingdom. A body subject to death cannot inherit the kingdom. We must have a resurrected
body that has completely conquered death in order to inherit the kingdom. Only we who have born the
heavenly image morally will attain this complete victory over death. Therefore, the all of 1Cor 15:51 does not
mean that all believers will attain this victory and inherit the kingdom. The fullest meaning of the all is restricted
to those believers who attain victory over all forms of death and thereby qualify for inheriting the kingdom. A closer
look at the victory over death is warranted and supports Lang’s position.
                                      Misthological Victory in 1Cor 15:54-57
        Previously, I have allowed the possibility that Paul might be focusing on a limited application of Is 25:8 in
1Cor 15:54-57, by making it applicable in a limited sense to all transformed believers, so that Paul is affirming that
(1) all believers will have some degree of victory over death but (2) faithful believers experience full victory over
death. That possibility is not a probability, however. Even if the possibility were conceded that Paul intends to
affirm a limited victory over death for all believers, compelling arguments refuse to allow concession to the notion
that all believers shine in glory. In any event, the more likely inference and the greater exegetically satisfying
deduction is that Paul intends to limit the victory over death to victorious believers. Overcomers inherit this
promise. Paul is not talking about the soteriological victory over death that all believers receive by grace apart from

35
   G. H. Lang, “‘We shall not all sleep, but we shall all be changed,’ (1Cor. 15: 51). Available at:
http://themillennialkingdom.org.uk/AnImportantText_2.htm. Accessed 12/24/2020.
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                               Page 31
works but about the misthological victory over death that faithful believers earn by grace that works in conforming
to the morality of our heavenly calling.
       As noted in LTPW, in Revelation, John derives the intended application of Is 25:8 from its OT background
by litotetically limiting its applicability to overcomers. Isaiah says, “He will swallow up death for all time, and the
Lord God will wipe tears away from all faces, and He will remove the reproach of His people from all the earth”
(Is 25:8). John affirms, “And He shall wipe away every tear from their eyes; and there shall no longer be any death;
there shall no longer be any mourning, or crying, or pain; the first things have passed away…He who overcomes
will inherit these things” (Rev 21:4,7). By limiting the scope of Is 25:8 to apply only to overcomers, that is, to
those who inherit the kingdom, John deepens our perspective of the truth being presented by Isaiah. In
Is 30:19, those who dwell in Zion are the ones who will weep no more. The promise is for city dwellers, not for
mere kingdom inhabitants. Is 35:10 promises eternal joy, no sorrows, for those in Zion (cf. Is 51:11). Is 57:18
contextually promises comfort for those who respond to His chastisement, not for those who continue to walk in
wickedness (cf. Is 57:20-21; 59:20). The promise is for those who take refuge in the Lord and who will thus
inherit the land and possess His holy mountain, that is, Mount Zion (Is 57:13). The NAS expresses it this way:
“He who takes refuge in Me will inherit the land and will possess My holy mountain.” Conversely, the NKJV
transposes the word order: “He who puts his trust in Me shall possess the land, and shall inherit My holy mountain.”
It makes little difference. The two words are synonymous and, therefore, may be translated interchangeably. Those
who inherit the land will be the ones who possess the City and inherit the kingdom. Overcomers (i.e., bridal
saints) will possess dwelling places in the Bridal City (Jn 14:2-3). Subcomers will not.
       The most straightforward way to harmonize Paul with Isaiah and John is to conclude that Paul, like the other
two writers, intends to limit this overwhelming victory over death for those who dwell in Zion. Paul does not
intend to apply it to those believers who fail to inherit the kingdom. Therefore, as a result of the present study, I
amend my previous statement in MMP1 so as to now infer that not all raptured believers will receive glorified
bodies raised in glory (1Cor 15:43). Only overcomers receive glorified bodies that bear the image of the
heavenly glory (1Cor 15:40,49). Such bodies are limited to those believers who overcome. Like diamonds, these
glorified bodies are imperishable (1Cor 15:52-54). In this very passage, Paul implies misthological distinctions
when he reminds these believers that star differs from star in glory, alluding to a well-known misthological passage
in Dan 12:2-3. Immortal bodies are necessary to inherit the kingdom (1Cor 15:50), but so is morality (1Cor 6:9).
The injunction to morality in 1Cor 15:58, therefore, which concludes this promise of glorified bodies for overcomers
in 1Cor 15:40-58, makes sense when one recalls, from the previous context, that not all believers will have the glory
of inheriting the kingdom. Consequently, not all believers are luminous, to speak in terms of Paul’s stellar imagery.
Having a glorified spiritual body is a reward given to faithful believers at the time of their transformation
because they alone are changed into stellar bodies. Not all believers will have a body that is luminous and
which qualifies them as heirs of the kingdom. All believers get a spiritual body, but only overcomers get a
glorified body. Just as all members of Christ are members of His body, but not all members of His body are
members of His bridal body, so not all believers have the same body. Having a glorified body that qualifies bridal
saints for kingdom rulership is a reward.
       To expound upon a statement in MMP2, only for victorious believers is misthological death “swallowed up
in victory” when believers take on immortal bodies (1Cor 15:54). “Therefore, my beloved brethren, be steadfast,
immovable, always abounding in the work of the Lord, knowing that your toil is not in vain in the Lord”
(1Cor 15:58). Why work? Because your misthological immortality is at stake. Those believers who do not achieve
misthological immortality will be subject to misthological mortality for eternity. Misthological death is eternal. All
believers will have soteriological immortality (1Cor 15:53-58). But only those believers who overcome will have
this foundation of soteriological immortality expand into misthological immortality that completely overcomes
death (1Cor 15:54-58).
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                         Page 32
                                 Illustration 4. Redeemed Versus Glorified Bodies




       To tweak a statement and illustration in MMP3, I will surmise that the transformation of unfaithful believers
will be an occasion for their shame. Something about the nature of their transformation will shame them. Possibly,
they will experience a Heimlich-like transformation, which I adapted from Martha Lever’s book, Morphie the
Caterpillar. The Disney movie, A Bug’s Life, readily suggests an illustration that can be used for children. In the
movie, Heimlich, a large gluttonous caterpillar, dreams of becoming a beautiful butterfly. At the end of the movie,
however, when he undergoes his transformation, he emerges still very much a caterpillar, with a tiny pair of wings,
much too small for his body. He needs the assistance of others to fly.
       Lever’s illustration is splendid because, biblically, all believers will not undergo a transformation into
glorified bodies, even by the time they reach the eternal state. In Jesus’ post-resurrection appearances, He looked
like a normal, untransformed human being even though He had a resurrected body. One may posit, then, that
subcomers will still look like caterpillars even after they receive their resurrected bodies. That is, those believers
cast into the outer darkness will be in transformed bodies that are incapable of physical death—what we technically
would call spiritual bodies—even though in appearance their bodies will appear to be normal flesh and blood bodies.
They will no longer actually be in flesh and blood bodies, but for all appearances, it will appear as if they are still
in mortal bodies. In other words, Lever’s illustration is accurate, just the way it is, in leaving subcomers
pictured as caterpillars, even if for all eternity. Subcomers may not appear to have immortal bodies though in
fact they do have immortal bodies. Their immortal bodies may look perfectly normal, not old or frail, to be sure,
but perfectly normal. Rather than being transformed into beautiful butterflies, their spiritual bodies will appear to
be very much like normal bodies. They will not be old, frail, or crippled, of course. But they will not be able to
radiate the beauty of their Lord’s glory in their transformed form since they lack glorified bodies that are limited
to those who have lived faithfully and thus have a super-transformed form.
       An abbreviated extract from RAE provides a summation: The trumpet sound and imperishable transformation
for the church takes place at the rapture. But notice where that puts the swallowing up at death—at the point of the
rapture: then is when. Because this complete victory over death is given at the point of rapture, it must be
limited, at least in its full aspect, to those believers who are victorious, that is, to overcomers. The victorious
swallowing up of death, as anticipated in this passage, cannot possibly include unfaithful believers. To be
sure, they will have imperishable bodies that are not subject to physical death. But Paul is envisioning something
much more than mere victory over physical death. In this immediate context, he is discussing the necessity of having
imperishable bodies so that one can inherit the kingdom (1Cor 15:50). Having an imperishable body is necessary
to wear an imperishable crown, and Paul already had discussed the necessity of running victoriously in order to win
this crown earlier within this same epistle (1Cor 9:24-27), and he warned the Corinthians that they must live
righteously if they were going to inherit the kingdom (1Cor 6:9-10). Consistent with this misthological focus, Paul
concludes ch. 15 with this admonition: “Thanks be to God, who gives us the victory through our Lord Jesus Christ.
Therefore, my beloved brethren, be steadfast, immovable, always abounding in the work of the Lord, knowing that
your toil [labor] is not in vain in the Lord” (1Cor 15:57-58).36 Verse 58 demands that verse 57 be understood as
holding forth the possibility of reward: This victory over death is given as a reward to those believers who

      6

       Misthologists recognize that this lavish banquet is a reward experienced during the millennial kingdom. It
would be better to say that it is inaugurated in the millennial kingdom.38 Paul sees the swallowing up of death for
all time (millennial kingdom and eternal kingdom), at the misthological level at least, which is his focus, as a reward.
His doing so is consistent with the OT background, for Isaiah also considers the swallowing up of death a reward,
limiting it, like the banquet, to those who live on this mountain (a.k.a., the Mountain City). In short, residents
of this Reward City (i.e., Mount Zion) will qualify for this reward. And residency in this City is limited to the
faithful. The wiping away of tears is also a reward promised to all residents in this Reward City. Since it is a
reward, it does not apply to unfaithful believers. John pictures this wiping away of tears as a reward, limiting it
to those faithful believers who wash their robes and make them white (Rev 7:14-17).39 In Rev 21:1-7, John also
depicts this wiping away of tears and the complete absence of any form of death as rewards limited to overcomers
(nikao).40 Overcomers are believers who are victorious. He associates these rewards with New Jerusalem (a.k.a.,
the Reward City, Mount Zion, Heb 12:22). “These things,” as John calls them, are inherited by the overcomers, not
by subcomers (Rev 21:7). Victory over death is one of the misthological benefits given/rewarded to those
believers who are victorious. The inaugurated nature of these rewards is confirmed by John, who affirms that the
wiping away of tears is a reward that starts long before the millennial kingdom even commences (Rev 7:17). Clearly,
Isaiah, Paul, and John have their eyes fixated upon the misthological nature of these benefits, and they see the
experience of these rewards as beginning before the end of the millennial kingdom and retaining their misthological
nature at the conclusion of the millennial kingdom and therefore into eternity. The end of the millennial kingdom
provides a suitable climax, not a conclusion, for the focus of these rewards, a climax anyway to the extent that we
are able to fathom them readily presently. These rewards will be even more precious and rare in eternity as humanity

   Since both Paul and John draw upon the same OT background for their conclusions, we do not have to dig into

victory over death in 1Cor 15:54-55,57. What Paul makes implicit in 1Corinthians is made explicit by John in
Revelation. Victory (nikos) over misthological death is only inherited by those believers who overcome (nikao).

being hurt misthologically by the second death (cp. Rev 2:11). According to Paul’s 1Cor 15:50-57, those believers
who have victory over death inherit the kingdom. Yet, according to Paul’s 1Cor 6:9-10 (etc.), not all believers
inherit the kingdom. We necessarily must deduce either that (1) not all believers have the victory over death
to which Paul refers or (2) they do not have the same type/degree of victory. Consequently, the victory of
some believers over death will be limited to having redeemed, imperishable, immortal, spiritual bodies.
Contrastively, the victory of faithful believers will be complete, replete with the inheritance of the kingdom
in glorified bodies.

       Even this passage from Isaiah (25:6-8), when properly understood, takes us up to the threshold of eternity
and holds forth the promises of no death and no tears as rewards during the eternal state. Indeed, Isaiah peers across
the threshold and says, “Instead of your shame you will have a double portion, and instead of humiliation they will
shout for joy over their portion. Therefore they will possess a double portion in their land, everlasting joy will be
theirs” (Is 61:7). A double portion is a misthological benefit limited to the faithful. They will have everlasting joy
over their double portion. Consequently, their reward must be eternal.
                                      Misthological Exhortation in 1Cor 15:58
        Commentators who reject a misthological understanding of 1Cor 15:49 tend to reject a misthological
understanding of 1Cor 15:41. Calvin is such an example, and those adopting this approach will sometimes cite him
in support. However, he is a poor role model of hermeneutical consistency within this passage. By rightly
acknowledging that 1Cor 15:58 requires the pursuit of reward while denying that the context deals with rewards,
Calvin places himself in an unnecessary quandary shared by others such as Garland.
        In his rejection of the subjunctive, Garland cites Robertson in noting that what is at stake, in the case of a
subjunctive, is obtaining a glorified body: “‘Robertson and Plummer (1914: 375) recognize that the subjunctive
implies that ‘the attaining to the glorified body depends upon on our own effort.’ Nothing could be more wrong.
Internal evidence must outweigh the external evidence. The context is neither polemical nor hortatory, but didactic”
(BECNT, s.v. 1Cor 15:49).
        Contrariwise, as already noted, Robertson’s objection to the subjunctive was flawed because he misconstrued
attaining a glorified body with receiving a resurrected body. One must consider the probable distinction between
the two, which Garland fails to do. Having a resurrected body at the time of the Lord’s return will be a gift. Having
a resurrected body that shines with glory will be a reward. Garland should have considered this possibility since he
acknowledges, “Mentioning the stars might be intended to bring to mind the glorious state of those resurrected
from the dead, who are likened to bodies (Dan. 12:2–3; Matt. 13:43; Wis. 3:7; 2 Bar. 51:10; so D. Martin 1995:
118–20)” (s.v. 1Cor 15:39-41). Garland is refuted by the cross-references he cites. This glorious state is conditioned
on being experientially righteous in those texts. Consider the context of 2 Bar 51:10, for example, which Garland
cites in support:
      7
        Miracles, however, will appear at their own time to those who are saved because of their works
      and for whom the Law is now a hope, and intelligence, expectation, and wisdom a trust. 8 For they
      shall see that world which is now invisible to them, and they will see a time which is now hidden to
      them. 9* And time will no longer make them older. 10 For they will live in the heights of that world
      and they will be like the angels and be equal to the stars. And they will be changed into any shape
      which they wished, from beauty to loveliness, and from light to the splendor of glory. 11* For the
      extents of Paradise will be spread out for them, and to them will be shown the beauty of the majesty
      of the living beings under the throne, as well as all the hosts of the angels, those who are held by my
      word now lest they show themselves, and those who are withheld by my command so that they may
      stand at their places until their coming has arrived. 12 And the excellence of the righteous will then
      be greater than that of the angels.41 (2Bar 51:7-12)

This misthological salvation by works is associated with shining like the stars and being more glorious than the
angels. This misthological reward is reserved for the righteous. Obviously, an imputational righteousness apart from
works is not in view. Likewise, when Jesus says, “Then the righteous will shine forth as the sun in the kingdom of
their Father” (Mt 13:43), He is referring to those believers who are experientially righteous. Likewise, Daniel
conditions this starlike glory on insight and performance: “And those who have insight will shine brightly like the
brightness of the expanse of heaven, and those who lead the many to righteousness, like the stars forever and ever”
(Dan 12:3). This glorious state is conditioned on being experientially righteous in the very texts and contexts to
which Garland appeals.
       Additionally, Garland shortchanges his own appeal to the Corinthian context. His own contextual argument
concedes that the passage concludes with the promise that God “will give the laborer the due reward in the end”

41
  J. H. Charlesworth, The Old Testament Pseudepigrapha, vol. 1 (New York; London: Yale University Press,
1983), 638.
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                Page 35
(s.v. 1Cor 15:58). Rewards are embedded in the context. At the very least, a hybrid view is necessary,
acknowledging that 1Cor 15:41 deals with rewards, even if 1Cor 15:49 does not.
       A consistent duality is preferable to the hybrid view, though. Verse 41 deals primarily with the distinction
between terrestrial and celestial bodies and secondarily implies misthological distinctions—a
physical/misthological duality. Verse 49 exhorts us to bear the image of Christ morally now and promises us that,
if we do so, we will bear it misthologically in the future—a temporal/eschatological duality. Verse 50 focuses on
the necessity, but not sufficiency, of an immortal body to inherit the kingdom. This spiritual body must also bear
the celestial image in radiant splendor if it is to qualify for inheriting the kingdom—a spiritual/celestial duality.
Verse 58 presumes both the historical and eschatological validity of the resurrection and the misthological integrity
of the believer’s work—a historical/eschatological duality.
       The type of body that Paul describes as inheriting the kingdom is a resurrected body that has been transformed
so that it is: imperishable, glorious, honorable, powerful, spiritual, heavenly, immortal, and victorious. Some of
these eight attributes are affirmed for all resurrected believers. Namely, all believers will have resurrected bodies
that are: imperishable, spiritual, and immortal. Thus, three of these eight affirmations apply to all believers. Five do
not. Not all believers will inherit the kingdom. Accordingly, they will not be misthologically victorious over death
but are put to shame instead. The resurrected bodies of the unfaithful will not be honorable. Due to the implications
of the context and the background of the passages regarding glory, we can also safely infer that their bodies will
not radiate with celestial glory. Therefore, some of the potential attributes of our resurrected bodies are limited to
believers who morally bear the image of Christ. Namely, the resurrected bodies of the faithful will have the
additional attributes listed in the context: glorious, honorable, powerful, heavenly, and victorious. Our behavior as
believers determines if we will have glorified bodies that are suitable for inheriting the kingdom. Only
misthologically glorified believers will have glorious bodies that shine with radiance befitting inheriting the
kingdom. We can summarize with extracts from Paul’s illustration, hortation, and exhortation:

                                    Star differs from star in glory (1Cor 15:41)….
                              Let us also bear the image of the heavenly (1Cor 15:49)….
          Therefore, my beloved brethren, be steadfast, immovable, always abounding in the work of the Lord,
                         knowing that your toil is not in vain in the Lord (1Cor 15:58).
                                                    Conclusion
       Paul uses analogies to make his argument, and I will use an analogy to summarize his analogies. Consider a
building with three basic components: a foundation, structure, and roof. Paul spells out the foundational aspect of
his analogy. No one can miss it. Notwithstanding, the OT background for his analogy requires an inescapable
primary implication built directly on that foundation. Yet many miss or deny this implication, even though it is the
immediate implication of his position. This primary implication culminates in a secondary implication built upon
the primary implication. Even fewer see this secondary implication.
                                      Illustration 5. Three-Tiered Summation
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                        Page 36

       The foundational component of Paul’s argument is the validity and necessity of the resurrection. You cannot
help but see this bedrock part of his argument. All commentators acknowledge that Paul is providing an defense for
the resurrection. Amazingly, many modern commentators think that Paul is simply making an affirmation of the
resurrection. They deny any further implication is intended. Appreciably, though, many commentators, both ancient
and even modern, perceive that Paul implies a distinction in degrees of rewards with his analogy of stars differing
from stars in glory (1Cor 15:41). Additionally, Paul is stressing the necessity of the resurrection for inheriting the
kingdom (1Cor 15:50), and inheriting the kingdom is a reward concept. Some interpreters among this group of
commendable commentators acknowledge that, in keeping with his misthological implication, Paul uses a
subjunctive exhortation (1Cor 15:49). A few interpreters follow through on the implication of Paul’s affirmation
about degrees of reward to perceive that he is making these rewards exclusive to those victorious believers who
earn these rewards.42
       The inheritance of the kingdom is obviously a reward limited to overcomers, as anyone taking a misthological
view of inheriting the kingdom can see. The misthological implication of 1Cor 15:50 is limited to overcomers. They
alone inherit the kingdom. But inheriting the kingdom is not the only reward limited to overcomers in the context.
Having glory like the stars is another affirmation that is limited to overcomers. Some interpreters taking a
misthological view of the stellar glory, unfortunately, miss the fact that it is limited to overcomers. They wrongly
think that the dimmer stars refer to believers at the lower end of the reward spectrum. Not so! Unfaithful believers
who are cast into the outer darkness are at the lowest end of the misthological spectrum. The OT background, as
well as associated NT texts, makes it impossible to include unfaithful believers in the stellar range of the
misthological spectrum. Paul is dealing with the visible misthological spectrum, denoted by the variation in
starlight. He is not dealing with a conceivable invisible glory that unfaithful believers might be conjectured to have.
They are excluded from the visible stellar realm of glory. The misthological implication of 1Cor 15:41 is limited to
overcomers. They alone have visible stellar glory. Any misthologist worth his salt should be able to make that
simple deduction.
       The real debate among misthologists is in 1Cor 15:43. Some experts in misthology consider it a soteriological
degree of glory, separate from the stellar glory of v. 41. Indeed, until this study, I was in that camp. The transition
from dishonor to glory in 1Cor 15:43 is better taken, however, as having misthological implications. V. 43 should
be recognized as the application of stellar glory to the resurrection rather than (1) disassociated with the
misthological degree of stellar glory in v. 41 or (2) seen as outside that spectrum of glory or (3) seen as foundational
to that as a higher degree of glory. Transition from dishonor to glory in v. 43 is misthological, not accidental or
merely physical. V. 41 limits the glory of v. 43 to overcomers, and v. 43 provides the timing of the glorification of
overcomers as being the resurrection (just as Paul does in Rom 8:17b-25). Only overcomers have the dishonor-to-
glory transition in v. 43. Other rewards in the context are likewise limited to overcomers, most notably,
misthological victory over death. The pinnacle of Paul’s misthological implication leads to the conclusion that the
wide variety of rewards embedded in this passage is limited to overcomers. Having a glorified body is but one of
them.
       Paul’s discussion and exhortations comprise three levels. At the foundational level, he broadly affirms the
necessity of the resurrection. At the first implicational level, he implies the necessity of the resurrection for
misthological realization. At the second implication level, he limits this misthological culmination exclusively to
those believers who qualify for it by heeding his injunctions to live morally and work diligently. Full misthological
realization requires a resurrection for its culmination.




42
  Some commentators acknowledge the subjunctive as superior but fail to proceed to acknowledge its misthological
implications. Gardner (ZECNT), for example, falls short of such implications and simply surmises: “Paul is asking
the Corinthians to begin to prepare for what will come.…Christians are asked to become Christlike in this age, even
though their bodies and the perfection of the Christlikeness still lies in the future at ‘the end.’” Why prepare if this
glorious perfection is going to happen unconditionally and inevitably, even if you do not prepare? What is missing
is Gardner’s assessment that Paul is exhorting them to prepare morally for such glory because such glory is
contingent upon their present preparation. Gardner should have concluded that Paul is exhorting them to prepare
now morally because such perfection, in the end, will be a reward based on their present preparation.
Star Differs from Star in Glory                                                                                Page 37
                                  Illustration 6. Stary Butterflies Versus Caterpillars




        To recap, using a combined analogy, Paul intends for the differentiation of the stars in glory to illustrate
the variation in stellar glory that will occur for those believers who attain stellar glory. He is not insinuating
that all believers will attain such glory. Rather, the resurrection is necessary for overcomers to have such glory and
rule throughout the universe/multiverse. This physical necessity of an eschatological resurrected body does not
nullify the misthological necessity of temporal moral conformity. To have the glory of a star, one must be a star.
To have the resurrected glory associated with overcomers, one must be an overcomer. Just as stars are unique, so
resurrected overcomers will be unique as well. The resurrected bridal overcomers will vary in glory/radiance
amongst themselves and retain their individuality. They will not form a heavenly Borg but a harmonious society
of aristocracy. If we were to zoom in on each winged queen, we would see each one has a distinctive set of wings,
signifying Paul’s variation in glory.
        Those believers who do not qualify for stellar glory, on the other hand, will be excluded from this stellar
realm. Jesus used parables to picture this exclusion as being cast into the outer darkness (Mt 8:12; 22:13; 25:30).
This spatial restriction is not merely parabolic, however, in that Jesus introduced the concept with didactic material
(Mt 8:12). With his analogies, didactic material, and exhortations, Paul has implied this misthological exclusion as
well. By calling upon believers to live morally so that they might win glorified heavenly bodies, he indicates that
those believers who fail to live morally will fail to attain this heavenly image associated with stellar glory. Speaking
analogously, we are left to imagine unglorified believers as diminutively winged caterpillars. When such believers
are initially cast into the outer darkness, they will weep, just as Jesus said, “In that place there shall be weeping.”
Nevertheless, we should not infer that they will weep unceasingly throughout eternity. More reasonably, if we were
to zoom in on their faces in eternity, we would probably only see an occasional tear run down the face of a caterpillar
here and there.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies   Page 38
                           Appendix 1: Variations in Glory
       I posted an online video summarizing the foregoing material in this book before releasing it.43 A viewer
responded with some musings leading to the clarification in this appendix. Just as there are hierarchies of angels,
so there will be hierarchies of glorified believers. Some believers will be more glorious than other believers. Some
of those having near death experiences (NDEs) and claiming to have visited New Jerusalem report that the City is
divided into numerous strata and three primary sections. The higher up you live in the Heavenly City, the greater
degree of glory you radiate. When those saints having a higher degree of glory visit saints who live in the lower
levels, they must tone down the degree of glory they manifest to accommodate those in the lower levels.
Accordingly, those saints living on one level would have basically the same degree of glory relative to one another
and more or less glory than those living in strata above or below them, respectively.
                                    Illustration 7. Horizontal and Vertical Glory




       In the above illustration, the size of the butterfly queens has been modified to represent the stratification of
glory. The butterfly queens at the top are larger than the ones below them. Several strata are represented by this
vertical variation in size to represent the differentiation in degree of glorious radiance in the various hierarchies of
these heavenly beings. Each butterfly queen will be a member of the Bride, but not all members of the Bridal body
will have the same degree of glory. Some overcomers will be more glorious than others. Overcomers at a higher
stratum will be more glorious than overcomers at a lower stratum. The more glorious overcomers will live in the
higher tiers of the Heavenly City.
       On the other hand, if one looks at the loosely arranged rows, the butterfly queens horizontally are roughly
the same size. This horizontal similarity in size denotes having the same relative degree of glory for their stratum.
Nevertheless, each butterfly queen has a unique pair of wings. Even relative to one another on the same stratum,
they have a unique stellar identity.

43
     See https://youtu.be/YVPftMGwljk.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                        Page 40
       If Paul means to suggest an additional differentiation in degrees of glory relative to the sun and moon, my
speculation is that the sun represents God’s glory, and the stars represent the glory of overcoming believers since
they share His stellar nature and radiate the glory of that shared nature from within themselves. In contrast, since
the moon does not share this stellar nature but only reflects it, its lunar glory might depict the glory of the holy
angels as being less than that of the sun and stars. Stellar glory is greater than lunar glory. Likewise, the Bride will
be a higher order of elohim than the angels and, therefore, more glorious (see MMP5).
                                  Illustration 8. The glory of Sun, Moon, and Stars




      Chrysostom sees the matter differently: “Thou canst not be a sun? then be a moon. Thou canst not be a moon?
then be a star. Thou canst not be a large star? be content to be a little one if only thou art in the Heaven.”44 In his

44
     John Chrysostom, Saint Chrysostom: On the Priesthood, Ascetic Treatises, Select Homilies and Letters, Homilies
Appendix 1. Variations in Glory                                                                               Page 41
opinion, since the moon is brighter than distance stars, we should aspire to be the moon rather than stars. My
assessment differs in that mine is based on (1) the unbalanced nature of Paul’s chiasm, which equates the variation
in stellar glory with that of the overcomers in juxtaposition to the singular solar glory and singular lunar glory of
the other two heavenly bodies in contradistinction to the unmentioned subvariation of fleshly bodies,45 (2) the shared
nature of the sun and stars versus that of the moon, (3) the realization that the stars are actually brighter than the
moon, and (4) the deduction that in the end overcomers will outshine the angels. The latter point can be inferred
from passages such as Heb 2:5-10 (see MMP4). As to the second and third points, the objection will be raised that
Paul did not realize that the sun is a star; therefore, we cannot read modern science into his intention. Contrarily, I
contend that Paul had passed through our universe to reach the third heaven in his NDE (near death experience) and
was aware that our sun is a star. Others having NDEs have reported passing through our universe on their way to
New Jerusalem (see MMP4). So, we need not rule out that NDE possibility for Paul. Additionally, note that Paul
does not say that stars differ from the sun and moon in glory. This difference is left unstated as merely implicit. His
explicit focus is instead on the variation in glory between the stars: for star differs from star in glory.
        If Paul intends a secondary misthological contrast between the glory of the stars with that of the moon and
sun, which is intuitively plausible in consideration of his unbalanced chiasm, then he may be indicating that the
moon is mere a reflective glory and smaller in size than that the sun and stars. He could reason that, despite present
earthly appearances, just as the stars are actually brighter than the moon, so overcomers will be brighter than angels
eventually. He would have known that the moon only appears brighter than the stars because it is closer. If you go
out far enough in space, then, despite the present earthly vantage point, you realize that the stars are brighter and
bigger than the moon. Paul, like other NDErs, made that trip through space via a NDE. Paul told the Corinthians
about his NDE but informed them that he was not permitted to give the details of the trip (2Cor 12:2-4).
Nevertheless, via that trip, he had seen the variation of stellar glory up-close and personal. Per Scripture, he also
knew that if you travel far enough out in time, namely, to the time of the resurrection, you will find that those
believers who have glorified bodies will be brighter than the angels.
        Paul could have put two and two together to conclude that further out in time, overcomers will outshine the
angels just as traveling far enough out in space enables one to see that the stars vastly outshine the moon. Plus, he
knew that the stars share the nature of the sun. Yet, because of the gag order not to reveal the details of his NDE
and because of the limited knowledge of the universe on the part of the Corinthians, he could not go into that much
detail. He could only drop an implicit hint by noting the difference between the sun, moon, and stars in glory, while
explicitly focusing on the fact that the stars vary in glory amongst themselves in his unbalanced chiasm. He knew
that this stellar glory would have been picked up on by the Corinthians as applying to overcomers via the OT
background: “And those who have insight will shine brightly like the brightness of the expanse of heaven, and
those who lead the many to righteousness, like the stars forever and ever” (Dan 12:3). Jesus associated this glory
of the overcomers as stars with that of the sun: “Then the righteous will shine forth as the sun in the kingdom of
their Father” (Mt 13:43). Paul could leave it up to his discerning readers to deduce that their victorious glory as
stars is linked with that of the sun. In light of all such considerations, Chrysostom’s question should be amended to
ask instead, Why would you want to shine like the mere moon when, as sons, you can shine like the sun, as stars
throughout the vast expanse of heaven and rule the entire kingdom of heaven?

                                                   Introduction
       OT believers believed in the physical resurrection (Job 19:25-26; Ps 16:9-11; 17:15; 23:6; 49:15; 73:24;
Is 25:8; 26:19; Dan 12:2,13; cp. Eze 37:10). It was believed to be a time of positive reward for the faithful, punitive
reward for the unfaithful. The faithful would be rewarded with the Reward City as their celestial reward and the
Earth as their terrestrial reward. As discussed in MMP5, this distinction can be traced back at least as far as the
beginning of the Jewish race in Gen 22:17 with the call of Abraham.46
                            Illustration 10. Two Types of Collective Seed in Gen 22:17




The reward for the resurrected pertains to entrance into the Heavenly City versus being excluded from the City.
The reward for the rescued pertains to entrance into the Promised Land versus exclusion from it. Chitwood
mistakenly thinks that “the Old Testament saints will be raised from the dead at this time in order that those who
died out of the land can return back to the land with the living (Ex. 12:41; 13:19; Dan. 12:1, 2).”47 Not so! Saints
who are experiencing a celestial resurrection shine like stars in the Heavenly City; they are not brought down to
Earth to live there. Pentecost’s assessment of Dan 12:1-3 is superior:

             It would thus be concluded that it is the consistent teaching of Scripture that the Lord will gather
      unto Himself in the eternal city the unfallen angels, the Old Testament saints, and the New
      Testament believers, where they, in resurrected glorified bodies, will share in the literal city and
      its glory, into which place they can only enter by resurrection. It should be noted that this

      heavenly Jerusalem is not the sphere of the living saved who go into the millennium, for they will
      look to the rebuilt earthly Jerusalem as their capital city, but rather the dwelling place of the

      promises of the Old Testament in the millennium, while the resurrected will realize the fulfillment of

The heavenly realm, with Heavenly Jerusalem as its capital, is for resurrected immortals. All those resurrected
with glorified bodies will reside in the gloriously celestial Jerusalem. The earthly realm, on the other hand, with
rebuilt earthly Jerusalem as its capital, is for rescued mortals. Daniel provides information concerning that event.
He uses this same Hebrew word olam to describe the eternal misthological shame of unfaithful OT believers in
juxtaposition to the eternal glory of the faithful.
            1
            Now at that time [“the end of time,”11:40, just before the end of the tribulation] Michael, the
      great prince who stands guard over the sons of your people [the Jews], will arise. And there will be a
      time of distress such as never occurred since there was a nation until that time; and at that time your
      people [the Jews], everyone who is found written in the book [of life], will be rescued. 2 And many
      of those who sleep in the dust of the ground will awake [be raised/resurrected], some to
      everlasting life, but others to disgrace and everlasting shame. 3 And those who have insight will
      shine brightly like the brightness of the expanse of heaven, and those who instruct the many in
      righteousness, will shine like the stars forever and ever. (Dan12:1-3; TM)
                                                    Simple Soteriology
        Three basic options for interpreting this Danielic passage are generally posed. Non-dispensationalists say
that it refers to the general resurrection of all the dead (believers and unbelievers) at the end of time to stand before
the Great White Throne Judgment of Rev 20:11-15. Thus, in their opinion, the texts in both Revelation and Daniel
are simply dealing with a singular soteriological judgment.
                                Illustration 11. Daniel Only Sees One Resurrection




Jackson appeals to the grammatical structure of Dan 12:2 in his argument for a singular resurrection:

      Some” will be raised to enjoy “everlasting life,” and “some” are destined for “shame and everlasting
      contempt.” The dual use of the demonstrative pronoun, ’eleh [elleh], (rendered “some” KJV; ASV;


48
  J. D. Pentecost, “The Relation between Living and Resurrected Saints in the Millennium” BibSac 117 (1960):
337.
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                      Page 45
      ESV) argues for a single resurrection consisting of two groups (cf. Acts 24:15), not two resurrections
      separated by 1,000 years, as taught by millennialists.49 (Emphasis his.)

Jackson is correct that one resurrection is in view in Dan 12:3. However, he has an unwarranted assumption that
invalids his criticism of millennialism. As defended in TOD, a dispensational framework is superior. Revelation
sees two distinct resurrections. Postmillennialism and amillennialism both criticize premillennialism as being a one-
text eschatology. Their joint contention is that premillennialism only has one text, Rev 20:4-6, which explicitly
teaches two distinct resurrections at two different times. Notwithstanding, John is not the first biblical writer to
describe two separate resurrections. Daniel does so as well, but his sight is limited to the first one. The second
resurrection is left implicit. A heavenly being informs him that, at the resurrection being described, many (as
opposed to all) will be raised. This Danielic text implicitly substantiates the premillennial position. Therefore, the
simplistic interpretation, which perceives Daniel as only being shown one resurrection with the faithful going to the
Heavenly City and unbelievers going to Hell, must be rejected. Daniel can see quite clearly, among other things,
that this resurrection is limited. The resurrection of the rest of humanity must be at another point in time. Daniel
does not see a simple soteriological-misthological dichotomy taking place for all humanity at this time. Jackson is
mistaken in making this assumption.
                                              Telescopic Soteriology
       The dispensationalist frequently responds to the general resurrection theory of this Danielic passage by
assuming a misthological-soteriological dichotomy taking place for all humanity but asserts that it happens at two
different times. The many are resurrected first. The rest are resurrected a thousand years later.
                 Illustration 12. Daniel Sees Two Resurrections (Misthological & Soteriological)




       In the opinion of such dispensationalists, this misthological resurrection of believers together with the
soteriological resurrection of unbelievers is simply a telescopic portrayal in which the resurrection of the believers
at the beginning of the millennium is lumped together with that of the unbelievers at the end of the millennium.
This Danielic passage is merely a summary statement that both groups will be raised after the tribulation when the
Jewish people are rescued. It does not establish that both are raised at the same time. Granted, this interpretation is
superior in that it at least acknowledges that Daniel is describing the resurrection of many as opposed to all initially.
Even so, it is flawed to pose that Daniel is posing the resurrection of an unrestricted all. Daniel is not describing the
resurrection of all broken up into two different time periods. This dispensational interpretation fails to do justice to

49
    Wayne Jackson, “What About Daniel’s Prophecy of the General Resurrection?” Available at

2/24/2021.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                       Page 46
Daniel’s limitation. Telescopic eschatology is, of course, valid in various contexts. However, this particular text
does not lend itself to a soteriological interpretation, even if it is telescopic.
                                                Simple Misthology
       The dispensational interpretation that Wilkin favors is superior to these two previous interpretations and sees
this text as describing a misthological judgment dealing exclusively with believers before the millennium.

           Note that “many” will awake. Why doesn’t it say “all”? The reason is that Daniel is thinking of
      the resurrection of the righteous before the Millennium. Unbelievers will be resurrected after the
      Millennium (Rev 20:13).
           Thus Daniel speaks of two types of resurrection that OT believers will experience. Some will be
      resurrected to “everlasting life,” which is a reference to abundant eternal life. We find the same concept
      in Matt 19:29, Gal 6:8, and Heb 11:36. Whenever eternal life is spoken of as a possible future
      experience, the reward of abundant eternal life is in view. Even in the OT.
           But some believers will experience shame when resurrected. Of course this is a New Testament
      teaching as well: “Little children, abide in Him, that when He appears, we may have confidence and
      not be ashamed before Him at His coming” (1 John 2:28). See also Mark 8:38.
           Now admittedly Daniel does speak of “everlasting contempt” which is literally “everlasting
      abhorrence.” But this is parallel to shame. It simply means that forever this person will live with the
      consequences of their unwise life. “Those who are wise shall shine like the brightness of the
      firmament…forever and ever” (Dan 12:3). The implication is that those who are unwise shall not
      “shine” forever in their service for Christ.50
                       Illustration 13. Daniel Sees the First Resurrection (Misthological)




       First, as rightly noted by Wilkin, Daniel says that many rather than all “of those who sleep” will be raised
because Daniel is not talking about a general resurrection of all people (many ≠ all). Some of the dead (i.e., the
unsaved dead) will not be raised until the end of the millennium a thousand years later. Counters to this position are
not convincing. Peterson acknowledges that many rather than all is the most common meaning of this word and yet
seeks to maintain the traditional interpretation by asserting that God will only raise some—friends and foes—
basically martyrs and those who martyr them.51 While Peterson’s affirmation that this is only a resurrection of the
part rather than the whole is appreciated, it is unlikely that God will only raise part of the unbelievers at the time

pictured by Daniel. The resurrection of the lost is better understood as occurring at the end of the millennium as
affirmed by John. God will not raise any of the lost before the millennium. There is no need to raise the unregenerate
before the millennium since they will not have any entrance into the millennial kingdom. It is much more natural
to suppose that the part (i.e., the many) God will raise at this time described by Daniel will be those who are
believers. All believers will need to be raised before the millennium for their participation in the millennial kingdom
(or for exclusion within it). There is no need to raise unbelievers at this time. As already demonstrated in the
Johannine aside in Rev 20:4-5, the martyrs are raised at the beginning of the millennium, but “the rest of the dead
were not resurrected until after the millennium” (TM).
       Secondly, those raised in Dan 12:2 appear to be those who have their names written in the Book of Life in
verse 1. Those Jews living at the end of the tribulation who have their name in the Book of Life will be rescued,
while those Jews who are dead at the end of the tribulation who have their name in the Book of Life will be raised.
Thus, those Jews who have their names written in the Book of Life are either rescued or raised at this time. This is
a resurrection of only those who, at the end of the tribulation, have their names in the Book of Life.52
       Thirdly, those being raised are described as those who sleep. This imagery is certainly capable of describing
dead believers (cp. Is 26:19). Although the NT uses a different word for sleep in reference to NT believers of the
church age being asleep in Christ (in order to limit that reference to believers, cf. 1Thess 4:16), no insurmountable
reason is present in this Danielic context for limiting those asleep in the ground to believers. The context, rather
than the phrase itself, supplies the limitation. In other words, the context would limit those who sleep in the ground
(with their names in the Book of Life who are resurrected at this time) to be understood as believers at the conclusion
of the tribulational period.
       Fourthly, the LXX word for shame (aischune) is the same word that Jesus uses concerning believers
experiencing shame at His judgment (Rev 3:18). And, as Wilkin points out, the verbal form (aischuno) is used of
believers being ashamed when the Lord returns in 1Jn 2:28, and a compound of it is used of their being put to shame
in Mk 8:38.53 As for disgrace (oneidismos), Paul says believers may fall into disgrace (1Tim 3:7).54 These disgraced
believers will be sent to the outer darkness. Glorified believers will shine like stars in the Heavenly City. The second
resurrection of the rest is grayed out and lowered below the line of Daniel’s site in Illustration 13. Daniel Sees the
First Resurrection (Misthological) because what Daniel is being shown does not include this resurrection.
       Overall, there is no compelling reason to believe that the second part of Dan 12:2 is not describing believers.
Instead, there are significant eschatological and misthological grounds for concluding that those resurrected to
everlasting shame are believers. Contextual considerations lean in favor of this conclusion as well. Therefore, it is
best to conclude that the rewards being described in this verse are for faithful and unfaithful believers. The negative
rewards are just as permanent as the positive rewards. The shame for the unfaithful believer will be just as eternal
as the shine for the faithful believer. In comparison, the shameful believers will have a darkened misthological
experience throughout eternity future. Likewise, David warns, “As for you, my son Solomon, know the God of your
father, and serve Him with a whole heart and a willing mind; for the Lord searches all hearts, and understands every
intent of the thoughts. If you seek Him, He will let you find Him; but if you forsake Him, He will reject you forever”

   Although the Hebrew word for contempt (deraon) is only found in Is 66:24 and Dan 12:2, the usage need not be
