(1Chron 28:9). At the Jewish Bema, King Solomon will be rejected permanently from being king in the millennial
and eternal kingdom because he forsook the Lord. He will be rejected from living in the Heavenly City. Limiting
those raised to the righteous, as if all believers are experientially righteous, is accursed soteriology and poor
exegesis. All believers will be raised, both the good and the bad. All who have eternal life, not merely those who
lived a good life, will be raised.
                                Illustration 14. Danielic Misthological Resurrection




        This is a premillennial resurrection of believers so that they can be judged to determine what their status will
be in the millennial kingdom. This text counters the postmillennial and amillennial claim that there is only one
resurrection—a general resurrection of the lost and saved. Not so. This is a resurrection of only some of those who
are dead; evidently, we may logically infer the resurrection of the rest is to follow later, just as John clarifies: “The
rest of the dead did not come to life until the thousand years were completed” (Rev 20:5). This Danielic counterpart
also refutes those who wish to point at the soteriological division between good sheep and bad goats in the Matthean
passage as proving that there are no bad sheep at this judgment. Whereas Matthew is dealing with all the [living]
nations at this judgment (Mt 25:32), Daniel is focusing on the believers at this judgment, specifically Jewish
believers. A Jewish Bema is principally in view. The two accounts coalesce to complement one another and show
that black sheep will be judged at this time. Therefore, a black sheep has been added in Illustration 13. Daniel Sees
the First Resurrection (Misthological).
        The premillennial judgment being described in Dan 12:1-3, in which only some are raised, is part of a
complex of judgments taking place at the time of the Sheep and Goats Judgment described in Mt 25:31-46.55 The
Danielic aspect is limited to what happens to resurrected OT believers at this judgment, many of whom will be OT
Jewish believers. This judgment also will include the Jewish believers who are alive and rescued when the Lord
returns at the end of the tribulation. Hence, this Danielic judgment is predominantly a Jewish Bema (i.e., Jacobean
Bema), and it is appropriately termed as such in contradistinction to the Gentile Bema (i.e., the Sheep and Goats
Judgment) that occurs shortly thereafter. Daniel is describing a premillennial resurrection of OT (predominantly
Jewish) believers so that they can be judged to determine what their status will be in the millennial kingdom.
Whereas Matthew is dealing with all the [living] nations (Gentiles) at this judgment (Mt 25:32), Daniel is focusing
on the believers at this judgment, specifically Jewish believers. A Jewish Bema is in view.
        Emphatically, Daniel does not limit the outcome of this Jewish Bema to the millennial age. Although this
judgment occurs before the millennial kingdom, the results of this judgment are not limited to the millennial
kingdom. Daniel is very clear, here as he is elsewhere, that the results are eternal. Even if one insists upon translating
the Hebrew word olam in Dan 12:3 as referring to the millennial age, then the addition of the second Hebrew word

55
     See Illustration 16. Sequence of Separation Complex, 51.
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                     Page 49
ad moves beyond this millennial age to eternity, eternalizing the misthological benefit. According to this rendering
of the Hebrew text, the reward of shining brightly as a star lasts for the millennial age plus eternity—encapsulated
by one Greek word, aion, used in its plural form, meaning forever. Eternity cannot be grayed out in Illustration 13.
Daniel Sees the First Resurrection (Misthological). Daniel sees the eternal misthological division between the
faithful who shine like stars in the Heavenly City and those who do not.
       Jewish believers will be resurrected and judged at the Jewish Bema. Next, Gentile believers will be
resurrected and judged at the Gentile Bema. Overlapping applicability applies, however, between the two
judgments. The Jewish Bema promises in Dan 12:2-3, although focused on Jewish believers, have applicability to
Gentile believers. The Gentile Bema promise in Mt 25:31 will have applicability to faithful Jewish believers. In
summary, both Jewish and Gentile believers who overcome will shine like the sun and inherit the kingdom. All
faithful believers of all dispensations live in the Heavenly City as part of the Bride of the Lamb. Unfaithful believers
are currently in the celestial outer darkness. Faithful believers are currently in the Celestial City. After the Bema,
unfaithful believers will be sent back to the celestial outer darkness. Faithful believers will continue to have their
primary residence in the Bridal City.
                                           Separation Precedes Judgment
        Because this Danielic passage is part of a complex of judgments, it is reasonable to look at it in the context
of those passages, especially the sheep and goats. Two interesting facts that Jesus presents in this parable should
first be noted. First, the separation of the sheep from the goats precedes their judgment. The separation is based
on what they are (a sheep or a goat) and determines whether they enter the kingdom or the eternal fire. Second, the
judgment that comes after the separation is based on one thing—on what they do—not what they are. This is exactly
what one would expect from the Lord’s earlier comment in Mt 16:27. “For the Son of Man is going to come in the
glory of His Father with His angels; and will then recompense every man according to his deeds.” Judgment is
always based on deeds (i.e., works). However, it must not be concluded that the determining factor as to whether
they go to Hell is their works. For this would teach that salvation from eternal damnation is by works. This is an
accursed gospel, and those who believe it will be declared to be accursed (cf. Mt 25:41). The judgment is not to
determine whether they enter the kingdom but whether they inherit the kingdom. Their entrance into the kingdom,
in contrast, was determined by the separation which preceded the judgment.
        From the OT background, we learn that a separation evidently already had taken place in regard to Jesus’
brethren, the Jews, whom Jesus mentions in this Matthean context (25:40). Feinberg points out that this Matthean
separation of the sheep from the goats is actually the second of the two separations that take place after the
tribulation:

         While many unbelievers will be slain at Christ’s return, two judgments follow to root out all who
         remain. Ezekiel 20:37, 38 indicates that when Israel is established in the land at the return of their
         Messiah, there will be renewal of the covenant (v. 37) and purging of the rebels from the nation
         (v. 38). Thus, all wicked will be separated from Israel. All remaining Gentile unbelievers will be
         eliminated at the sheep-and-goat judgment (Matt 25:31-46). Therefore, no wicked will enter the
         kingdom. (Italics his.)56

        Following Christ’s return, after the Jews are gathered together to be judged, the Jewish rebels among them
will not be allowed to enter the Promised Land. Thus, the Pauline affirmation that all Israel will be saved should be
understood as a relative all. However, it does not necessarily follow that these rebellious Jews are not allowed into
the kingdom itself or that the salvation at stake for these unfaithful or rebellious Jews is salvation from eternal
damnation. The context in Ezekiel is suggestive that the rebellious Jews will be saved soteriologically in that their
judgment is likened by Ezekiel unto the manner in which God dealt with the Jews who died in the wilderness and
who thus were not allowed into the Promised Land. God’s rebellious children (the Jews) died in the wilderness, but
they were not sent back to Egypt. Misthological salvation from a wilderness experience and pertaining to receiving
the misthological inheritance appear to be the issues at stake for these Jews rather than being thrown into an eternal
fire. The typology is suggestive that this picture is dealing with misthological exclusion from the Promised Land
itself rather than confinement in a subterranean imprisonment. The Promised Land is the land of Canaan, not the
entire planet.

56
     Feinberg, Rapture, 74.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                    Page 50
                      Illustration 15. Heavenly Jerusalem in Relation to Promised Land




                                                                    Moon




      The above planetary illustration from TOD is drawn to scale. Celestial promises pertaining to resurrected
believers being glorified like the stars of heaven are related to the Heavenly City, which is pictured as white.
Terrestrial promises of the Promised Land are pictured by placing the Promised Land in white. The Heavenly City
is much bigger than the Promised Land. Likewise, the Promised Land is much smaller than the entire planet.
Therefore, being excluded from the Promised Land is not necessarily the same thing as being excluded from the
millennial kingdom since the latter will encompass the entire planet. Consequently, Constable’s remarks on
Eze 20:36-38 may be a little closer to the mark (TCENB):
           As the Lord had judged the fathers in the wilderness long ago, so He would judge the
      children of His people. He would discipline them to sanctify them (cf. Jer. 33:13). It was customary
      for shepherds to count their sheep as they passed under their staff that they held over the doorway of
      the sheepfold….
           The Lord would also bring His people under obligation to keep the terms of a covenant. This is
      evidently a reference to the New Covenant (cf. 36:25-38; Jer. 31:31-34). He would weed out the
      rebels and transgressors from among them and bring them out of the countries where they lived
      but would not bring them into the Promised Land. This probably refers to the Jews who will die
      during the Tribulation period (cf. Zech. 13:8; Rev. 12:15-17) and or when the Lord returns to the earth
      (cf. Matt. 25:31-46).
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                     Page 51

        Those unfaithful Jewish believers who survive the tribulation will be allowed into the millennial kingdom
but excluded from the Promised Land itself within the millennial kingdom. While entrance into the millennial
kingdom is free, this land of promise within the kingdom is limited to those who meet the qualifications necessary
to reside there. This would indeed correspond very well with a misthological understanding of the outer darkness
regarding these Jewish outcasts presented by Matthew earlier in his gospel (Mt 8:11-12). To his credit, in his chart
A Survey of the Judgments, Betz lists seven judgments and places four of them as occurring at this point in time
(i.e., after the tribulation and before the millennium).57 The first of these post-tribulational judgments (the judgment
of the beast and false prophet in Rev 19:11-20) is not included in the chart below since that judgment does not entail
the separation of one group from another. Betz’s remaining intervening judgments are included with amendment
and addition in the following chart.
                                 Illustration 16. Sequence of Separation Complex
         Judgment               JSC    SGJ      GWT                            Scripture
 1. Christians                   S                       Rom 14:10,12; 1Cor 3:11-15; 2Cor 5:10

 1. Living Jews                         SL               Mal 3:2-5 (1a separation)
 2. Believing Jews                       S               Eze 20:33-38; Mal 3:2-5; Mt 25:1-13 (1b separation)
 3. Resurrected Jews                     S               Dan 12:1-3; Rev 20:4,5b,6

 1. Living Gentiles                     SL               Joel 3:1-2; Mt 25:31-46 (2a separation)
 2. Believing Gentiles                   S               Mt 24:13-14 (2b separation)
 3. Resurrected Gentiles                 S               Rev 6:11; 7:14; 14:13; 20: 4,5b,6

 • Remaining unjudged                          SL     Rev 20:5a; 20:12-13
S = Saved   L = Lost          SGJ = Sheep and Goat Judgment complex       GWT = Great White Throne Judgment

       The evidence concerning this time frame leads one to anticipate two types of separations dealing with two
basic groups of people—Jews and Gentiles. (1) First, if there are any lost Jews who are alive at the end of the
tribulation, the Lord will separate these lost Jewish goats from the saved Jewish sheep who are alive at the end of
the tribulation. Betz affirms that this is indeed the case in his chart, and I will entertain the possibility as well.
(2) Second, the Lord misthologically will separate the good sheep from the black sheep, in regard to the living
Jewish believers. That is, rescued Jewish believers who live until the end of the tribulation will be judged as to
whether they qualify to inherit the kingdom. Being morally pure in terms of not having taken the mark of the beast
is not enough to qualify as a kingdom ruler. Unfaithful living Jewish believers will be excluded from their ancestral
kingdom but be allowed to live in the terrestrial kingdom among the Gentiles, which would be a terrestrial outer
darkness for unfaithful living Jews banished to live among the nations. (3) Third, the Lord likewise will
misthologically separate the resurrected good Jewish sheep from the black Jewish sheep.58 This resurrection,
followed by separation, deals exclusively with Jewish believers from both the OT and tribulational time periods.
The lost are not resurrected until a thousand years later, so they are not in view.
       After dealing with the Jews, the Lord will then repeat this process in dealing with the Gentiles. That is, just
as before, the symmetry will again be that the soteriological separation of sheep from goats will be followed by the
misthological separation of good sheep from black sheep. (1) First, the Lord will separate the lost Gentile goats
from the saved Gentile sheep. This separation of the Gentile nations will be in accordance with how they responded
to the gospel of grace—the soteriological component of the kingdom message which had been proclaimed by the
Lord’s Jewish brethren during the tribulation. Those Gentiles who believe in the Jewish Messiah for eternal life will
be found to have that life and therefore allowed into the millennial kingdom. (2) Next, the Lord misthologically will
separate the good Gentile sheep who have lived through the tribulation from the black Gentile sheep who lived
through it. This will be in regard to Gentile believers and their response to the gospel of the kingdom preached by

57
     Harlan D. Betz, Setting the Stage for Eternity (Kingwood, TX: Falcon Publishing LTD, 2006), 321.
58
     Betz correctly identified the Danielic passage as dealing exclusively with misthological themes.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                        Page 52
the Jewish evangelists: “He who endures to the end will be saved” (Mt 24:13-14). Those Gentiles who responded
favorably to this message and to its Jewish messengers will be saved from misthological exclusion and not only be
allowed to enter the millennial kingdom but perhaps even inherit the kingdom depending on their degree of
faithfulness. Those Gentile believers who are found to be unfaithful will merely enter the millennial kingdom in
non-glorified bodies rather than inherit the kingdom. They and their children and the children of the lost will
repopulate the planet, and these descendants will comprise the nations that rebel at the end of the millennium.
(3) That the present Matthean passage only deals with the good sheep does not mean that other details, such as the
existence of black sheep, cannot be inferred. For example, it is necessary to deduce, among other things, that there
will be a resurrection of faithful Gentile believers so that they can receive their promised inheritance. So, it may be
inferred that there will also be a resurrection of all Gentile believers from both the OT and tribulational eras. As
above, this resurrection, followed by separation, deals exclusively with Gentile believers from both the OT and
tribulational time periods. The lost are not resurrected until a thousand years later, so they are not in view.
                                                Complex Misthology
Questions
       The misthological information to which Daniel was privy cannot be limited to the simple misthological
inferences drawn by Wilkin. To be sure, Wilkin’s analysis is a great starting point, an excellent summation of what
Daniel could see. Even so, we would do well to proceed further and consider what additional inferences Daniel
could perceive. Consider the following questions:

1.   Could Daniel infer a Jewish versus non-Jewish duality to the resurrection of the many?
2.   Could Daniel infer a subsequent resurrection for the rest of humanity?
3.   Could Daniel infer a celestial versus terrestrial scope for Jewish versus non-Jewish resurrected believers?
4.   Could Daniel infer a celestial versus terrestrial scope for faithful versus faithful believers?
5.   Could Daniel infer a variation in the celestial scope for faithful versus unfaithful resurrected believers?
6.   Could Daniel infer a variation in the terrestrial scope for faithful versus unfaithful rescued believers?

       Daniel would have known that the resurrection of those raised to eternal astral glory (to shine like stars
because of their insight) cannot be limited to Jewish believers. Righteous OT believers who lived before the time
of Abraham, such as Able and Noah and Shem, would certainly be included. So, although the focus of the rescue
in Dan 12:1 is on the Jewish people who live to the end of the tribulation, the resurrection at that time is not limited
to the Jewish people. Daniel would perceive this to be the case. He could reasonably infer that the statements in
Dan 12:2-3 were not limited to the Jewish people. All believers would be raised, both Jewish and Gentile, both the
faithful and unfaithful. This deduction would be elementary for him.
       Reasonably, he could infer that unbelievers would be resurrected and judged sometime subsequently.
Admittedly, he would not know the length of time between the resurrection of believers versus unbelievers. He
would not know that it was a thousand years later since that detail was reserved for John. Nonetheless, Daniel could
perceive that after some indeterminate period of time, the rest of the dead would be resurrected and sent to their
fiery judgment.
Variations
       Since he knew that the righteous are to be like stars and inhabit the Heavenly City, he could logically infer
that unrighteous believers would not be like stars or inhabit the City. A celestial variation would have to exist
between those resurrected believers who shine and those who do not. Posing an alternative celestial location for
resurrected believers who do not shine like stars would have been intuitive inference for Daniel.
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                   Page 53
                        Illustration 17. Terrestrial and Celestial Misthological Variations




       Daniel knew the promises regarding the Promised Land for the Jewish people. Plus, he knew that there were
additional lands not promised to the Jewish people and that the Gentiles would inhabit those lands. Therefore, he
knew that some of the promises were terrestrial as opposed to celestial in scope. He also knew of the terrestrial-
celestial misthological variation. He further recognized that mistholic differentiation would exist in the terrestrial
realm and was thus aware of a terrestrial misthological variation. He had every reason to suspect a celestial
misthological variation as well.
Wandering Starlike Planets
      Might Daniel have pictured unfaithful resurrected believers as wandering planets in celestial outer darkness?
Jude did! Why not Daniel also? Through Enochian prophecy, Jude indicates that the black darkness of stellar space
applies to all the godless:
      12
        These men are those who are hidden reefs in your love feasts when they feast with you without fear,
      caring for themselves; clouds without water, carried along by winds; autumn trees without fruit, doubly
      dead, uprooted; 13 wild waves of the sea, casting up their own shame like foam; wandering stars
      [planets], for whom the black darkness has been reserved forever. 14 And about these also Enoch,
      in the seventh generation from Adam, prophesied, saying, “Behold, the Lord came with many
      thousands of His holy ones, 15 to execute judgment upon all, and to convict all the ungodly of all their
      ungodly deeds which they have done in an ungodly way, and of all the harsh things which ungodly
      sinners have spoken against Him.” (Jude 1:12-15)

As to the Enochian background, Bauckham (WBC) provides these comments:
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                        Page 54
             This imagery is taken up in the later Book of Dreams (1 Enoch 83–90), which in its allegory of
      world history represents the fall of the Watchers as the fall of stars from heaven (86:1–3); then, in a
      passage corresponding to 1 Enoch 10 (which Jude quoted in v 6) the archangels cast the stars down
      into the darkness of the abyss and bind them there (88:1, 3) until their judgment at the End, when they
      will be cast into the abyss of fire (90:24). If Jude is alluding to these passages, as many think (Plummer,
      Mayor, Windisch, Chaine, Kelly; Milik, 1 Enoch, 239), then he is once again, as in v 6, comparing
      the false teachers with the fallen angels. His phrase ἀστέρες πλανῆται (“wandering stars”) was
      normally used of the planets (references in TDNT 6, 229 n.7), and was still so used, even though in
      Jude’s day astronomers were aware that in fact their movements are regular (Cicero, De natura deorum
      2.51, quoted Mayor). This corresponds to Enoch’s reference to “seven stars” (1 Enoch 18:13; 21:3),
      though the fall of the stars (86:1–3) would perhaps better suit comets or meteors.
             A minor criticism of this view would be that in 1 Enoch darkness is the temporary fate of the
      fallen angels (1 Enoch 88:1; cf. 10:4–5), as it is in Jude 6, until at the Last Judgment they are thrown
      into the fire (1 Enoch 90:24; cf. 10:6, 13; 21:7–10). In Jude 13 darkness is the eternal destiny of the
      stars. But this may indicate only that Jude is using his source material flexibly.
             There is, however, another passage in 1 Enoch which may have a better claim to have been
      immediately in Jude’s mind: the passage already mentioned, in 1 Enoch 80, which describes the
      lawlessness of nature in the last days: “And many heads of the stars in command will go astray, and
      these will change their courses and their activities, and will not appear at the times which have been
      prescribed for them” (80:6, tr. Knibb)…..
             …. In 1 Enoch the place of final damnation is usually represented by fire, but Jewish
      thought also knew the idea of consignment to eternal darkness (Tob 14:10; 1 Enoch 46:6; 63:6;
      Pss. Sol. 14:9; 15:10; cf. Matt. 8:12; 22:13; 25:30), and the two images were sometimes combined
      (1 Enoch 103:8; 108:14; & SibOr; 4:43; 1QS 2:8; 4:13; 2 Enoch 10:2). Jude will have chosen the
      image of darkness here because it is a more appropriate fate for stars. (Italics his.)

        Commentators are frequently divided as to whether the reference is to wandering stars or wandering planets.
Schreiner (NAC) prefers the latter: “The NIV translates ‘wandering stars,’ which is certainly possible, but it is
even more likely that wandering planets are intended.” Other commentators, like Green (TNTC), would
advocate shooting stars, “Jude is thinking not of planets, but of shooting stars which fall out of the sky and are
engulfed in darkness—to the confusion of all who watch them. For this metaphor he goes to 1 Enoch once more
(xviii. 14ff.) where the angel shows Enoch ‘a prison for the stars of heaven’.”
        Regardless, Jude is thinking in terms of stellar imagery in which some stellar bodies that otherwise look
like stationary stars move in the stellar darkness. Since the Greek word for wondering is planetes (from which
we get the word planets) and the word for star is the typical aster, translating the combination as wandering
starlike planets is preferable.

             Jude uses this last metaphor to cause the reader to look at the night sky and see the planets.
      He knows that a careful observer sees some planets wander through the heavens. They illuminate
      the darkness but because of their wandering courses, they cannot be relied on for navigational
      purposes. We should stay with the exact wording of the text and not interpret the words to mean
      meteors or shooting stars that disappear in the darkness of the night. Jude places the emphasis on the
      concept wander, which aptly applies to the false teachers. In fact, the word error (v. 11) derives from
      the verb to wander. By applying this concept to the heretics, Jude describes them as apostates in whose
      company no Christian can chart a straight paths. Their devious course of life leads to eternal
      damnation. (Kistemaker, NTC)

      “The Greek word used here may be literally rendered as ‘wanderer.’ It could refer to planets, which
      in the ancient mindset disappeared into blackness for no apparent reason” (Barry, FSB).

      The Greek word planetes, which transliterated means “planet,” really means wanderer. Long
      ago stargazers observed that these wanderers across the sky were different from the fixed stars.
      Likewise the false teachers had gone off course and had led people astray. (Constable, TCENB)
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                     Page 55


         The word rendered “wandering” (πλανῆται planētai) is that from which we have derived the word
         “planet.” It properly means one who wanders about; a wanderer; and was given by the ancients
         to planets because they seemed to wander about the heavens, now forward and now backward
         among the ether stars, without any fixed law. (Barnes, BN)
                                            Illustration 18. A Rouge Planet




       These wandering starlike planets were originally thought to be rouge planets. The above image of a rouge
planet is from NASA. Therefore, if we want to picture these wandering planets the way intended by Jude, we must
think in terms of rouge planets. Wikipedia provides this definition: “A rogue planet (also termed an interstellar,
nomad, free-floating, unbound, orphan, wandering, starless, or sunless planet) is a planetary-mass object that does
not orbit a star directly.” It also supplies an artistic video.
Misthological Darkness
       Whereas commentators presume a soteriological equation with the fires of Hell, Chitwood’s commentary is
the rare exception in that he insists that the passage is misthological:

               The condition of Christians who follow “the way of Cain,” “the error of Balaam,” or “the
         gainsaying of Korah” is depicted different ways in Jude 12, 13. Their present condition is depicted by
         showing a fruitless, shameful condition in which they are carried about every which way in the world;
         and their future condition is depicted by the statement, “wandering stars,” to whom is reserved “the
         blackness of darkness forever.”

                    (The word “forever [Gk., aion]” in verse thirteen should be translated “for an age,”
              or “with respect to an age.” The age in view can only be the coming age, the Messianic
              Era.)

                Apostate Christians described in Jude 12, 13 will occupy no position with Christ in the
         kingdom. These are the same ones who previously, prior to the establishment of the kingdom,
         will have found themselves in “outer darkness” during the time of the wedding festivities (Matt.
         22:1-14; cf. Matt. 8:11, 12; 25:14-30; Luke 13:28, 29); and the thought of darkness is used by Jude
         to describe the continued condition of these Christians in the kingdom itself.
                Compare Jude 13 with I Peter 1:4. There is an inheritance “reserved” for Christians, and there
         is also the blackness of darkness “reserved” for Christians (the word “reserved” is the same in both
         passages in the Greek text). The former is reserved for faithful Christians, and the latter is reserved
         for unfaithful Christians. “The inheritance,” according to I Peter 1:5, is reserved for those “who are
         kept by the power of God through faith [faith being brought to its goal through works (cf. vv. 6-9;
         James 2:14ff)] unto salvation [salvation of the soul (cf. vv. 9-11; James 1:21)] ready to be revealed in
         the last time”; and “the blackness of darkness,” according to Jude 10, 11, is reserved for those who
         “corrupt themselves” through following “the way of Cain,” “the error of Balaam,” or “the gainsaying
         of Korah.” (Italics his.)59


59
     Arlen L. Chitwood, Jude: Acts of the Apostles (Norman, OK: The Lamp Broadcast, 2012), 111-112.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                           Page 56
       Chitwood is wrong to assert that their confinement is only for an age. If this passage is talking about
misthology, as Chitwood asserts, then the result is eternal since rewards are eternal (see RAE). Conversely, if the
passage is talking about soteriology, as is virtually universally assumed, then the result is likewise eternal. Chitwood
insists that the entire passage, including Enoch’s prophecy, is limited to the regenerate:

                It is commonly taught by most interpreters of Jude that Enoch’s prophecy concerns judgment
         befalling the unregenerate earth-dwellers when Christ returns to the earth at the end of the Tribulation.
         However, that is not at all what the text states. Enoch “prophesied of these” (vv. 14-16). The
         reference is to individuals in the preceding verses, to apostates. The scope of judgment in Enoch’s
         prophecy is “upon all” Christians, but he particularly singles out the apostates “among them”
         (v. 15).
                Attention has been called in past chapters to the fact that II Peter and Jude are companion
         epistles, paralleling one another in a number of places. The parallel section to Jude 14-16 is II Peter
         2:18, 19. These verses in II Peter, as the verses in Jude, refer to the ones who have corrupted themselves
         through apostate acts, outlined in the preceding verses. These are the ones for whom the “blackness of
         darkness” is reserved (II Peter 2:17; Jude 13), rather than the “inheritance” to which Christians have
         been called (I Peter 1:4).60 (Italics his.)

        Whereas Chitwood seeks to limit the passage to the misthological realm, virtually all other commentators
limit it to the soteriological realm. A dichotomy need not be the case, however. Duality is preferable. Although the
primary application might be intended by Enoch and Jude for the unregenerate, at least a secondary application is
intended for the regenerate. Enoch and Jude indicate this to be the case. The Enochian prophecy applies to all the
godless whom the Lord finds living here at the time, not just to the unregenerate godless (contrary to the majority
opinion) or just to the regenerate godless (contrary to Chitwood’s opinion). Enoch and Jude are clear. The Lord is
coming “to execute judgment upon all, and to convict all the ungodly of all their ungodly deeds.” Chitwood is
correct that a relative all is entailed. My contention, though, is that the relative all applies to all the living godless—
all those living when the Lord returns—not to the dead godless. This all applies only to the living, not to the dead.
Principle of Correlativity
      The following abbreviated adapted extracts from TOD will suffice for background material and correlativity.
Both 1 Enoch and Jude are referring to the same darkness. The judgment referred to in Jude 1:14-15 is the same as
1En 1:9.

         14And about these also Enoch, in the seventh generation from Adam, prophesied, saying, “Behold, the
         Lord came with many thousands of His holy ones, 15to execute judgment upon all, and to convict all
         the ungodly of all their ungodly deeds which they have done in an ungodly way, and of all the harsh
         things which ungodly sinners have spoken against Him” (Jude 1:14-15).
         9
          Behold, he will arrive with ten million of the holy ones in order to execute judgment upon all. He
         will destroy the wicked ones and censure all flesh on account of everything that they have done, that
         which sinners and the wicked ones committed against him. (1En 1:9)

The relationship between these two passages is well stated by Hiebert:

         The apocryphal Book of Enoch, compiled during the first century B.C. from some apocryphal works
         belonging to the last two centuries B.C., exerted a strong and widespread influence on Jewish and early
         Christian literature…
             When Jude wrote, “Enoch…prophesied saying”…he clearly accepted it as a historical fact…it is
         found in the Book of Enoch (1:9) with some variations…the quotation was drawn from the Book of
         Enoch. But this does not mean that Jude accepted the Book of Enoch as inspired or that he approved



60
     Ibid., 114.
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                       Page 57
      of all its contents. It simply means that under the inspiration of the Holy Spirit he accepted the
      statement as true.61

The biblical writers did not consider all their oral and written sources to be inspired. Many of their sources, such as
1 Enoch, contained “some truth and a good deal of error.”62 But writing under inspiration, the biblical writers only
accepted as true that part of their sources that was actually true. Or, as the old adage goes, all that is in the Bible is
true, but not all truth is in the Bible. There are many truths in worldly texts. A math book, for example, contains
truths not found in the Bible. Both Jude and Enoch are referring to the eternal punishment of the wicked in darkness
(Jude 1:6,13).63 And both writers make a truthful statement about that punishment. Hiebert is correct that Jude’s
statement has application to the unregenerate wicked in vv. 12-13.
      And angels who did not keep their own domain, but abandoned their proper abode, He has kept in
      eternal bonds under darkness for the judgment of the great day. (Jude 1:6)
      These men are those who are hidden reefs in your love feasts when they feast with you without fear,
      caring for themselves; clouds without water, carried along by winds; autumn trees without fruit, doubly
      dead, uprooted; 13wild waves of the sea, casting up their own shame like foam; wandering stars, for
      whom the black darkness has been reserved forever. (Jude 1:12-13)
        Hiebert is also very perceptive when he argues that these unregenerate wicked are described as hidden rocks
or reefs beneath the surface of the water and pose a genuine threat that morally may shipwreck genuine believers.64
Using the same analogy of being shipwrecked, Paul similarly writes concerning believers who have had their faith
destroyed (1Tim 1:19; 2Tim 2:18). The NT writers recognize the fact that regenerate believers can become
shipwrecked in terms of both their faith and faithfulness. Believers can participate in the evil deeds of the
unregenerate wicked (Eph 5:11). As a result of doing so, these believers become what might be called the regenerate
wicked. The wicked dead, whether regenerate or unregenerate, will both be judged according to their works. Still,
the beauty of the NT is that these wicked believers (Mt 25:26) will not be cast into the same darkness as unbelievers.
        Some similarities between the fates of wicked believers and unbelievers may be granted, but such similarities
do not prove identical fates.65 Matthew and the rest of the NT writers realize that the gift of eternal life is received
by a response of simple faith (Mt 21:32). Yet they also teach with equal clarity that this simple act of saving faith
may fail to result in faithfulness. However, whereas 1 Enoch would consign unfaithful believers to the judgment of
literal darkness and eternal fire, the NT does not. In contrast, the NT speaks metaphorically of such believers
experiencing misthological fire (Mk 9:49; Jn 15:6; 1Cor 3:15; Heb 6:7-8; Jam 5:3; Jude 1:23) and misthological
darkness (Mt 8:12; 22:13; 25:30). Believers will come into misthological judgment but not soteriological judgment
(Jn 5:24). They will never come into a judgment where the issue is the Lake of Fire versus the kingdom of heaven.
They may be cast out misthologically (Jn 15:6), but they will never be cast out soteriologically (Jn 6:37). They can
be cast out of the banquet; they cannot be cast out of the kingdom.
        The darkness to which Jude refers is the literal darkness of 1 Enoch. Osburn believes that the passage to
which Jude refers in 1:12-13 is 1En 67:5-7.66 But his argument is unconvincing. 1En 67 does not contain a reference
to darkness. As for other top contenders, 1En 10:4 is the only passage that speaks of angels being confined to
darkness and fire. Further, this judgment in 1En 10:4 is preliminary to the great day of judgment just as it is in Jude
1:6.

      4
       And secondly the Lord said to Raphael, “Bind Azaz’el hand and foot (and) throw him into the
      darkness!” And he made a hole in the desert…and cast him there; 5he threw on top of him rugged and
      sharp rocks. And he covered his face in order that he might not see light; 6and in order that he may be
      sent into the fire on the great day of judgment. (1En 10:4-6)

The only other passage that speaks of all three—darkness, confinement, and fire—is 1En 103:7. Perhaps, this
passage is foremost on Jude’s mind as he writes about the darkness in verse 13.
      4
       Woe unto you sinners who are dead!…7they shall experience evil and great tribulation—in darkness,
      nets, and burning flame. 8Your souls shall enter into the great judgment. (1En 103:4-8)

       On the other hand, in 1En 88:3 the darkness is connected with the sexual abuses of fallen stars, which may
parallel their licentiousness (Jude 1:4) and their description as stars (Jude 1:13). Then again, their being described
as receiving the error of Balaam as their reward in Jude 1:11 may allude to 1En 108 where “the spirits of the ones
who err” (v. 7) are “taken into darkness” (v. 14). Rather than limit Jude’s references to any one specific passage,
the safest conclusion is that Jude had the general impression created by a number of passages in mind. Nonetheless,
the passage to which Jude most directly alludes in Jude 1:6 is 1En 10:4-6. However, TOD also presents the theory
of correlativity to allow applicability of the Enochian darkness to believers.
          Illustration 19. Principle of Correlativity and Typology as Subsets of Biblical Correspondence
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                     Page 59

       To clarify the relationship between typology and the principle of correlativity67 being proposed herein, when
typology is defined narrowly as the intentional OT portrayal of a future NT event, then obviously many things that
would fall within the range of typology would fall outside the range of the principle of correlativity and vice versa.
Nevertheless, some overlap would occur in which an OT judgment that took place regarding unbelievers might
serve as a warning for NT believers.
       If some type of soteric-mistholic correlation exists between the soteriological black darkness of Jude 1:13
and the misthological outer darkness of Matthew, as herein posed, then the expulsion of those in the outer darkness
to planets in the outer realm of the third heaven might be entertained as correlative parallels.
       As to the correlativity hypothesized between the judgment pronounced on demons and unfaithful believers,
several passages indicate that some type of connection exists between the judgment of (1) Satan, demons, and
unbelievers as compared to that of (2) unfaithful believers. In Jude 1:11-13, being destroyed in the rebellion of
Korah is linked (not equated) with spending eternity in the black darkness forever. But the destruction that took
place at Korah would be presumed to represent misthological destruction. Thus, misthological destruction is
purposely linked with (but not equated with) soteriological destruction (Korah ≅ Hell; Korah ≠ Hell). Not only do
these two verses supply this link in back-to-back conjunction, but Jude uses the destruction (apollumi) that took
place after the people were saved from Egypt to depict the misthological outcome for believers who subsequently
disbelieved after being saved out of Egypt (Jude 1:5), and he links this destruction with the soteriological outcome
for unbelievers who never believed (Jude 1:11).

•    The Lord, after saving a people out of the land of Egypt, subsequently destroyed [apollumi] those who did not
     believe. (Jude 1:5)

The Lord saved them and then destroyed some of those whom He saved. One such occasion, in which He destroyed
those whom He saved out of Egypt, was at the rebellion of Korah:

•    11
       Woe to them! For they [unregenerate false teachers] have gone the way of Cain, and for pay they have rushed
     headlong into the error of Balaam, and were destroyed [apollumi] in the rebellion of Korah. 12 These men are
     those who are hidden reefs in your love feasts when they feast with you without fear, caring for themselves;
     clouds without water, carried along by winds; autumn trees without fruit, doubly dead, uprooted; 13 wild waves
     of the sea, casting up their own shame like foam; wandering stars, for whom the black darkness has been
     reserved forever. (Jude 1:11-13; TM)

       Since the destruction at Korah was part of the destruction that took place after the Israelites came out of
Egypt, the misthological destruction that occurs in v. 5 is not only verbally linked, but also contextually linked, with
the soteriological destruction that occurs in v. 11. This dual mistholic-soteric association makes it unmistakably
clear that both subcomers and noncomers reap destruction as their outcome. Many who were saved out of Egypt
perished in a manner similar to unbelievers.68



67
   The principle of correlativity is a subset of the broader field of correspondence that refers to a specific type of
intentional correlation in which a broad soteric-mistholic or mistholic-soteric typological relation is perceived to
exist between two different persons, events, or states. A mistholic-mistholic correlation, therefore, such as that
which exists between the out-resurrection and the outer darkness or between the priests that serve in the outer courts
versus their typological counterparts, would be outside the scope of the type of correlation being described by the
principle of correlativity.
68
   Soteric fire ≅ mistholic fire (Jude 1:6,23). Correlations (≅) can either be soteric-mistholic or mistholic-soteric.
Peter uses an entire chapter for a soteric-mistholic relation: soteric (2Pet 2:1-17) ≅ mistholic (2Pet 2:18-23). The
soteric judgment of unregenerate phenomenal apostates ≅ the mistholic judgment of regenerate moral apostates.
Phenomenal apostates depart from the faith rather than from their faith. They never personally came to faith. On a
smaller scale, believers are capable of falling correlatively from their secure position into the destruction of those
who distort Scripture (2Pet 3:16-17).
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                            Page 60
                                  Illustration 20. Salvation Linked with Destruction




       Dissimilarities must not be lost amidst similarities, however, in developing a principle of correlativity. In
the study of typology, dissimilarities can be just as important as similarities. Jude is not saying that those saved
typologically out of Egypt will be condemned soteriologically to the same black darkness as the unregenerate.
Nevertheless, Jude intends for us to see a correlation between the misthological destruction that occurred at
Korah and the soteriological destruction of the black darkness with which he links it. In addition, the plausible
usage of the Enochian soteric outer darkness as a backdrop for the Matthean mistholic outer darkness has been
noted already. Moreover, the fact that those cast into the outer darkness and the fires of eternal punishment both
experience weeping and gnashing of teeth is surely not incidental or coincidental, but neither is it identical (Mt 8:12;
13:42,50; 22:13; 24:51; 25:30; Lk 13:28). Given this plausible Enochian linkage, the highly probable Judean
linkage, and the provable verbal linkage made by Jesus, the position adopted herein is that some intentional
mistholic-soteric correlation exists between the outer darkness and the black darkness. Although not
identical, the darkness is applicable in some manner to both unbelievers and believers.
Relative All
        When the Lord returns, He is going to deal with both the unregenerate and regenerate godless who are alive
at the time. They will all suffer a dark fate. Although both groups will not suffer the same dark fate, the same dark
imagery can be used comprehensively (and correlatively) to depict the similarity of their sin and fate. On the other
hand, this all should not be stretched to mean an absolute all. A relative all is still entailed in that it deals with those
who are here at this location when the Lord comes. Accordingly, a judgment of the living, not the dead, is entailed
when the Lord returns. This judgment is of those alive when the Lord returns at His second coming, not a judgment
of the resurrected dead at the Great White Throne Judgment one thousand years later.
        According to Jude, this prophecy was known since the days of Enoch. Therefore, it was known in the days
of Daniel. Certainly, Daniel knew this prophecy. Daniel had no theological presumption that would have led him
to the assumption that this Enochian all includes a judgment of the resurrected dead. Conversely, he would not have
presumed that it only means some of the living godless: the unregenerate versus the regenerate. Like Jude, he would
have been content to let all mean all, though contextually limiting it to those living on Earth at the time the Lord
comes. Whereas Enoch’s prophecy is applied by Jude to all godless alive at the time of the Lord’s second coming,
Jude’s description of wandering starlike planets is not applied to the wandering starlike planets of history but to
the present manifestation of that planetary activity by those who are caught moving in the darkness when the Lord
returns.
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                    Page 61
                                           Illustration 21. Enochian All

                     The Lord comes and judges all the Living Godless
                                               Unregenerate                    Regenerate
                         Living                                                   
                         Dead                                                     

       Jude’s all is limited to the living who are moving/wandering starlike planets at the Lord’s coming. All the
living godless, both the regenerate and the regenerate, are objects of this judgment. The all being judged is limited
to the living godless on the Earth. The judgment of the dead in Hades is not in view. Neither Enoch nor Jude refers
to the judgment of resurrected dead in this particular prophecy. Their scope is limited to the living.
                                           Illustration 22. Daniel’s Sight

                        The Lord comes and judges all the Regenerate
                                                   Positive              Negative
                             Raised               Shine (PC)              Shame
                             Rescued             Inherit (PL)           Disinherit
                                       PC = Promised City     PL = Promised Land

       Daniel also poses a limitation on the group being judged in his prophecy. His prophecy is limited to some,
not all, of the godless. Admittedly, his prophecy does entail a resurrection, but it is a resurrection limited to some
of those who are dead. Consequently, it must be a resurrection of only the regenerate.
       Enoch’s prophecy dually applies to both regenerate and unregenerate godless. Daniel’s prophecy applies
dually to those rescued versus raised. Furthermore, regarding those raised, Daniel is informed of a dual outcome
for those experiencing this limited resurrection: some are graced with shining; some are disgraced with shame.
Furthermore, given what Daniel knew about the Promised Land (PL), Promised City (PC), and Enochian Prophecy
(EP), and the OT, he could make other inferences. As to the raised, those receiving a positive outcome when
resurrected would shine like stars and reside in the Promised City. Those receiving a negative outcome when
resurrected would be shamed like wandering starlike planets and reside in the comparative darkness outside the
Promised City. As to the rescued, those receiving a positive outcome when rescued would inherit the Promised
Land and live inside earthly Jerusalem. Those receiving a negative outcome when rescued would not inherit the
Promised Land and live outside the earthly Jerusalem and the Promised Land.
                                          Illustration 23. Daniel’s Insight

                        The Lord comes and judges all the Regenerate
                                                                  Positive        Negative
                            All Regenerate Raised                Shine (PC)        Shame
                          Jewish Regenerate Rescued             Inherit (PL)      Disinherit
                          Gentile Regenerate Rescued            Inherit (PL)      Disinherit
Daniel’s Insight
        Daniel knew that not all survivors would live inside the Promised Land. Gentiles would live outside of it.
Therefore, Daniel could reasonably be expected to infer that some of those rescued and left to populate the lands
outside of the Promised Land would be believing Gentiles. Since Gentiles would inhabit the land outside of the
Promised Land, Daniel had to expect that some of those rescued would be Gentiles. Just as the resurrection of the
regenerate at the time of the Lord’s return is not limited to regenerate Jews but also has to include regenerate
Gentiles, so also the rescue has to include regenerate Gentiles. He might be expected to infer that those regenerate
Gentiles who aid the Jews in their time of destress will be able to live with them in the Promised Land and thus
inherit it as well. Or he might have expected these faithful Gentiles to inherit positions of authority in the kingdoms
outside of the Promised Land. Or, better yet, he might have deduced that faithful Gentiles would be transformed
and live in the Heavenly City. Certainly, he would expect those regenerate Gentiles who did not aid the Jews in
their time of destress to be the majority of those comprising the group of subjugated Gentiles who lived outside the
Promised Land.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                       Page 62
                       Illustration 24. Terrestrial and Celestial Misthological Implications




       Daniel’s insight was not limited to the sight pictured for him in Dan 12:1-3. His sight was further informed
by the insight he had from other OT passages, as well as from Enoch’s prophecy. For instance, Daniel would have
known of Isaiah’s prophecy regarding the resurrection that was prophesied to take place when the Lord returns:
      19
         Your dead will live; their corpses will rise. You who lie in the dust, awake and shout for joy, for
      your dew is as the dew of the dawn, and the earth will give birth to the departed spirits. 20 Come, my
      people, enter into your rooms, and close your doors behind you; hide for a little while, until indignation
      runs its course. 21 For behold, the Lord is about to come out from His place to punish the inhabitants
      of the earth for their iniquity; and the earth will reveal her bloodshed, and will no longer cover her
      slain. (Is 26:19-21)

       When the Lord returns at the end of this indignation/tribulation, a resurrection of the departed spirits of the
regenerate occurs. Commenting on Is 26:19, Martin (BKC) rightly states: “This resurrection of Old Testament saints
will occur at Christ’s second coming (Dan. 12:2).” Accordingly, the resurrection of unbelievers is not in view at the
time period being prophesied. The unregenerate dead will not be raised at this time, as Isaiah prophesied a few
verses earlier: “The dead will not live, the departed spirits will not rise” (Is 26:14).69 The resurrection of which

69
   (1) The most plausible interpretation of Is 26:14 is that Isaiah denies a universal resurrection will occur at the
conclusion of Israel’s period of tribulation. The unregenerate dead are not raised at this time. (2) The second most
plausible explanation for this verse is that it denies the resurrection of the Rephaim: “They were the progeny of the
fallen angels,” and thus will not rise (Bullinger, CB). These two interpretations need not be mutually exclusive. The
denial of the resurrection of the Rephaim shows that a universal resurrection of the dead is not in view. Of course,
the Rephaim are not regenerate. So, this second interpretation is a subset of the first interpretation.
(3) Commentators, however, favor a third possibility. Ogden and Sterk (UBSH) follow the popular translation,
“They are dead, they will not live” (cp. KJV, NKJV, ESV). Supposedly, what is being denied by the antecedent they
is the resurrection of the lords in the previous verse. Gaebelein concurs: “The teaching is that the lordship of other
nations over Israel is forever gone. No other lords will ever rise again to domineer over Israel” (AB). Barnes (BN)
concurs. Smith (NAC) opts for the third interpretation: “God zealously intervened to punish all those foreign rulers,
so they would not live and their departed spirits (rĕpāʾim) would not rise again to oppress the land.” However,
UBSH acknowledges that the literal translation is, “The dead will not live” (cp. NAU). Therefore, literally, there is
no they, no antecedent referring to the lords of the previous verse. The literal interpretation is superior to the
imagined antecedent. Nevertheless, if one must assume the presence of the absent antecedent, even this
interpretation is not mutually exclusive of the preference for the first interpretation. These unregenerate foreign
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                      Page 63
Isaiah prophecies in Is 26:19 and subsequently Daniel prophecies in Dan 12:2 is only of some of the dead, not all
of the dead. A universal resurrection of all the dead is not envisioned, only the resurrection of OT believers. To be
sure, at the time Daniel was initially granted his prophecy, he could not understand its full scope, as he goes on to
explain a few verses later:
      8
        As for me, I heard but could not understand; so I said, “My lord, what will be the outcome of these
      events?” 9 And he said, “Go your way, Daniel, for these words are concealed and sealed up until the
      end time. 10 “Many will be purged, purified and refined; but the wicked will act wickedly, and none
      of the wicked will understand, but those who have insight will understand…. 13 “But as for you, go
      your way to the end; then you will enter into rest and rise again for your allotted portion at the end of
      the age.” (Dan 12:8-13)

We are living in a time when our insight can surpass what Daniel had. Nevertheless, he had enough information to
be able to draw the inferences provided in this appendix. For instance, he also knew that Isaiah’s prophecy of the
Lord’s judgment when He returns will apply also to the living Gentile nations:
      2
        Now it will come about that in the last days, the mountain of the house of the Lord will be established
      as the chief of the mountains, and will be raised above the hills; and all the [Gentile] nations will
      stream to it. 3 And many peoples [Gentiles] will come and say, “Come, let us go up to the mountain
      of the Lord, to the house of the God of Jacob; that He may teach us concerning His ways, and that we
      may walk in His paths.” For the law will go forth from Zion, and the word of the Lord from Jerusalem.
      4
        And He will judge between the [Gentile] nations, and will render decisions for many peoples
      [Gentiles]; and they will hammer their swords into plowshares, and their spears into pruning hooks.
      Nation will not lift up sword against nation, and never again will they learn war. (Is 2:2-4)

When the Lord returns, He is going to give the Promised Land to the Jews who qualify to enter it at that time.
Living Gentiles who qualify to enter the millennial kingdom will dwell peaceably outside the Promise Land.
Daniel would have known that the Lord’s judgment of those alive at the time of the Lord’s return would
include both Jews and Gentiles.

rulers are not being raised at this time. Thus, a universal resurrection of the dead at this time is denied. Walvoord
takes this approach: “The lords who are oppressing Israel are not able to raise themselves….This does not deny that
the wicked will be raised, but it does deny that they will be raised to have triumph over Israel in relation to the close
of the tribulation and the beginning of the millennial kingdom.” J. F. Walvoord, “The Resurrection of Israel,” BibSac
124 (1967): 10. (4) Yet another interpretation posed for Is 26:14 is that Isaiah mistakenly thinks that the wicked are
not resurrected. Longman (CSB): “In this verse, the wicked dead stay dead. God’s judgment will not be reversed.
A fuller understanding of the afterlife awaits the NT period (though it is anticipated in passages like Dn 12:2)”
(emphasis his). One problem with Longman’s answer is that Daniel is not talking about a universal resurrection of
the dead but a limited resurrection of the dead (including with regenerate wicked) for the very period (i.e., at the
end of the tribulation) that Isaiah allegedly denies the resurrection of the wicked. Another problem with Longman’s
proposition is that Isaiah himself affirms the resurrection of the righteous just a few verses later (Is 16:19). Longman
acknowledges the contrast between Is 16:14 and Is 16:19. A temporal contrast harmonizes the two passages: The
unbelieving dead are not raised at this time; they are raised later. This harmonization is made explicit in Rev 20:5.
One need not charge Isaiah with error if one simply allows a relative denial of the resurrection in Is 26:14, a denial
that these dead (whoever they are) are not raised at this time. This temporal harmonization can accommodate the
first three interpretations, thus invalidating the need for this forth interpretation. Wildberger (CCS) acknowledges:
“The interpretation of this verse has been hotly debated.” He only sees two solutions: the third option and a (5) fifth
one: “There is no return from death….death lets no one return.” This interpretation is refuted by Is 26:19.
Nonetheless, if one must give any credibility to this fifth interpretation, the approach taken by Constable (TCENB)
is acceptable, which is basically the third interpretation: “The prophet was not denying the resurrection of the dead
(cf. v. 19). He was simply affirming that these enemies did not continue to live nor would they rise to bother God’s
people again.” Yes, per Constable, Isaiah is denying the resurrection, not of all the dead, only of Israel’s enemies.
Yet, even here, once again, temporal harmonization rules: They do not rise at this time. They are never raised to
bother Israel, but they are raised later in the future to be judged by God for having bothered Israel in the past.

Purged Rebels
       As already noted, Daniel also knew about Ezekiel’s prophecy that the Lord would judge the Jewish nation at
that time and purge the Jewish rebels from among it:
         34
            And I shall bring you out from the peoples and gather you from the lands where you are scattered,
         with a mighty hand and with an outstretched arm and with wrath poured out; 35 and I shall bring you
         into the wilderness of the peoples, and there I shall enter into judgment with you face to face. “As I
         entered into judgment with your fathers in the wilderness of the land of Egypt, so I will enter into
         judgment with you,” declares the Lord God. 37 “And I shall make you pass under the rod, and I shall
         bring you into the bond of the covenant; 38 and I shall purge from you the rebels and those who
         transgress against Me; I shall bring them out of the land where they sojourn, but they will not
         enter the land of Israel. Thus you will know that I am the Lord. (Eze 20:34-38)

As Greenberg (AYBC) comments on Eze 20:38,

         The rebels against God (cf. 2:3) will be expelled from “the land of their sojourn” (what Canaan was to
         the patriarchs, Gen 17:8; Exod 6:4), where they preferred to remain and become like the nations. Their
         fate is that appointed in 13:9 for the false prophets of the exile. The cardinal point made in this
         subsection is that in the first, pre-land entry stage of the redemption, rebels will be purged; this point
         is stated in the selection figure of vs. 37a and enlarged upon in vs. 38..

Dyer (BKC) assumes a soteriological division so that all rebels are unbelievers:

                As the sheep will pass under the rod of the Great Shepherd, those who do not belong to Him—
         unbelieving Israelites who rebel against God—will be removed. God will not allow those sheep to
         enter His land. God’s process of purification will mean that only true sheep will enjoy the covenant
         of blessing.
                This scene described by Ezekiel is yet future. At the end of the Tribulation God will regather
         Israel to the land of promise for the Millennium (cf. 36:14–38; 37:21–23). But first the Israelites will
         be required to stand before the Lord for judgment. Those who have placed their trust in Him will be
         allowed to enter the land and participate in His kingdom (cf. John 3:3). Those who are rebels will be
         judged for their sin and banished to everlasting punishment. (Emphasis his.)

No doubt, unbelieving rebels will be not only excluded from the land of Israel but from the land of the living and
thus killed. The Lord will judge between sheep and goats (Eze 34:17). We may reasonably infer that He will slay
the unregenerate goats.70 Dyer is correct on such points. Nevertheless, the Lord will also judge between “the fat
sheep and the lean sheep” (Eze 34:10), between “one sheep and another” (Eze 34:22). We need not suppose that all
rebels are unregenerate goats who are killed or that all bad sheep are killed.
         15
            “I will feed My flock and I will lead them to rest,” declares the Lord God. 16 “I will seek the lost,
         bring back the scattered, bind up the broken, and strengthen the sick; but the fat and the strong I will
         destroy. I will feed them with judgment. 17 “And as for you, My flock, thus says the Lord God,
         ‘Behold, I will judge between one sheep and another, between the rams and the male goats. 18 ‘Is
         it too slight a thing for you that you should feed in the good pasture, that you must tread down with
         your feet the rest of your pastures? Or that you should drink of the clear waters, that you must foul the
         rest with your feet? 19 ‘And as for My flock, they must eat what you tread down with your feet, and
         they must drink what you foul with your feet!’” 20 Therefore, thus says the Lord God to them, “Behold,
         I, even I, will judge between the fat sheep and the lean sheep. 21 “Because you push with side and
         with shoulder, and thrust at all the weak with your horns, until you have scattered them abroad,
         22
            therefore, I will deliver My flock, and they will no longer be a prey; and I will judge between one
         sheep and another. (Eze 34:15-22)

70
     Cp. Amos 9:9-10; Zech 13:8-9; Mal 4:1.
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                      Page 65

The objection may be raised that the fat and strong sheep, not just goats, are destroyed with this judgment; therefore,
no unfaithful regenerate Jewish believers are left. Not so fast. One might argue that fat and strong goats are
destroyed/killed, and thus removed from the land of the living, while fat and strong sheep are merely excluded, and
thus barred from the land of Israel. On the other hand, one could concede that both the strong goats (unregenerate
Jews) and fat sheep (unfaithful regenerate Jews) are both destroyed yet insist that this judgment is different for the
two groups: the goats are destroyed physically and soteriologically (thus killed and sent to metaphysical torment),
whereas the fat sheep are destroyed misthologically (thus sent to the terrestrial outer darkness as a prelude to the
metaphysical outer darkness). Misthological destruction for unfaithful believers occurs in other passages (e.g.,
Acts 8:20; 1Cor 3:11-15; 10:5-10; Gal 6:8; Phil 3:18; Heb 10:39; Jude 1:5). And some of these passages draw upon
OT backgrounds for their misthological conclusions.71 Sometimes destruction is generic and can apply dually, that
is, soteriologically to the unregenerate and misthologically to the regenerate (Phil 1:28; 1Tim 6:9). Also, God will
raise all OT believers at this post-tribulation judgment. God does not kill the unfaithful Jewish believers only to
turn around and resurrect them and then send them to the celestial outer darkness. (The Jewish survivors are not
killed in either the parable of the unfaithful steward or unwise virgins.) Much more plausibly, unfaithful Jewish
survivors are judged as being unfit to enter the Promised Land and are sent to live with the Gentiles in the terrestrial
outer darkness. The destruction in Eze 34:15 should not be assumed to entail the physical termination of all bad
sheep. Their destruction could be terrestrial and misthological. We may reasonably infer that some of these bad
sheep will be regenerate. Their fatness will be destroyed, and their roles reversed. The lean sheep will become fat.72
        Rebel regenerate Jews are barred from the land of Israel, not removed from the land of the living
(Eze 20:38).73 This judgment of God’s harlot Jewish wife need not suppose that no regenerate Jew was involved in
this harlotry (Eze 16:15-23). Do we need to be reminded that some regenerate believers engage in spiritual harlotry
(1Cor 6:15-20)? The most reasonable supposition is that just as some regenerate Jews will be raised for shame in
Dan 12:2, so some wicked regenerate Jews will be rescued but excluded from the Promised Land (Dan 12:10).
Contrary to Dyer’s speculations, Daniel does not say that these rebels are sent to eternal damnation. Daniel merely
leaves them outside the Promised Land.
        Constable (TCENB), as already noted, also assumes that the rebels are unregenerate:

      He would weed out the rebels and transgressors from among them and bring them out of the
      countries where they lived but would not bring them into the Promised Land. This probably
      refers to the Jews who will die during the Tribulation period (cf. Zech. 13:8; Rev. 12:15–17) and
      or when the Lord returns to the earth (cf. Matt. 25:31–46).

Yet Constable’s assessment does not fit the description given by Daniel. Constable pictures the rebel Jews as dying
during the Tribulation. This cannot be! Constable acknowledges that they are brought out of the countries where
they lived. They are living in these countries when the Lord returns. They are brought out of the nations to outside

3:16–18; 4:1.” J. D. Pentecost, “The Relation between Living and Resurrected Saints in the Millennium,” BibSac
117 (1960): 333. Some of these fiery reward passages sound like an OT backdrop for 1Cor 3:11-15. Job was
