the land of Israel to be judged at the conclusion of the Tribulation. Therefore, they must have survived the
Tribulation. They are rescued from being killed by the antichrist during the Tribulation. The Lord rescues them but
finds them unfit to enter the land of Israel. Hence, they are left outside of Israel, not slain during the Tribulation.
The rebels (marad) to whom Ezekiel refers (Eze 20:38) are God’s redeemed people whom He saved out of the land
of Egypt who rebel (marah) against Him time and again (Eze 20:8,13,21) and who play the harlot with idols (Eze
20:30). They are judged by God time and again throughout the context. To assume that no regenerate tribulational
Jews will commit idolatry runs counter to Jewish history. Even the NT is replete with numerous warnings for
regenerate church-age believers not to commit idolatry.
       As to the sequence of the judgments, obviously Jewish rebels who are brought to the wilderness outside the
Promised Land will have to be purged from among those allowed to enter the Promised Land before the Gentiles
can come to the Promised Land to be instructed by the Jewish remnant that remains within it. So, this Jacobean
Bema logically precedes the Gentile Bema. As a result of this Jacobean Bema, rebel Jewish believers will be
excluded from the Promised Land and not allowed to enter it. They will have to live among the Gentile nations.
This terrestrial exclusion from the Promised Land will be their terrestrial outer darkness.
       To pose that Daniel, given some time to reflect upon the insights he had been given, would not be able to put
the pieces together seems rather incredulous. In any case, even at the initial moment Daniel was given the prophecy,
he knew that he would be raised and given his allotted portion at the end of the age. He knew that he would be
graced to shine like a star in the Heavenly City. He knew that those resurrected at that time who did not attain a
similar heavenly allotment would be raised to disgrace and not shine like a star and not attain to the Heavenly City.
Where would they be? The two most plausible conclusions he could have drawn are that (1) they would be on Earth
outside the Promised Land, thus in the terrestrial outer darkness, or (2) they would be in the heavenly realm outside
the Heavenly City, thus in a celestial outer darkness.
       The latter inference would be more probable for him to draw for several reasons. First, a celestial darkness
would be more fitting for resurrected celestial beings. Second, Enoch posed a metaphysical darkness for fallen
immortal angelic stars: “I saw a place which had no firmament of heaven above, and no firmly founded earth
beneath….This place is the end of heaven and earth. This has become a prison for the stars and the host of heaven”
(1En 18:12-14). This metaphysical prison for apostate angels will probably find a fitting counterpart for apostate
teachers. Posing some type of metaphysical darkness for immortal resurrected believers who had wandered in the
darkness like starlike planets would be a fitting parallel. Third, a terrestrial darkness outside the Promised Land
would be more naturally imposed upon those who are living in mortal bodies when the Lord returns. This terrestrial
judgment, he could deduce, would be more befitting those regenerate believers who are rescued but fail to qualify
to live in the Promised Land. Basically, Daniel had everything at his disposal to be able to anticipate the series of
judgments that would later comprise the Sheep and Goat Judgment Complex. Even if he failed to draw all the
necessary conclusions during his lifetime, however, we have no excuse for not doing so in our lifetime. The end of
time is upon us, and we have more unlocked insight than Daniel did in drawing these conclusions.
Mistholic Telescopic
       Notwithstanding the added vantage point of Daniel’s insight, Dan 12:2 is both misthological and telescopic.
The verse misthologically describes what will happen to faithful and unfaithful believers and telescopically
describes this millennial result as lasting into the eternal state. Daniel telescopically sees the millennial and eternal
misthological results simultaneously pictured together. Therefore, the Heavenly City is moved to the eternal state
in Illustration 24 to underscore the eternal nature of the eternal division that Daniel envisions commencing at the
resurrection he is describing.
Parabolic Confirmation
       Chafer’s treatment of Dan 12:2-3 is perhaps more on target than he anticipates. He is correct that the
resurrection in v. 2 is restricted to Jews and does not include unregenerate Gentiles:

      The fact that Daniel 12:2, 3 is somewhat restricted would indicate, as the context asserts, that it is only
      Daniel’s people, or Israel, who are in view. Having made reference to the incomparable trial that is
      predicted for Israel, the prophet declares, “And many of them that sleep in the dust of the earth shall
      awake, some to everlasting life, and some to shame and everlasting contempt” (Dan. 12:2). The
      restriction is to be noted in the words “many of them,” which is clearly not all that sleep in the dust of
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                       Page 67
      the earth. Doubtless those not raised at that time are the unregenerate Gentiles of whose resurrection
      there is specific revelation (cf. John 5:28; Rev. 20:12).74 (Emphasis his.)

In order to avoid the erroneous conclusion that the regenerate and unregenerate are resurrected at the same time in
one universal resurrection, dispensationalists tend to argue either that (1) a national rather than personal resurrection
for Israel is entailed75 or (2) those resurrected to everlasting life are the regenerate so that the unregenerate are
telescopically resurrected a thousand years later.76 Chafer apparently adopts the latter point of view. However, a
(3) third option is far superior: Acknowledge that the everlasting contempt is misthological, not soteriological. The
objects of misthological shame are regenerate, not reprobate. Loss of reward is in view. Daniel is only dealing with
regenerate Jews raised at this time, not unregenerate Gentiles raised a thousand years later! The context, as Chafer
admits, is only dealing with Jews, not with Gentiles. Chafer is correct to say, “Doubtless those not raised at that
time are the unregenerate Gentiles of whose resurrection there is specific revelation.” Daniel would be able to
deduce that unregenerate Gentiles would be resurrected later. Chafer is wrong, however, if he thinks this
resurrection of the unregenerate is what Daniel has in mind when he talks about being raised to shame. Daniel is
not affirming the universality of the resurrection spread out over a thousand years. He is affirming the misthology
of the resurrection as it applies to the Jewish people at the conclusion of the tribulation.
       Granted, in his Systematic Theology, Chafer does not say that the passage is dealing with soteriological
themes and indeed acknowledges that it is dealing with rewards (p. 407), so one cannot be too critical of his
statements in that section of his Systematic Theology. Referring to Eze 20:36, Chafer appropriately says that this
judgment “will occur in ‘the wilderness of the people’—evidently the very place where Jehovah pleaded with the
fathers when they came out of Egypt. This pleading will be ‘face to face’ and the judgment will result in a separation
of the [rescued] rebels and those that transgress from the rest of the congregation of Israel. These [rebels], it is said,
74
  L. S. Chafer, “Anthropology” 100 BibSac (1943): 361.
75
  Armerding is a case in point of a dispensationalist who believes that the passage is describing a national, spiritual
resurrection. The interesting thing about this perspective is that it refutes the telescopic dispensational argument:

      In Matthew 25 we read that “at midnight there was a cry made, Behold the bridegroom.” Daniel does
      not tell us how the sleepers are to be awakened. But in both passages we see two classes: in Daniel
      “some to everlasting life, and some to shame and everlasting contempt.” Of the ten virgins it is said,
      “Five of them were wise, and five were foolish.” All were awakened at the same time. We believe the
      same to be true of those mentioned in Daniel 12:2. There is no need to insert an interval where none
      is indicated. (Italics his.)

C. Armerding, “Asleep in the Dust,” BibSac 121 (1964): 157–158.
76
   In the next issue of BibSac, Walvoord refutes Armerding’s argument for national restoration from the previous
issue of BibSac. J. F. Walvoord, “The Resurrection of Israel,” BibSac 124 (1967). Walvoord traces this type of
interpretation back to Darby: “In the attempt to solve this problem a number of worthy expositors have taken the
position that this passage does not speak of resurrection, but rather of restoration. Darby seems to be the originator
of this point of view” (p. 6). However, Walvoord (p. 7) rejects it for good reason:

      The concept that restoration of Israel is intended in Daniel 12:2 has, however, some real problems in
      that the same verse mentions not only the resurrection or restoration of some to everlasting life, but
      also of some to shame and everlasting contempt. It is quite clear that the unsaved are not restored at
      the time of the second coming…. if the wicked are raised from the dead, then the righteous are also.”

Walvoord goes on to explain: “Hermeneutical consistency requires Daniel 12:2 to be interpreted literally, that is, if
the wicked are raised literally so are the righteous. The fact that both are raised literally does not mean that they are
raised at the same time” (p. 9). His point on literality is well taken. The unsaved cannot be resurrected at this time.
The unsaved must be resurrected subsequently. Walvoord’s assumption that those raised to shame are unsaved,
though, is refuted by the context. And if they are saved/regenerate, this makes his telescopic argument needless.
Nevertheless, the telescopic argument rightly rejects the notion that unregenerate Jews are raised at this time. Posing
a restoration instead of a resurrection is another strained dispensational attempt to reconcile this passage with the
dispensational framework.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                          Page 68
                                     77
shall not enter the land of Israel.” Likewise, he appropriately cites the parable of the householder (Mt 24:45-51)
and the parable of the virgins (Mt 25:1-13) as dealing with the judgment of Israel at this time. These parables
picture Jews who will be alive when the Lord returns; therefore, they deal with rescued Jews at the end of the
tribulation. However, Chafer strangely says that what is at stake is “failure to enter the kingdom” (p. 409),
seemingly implying a soteriological exclusion. On the other hand, if Chafer is limiting the kingdom to the land of
Israel, then his assessment is fine and well. In fact, he immediately proceeds to say, “Entrance for Israel into her
kingdom is made to depend upon the right use of talents (Matt. 25:14-30)…cast the unprofitable servant into outer
darkness” (p. 409).
       If “the kingdom” to which Chafer is referring is limited to “her kingdom,” that is, to “the land of Israel,”
then Chafer is correct. His analysis, as stated, is commendable if it is restrained to the actual textual implications:
      (1) The unfaithful servant is flogged, not killed.
      (2) The unwise virgins are excluded, not executed.
      (3) The rescued rebels are denied entrance into the land of Israel, not removed from the land of the living.
Proper interpretation of these parables reinforces a misthological understanding of Dan 12:2. Jesus makes explicit
what Daniel left implicit: A misthological division will occur for God’s covenant people when the Lord returns.
Neither Daniel nor Jesus is teaching that salvation from eternal damnation is by works. Jesus does not make
soteriological salvation conditioned on the right use of how Jews use their talents, and Daniel does not condition
his soteriology on their Jewish faithfulness to the covenant. Contrariwise, by placing the negative results for God’s
people who do not perform appropriately in the misthological realm rather than in the soteriological realm, both
Daniel and Jesus teach soteriological salvation by grace apart from works. Unfaithful believers are raised to shame,
not to flame.
       Chafer is correct to suspect that the shame of Dan 12:2 is associated with the outer darkness of Matthew. He
errs in assuming a soteriological association. The outer darkness is a misthological theme. If the background for
this theme is found in Dan 12:2, which is plausible, then the shame in this OT passage is most naturally taken as
misthological as well. Making entrance into the kingdom a soteriological entrance dependent upon the right use of
talents is an accursed gospel. Chafer has errored greatly and needlessly in implying a soteriological entrance
conditioned on works. Soteriological consistency requires that the shame of the outer darkness be interpreted
misthologically. Dispensational and soteriological consistency combine to require that the Danielic shame be
interpreted misthologically. Jesus says, “The sons of the kingdom shall be cast out into the outer darkness; in that
place there shall be weeping and gnashing of teeth” (Mt 8:12). These sons of the kingdom are regenerate Jews being
excluded from the Promised Land (i.e., the kingdom of Israel) at the conclusion of the tribulation. They will enter
the millennial kingdom, not their ancestral kingdom. The millennial kingdom is a temporal designator that refers to
a multitude of spatial kingdoms in existence during a thousand-year period. Unfaithful believers will be excluded
from certain spatial kingdoms within the millennial kingdom's thousand-year period. As Daniel confirms, though,
this shameful exclusion will not be limited to a temporal period but extends into misthological eternity.

77
   Lewis Sperry Chafer, Systematic Theology, vol. 4 “Ecclesiology & Eschatology” (Grand Rapids, MI: Kregel
Publications), 407. Earlier, Chafer stated that “all Israel will be raised and pass through this great assize….It is in
this judgment that Israel will be purified by the purging out of the rebels” (4:132). His statement is acceptable if it
is acknowledged that only regenerate Jews will be raised at this time, and the rebels will thus be regenerate. But
this is not Chafer’s intent, since he will later state, regarding Mt 25:1-13, “at the glorious appearing of Christ, Israel
shall be judged and many who have chosen the broad way which leads unto death cannot enter the
kingdom….the parable of the virgins represents the judgment of Israel only” (5:132-133). Therefore, Chafer is
posing that this judgment involves the resurrection of “all Israel,” and those who do not pass are sentenced to death
and unable to enter the millennial kingdom, which is a soteriological exclusion in his opinion. Thus, he has
introduced a soteriological resurrection and division in this premillennial resurrection by involving unregenerate
Jews in this resurrection. The telescopic argument would rightly reject interjecting a soteriological resurrection
before the millennium. Posing that unregenerate Jews are raised at that time is a ploy that dispensationalists should
not employ since it is counter to the dispensational framework. Such a ploy is not necessary. The solution is simply
to acknowledge that not all regenerate Jews are faithful. Their history makes this obvious. Those raised at this time
are regenerate. Some are faithful; some are not. Unfaithful resurrected Jews are not allowed into the Heavenly City.
Unfaithful rescued Jews are prevented from entering the Promised Land. The dispensational framework makes
sense. Abandoning it to pose that unregenerate Jews are raised at the end of the Tribulation is senseless and needless.

                             Illustration 25. Kingdoms Within the Universal Kingdom




       In MMP3, various forms of the kingdom within the kingdom were discussed: some spatial, some nonspatial.
The current discussion focuses on the spatial aspects of the kingdom. MMP4 and MMP5 picture the universal
kingdom as spatially containing the multiverse. Inside the multiverse is the terrestrial kingdom of Earth (along with
many other planetary terrestrial kingdoms). Part of Earth will be set aside for the Promised Land—the ancestral
kingdom of Israel. Other parts of Earth will host other nations/kingdoms (Ps 72:10-11; Is 49:23; 60:3-5,16; Lk 4:5-
6; Rev 21:24-26), as part of the terrestrial millennial kingdom, even into the eternal state. Above Earth will be a
misthopological form of the kingdom, known as New Jerusalem, as part of the celestial millennial kingdom, even
into eternity. These four spatial dimensions of the kingdom will be:
    •   Universal—soteriological
    •   Terrestrial—soteriological
    •   Ancestral—misthological
    •   Misthopological—misthological
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                        Page 70

       The Lord is King of kings. Within His Kingdom are many other kingdoms. The above illustration is parsed
down to represent the four principle spatial forms of His kingdom. (1) At the moment of new birth, a believer
becomes a permanent member of God’s family—soteriologically and spiritually/ontologically a family member of
God’s spatial universal kingdom (Col 1:13)—not just a temporary resident within it.78 Forever this believer will
live somewhere in this familial kingdom as a child of the King, sharing His nature via regeneration. (2) When the
Lord returns, some living Jewish believers will qualify to live in the Promised Land, the ethnically ancestral
kingdom promised to Abraham for his Jewish descendants. (3) Some living Gentile believers will live outside the
Promised Land somewhere else on Earth, thus in the terrestrial kingdom. (4) Heavenly Jerusalem is an unshakable
kingdom (Heb 12:22,28). Faithful believers will qualify to live in this Reward City, thus in the misthopological
kingdom.
       Normally, Jewish believers would qualify to live either in the ancestral kingdom or the misthopological
kingdom. However, if they are rescued yet found unfaithful, they will be sent to live in the terrestrial kingdom.
Otherwise, if they are resurrected and found unfaithful, they will be sent to live elsewhere in the universal kingdom,
probably in the celestial outer realm of the universal kingdom. Naturally, the names of all believers, both the faithful
and unfaithful, will be recorded soteriologically and permanently in the Book of Life since they will be living
somewhere in God’s universal kingdom as members of His family. Daniel had the means by which to come to much
the same conclusions.
Book of Life
       Many writers affirm that the Book of Life (BOL) can be used in various ways in Scripture. The BOL is used
biblically in four different ways.
                                Illustration 26. Four Meanings for the Book of Life
      (1) Book of terrestrial citizenship: it may refer to citizenship on Earth (secular usage).
      (2) Book of physical life: it may refer to life on Earth — (Ex 32:32-33; Ps 69:28a; 139:16).
      (3) Book of soteriological life: it may refer to life in Heaven — Dan 12:1-3 (dualistic); Lk 10:20
          (dualistic); 4:3 (dualistic); Rev 3:5b (dualistic); 13:8 (dualistic); 17:8 (dualistic); 20:12-15; 21:27
          (dualistic).
      (4) Book of misthological life: it may refer to life in the Heavenly Jerusalem — Dan 12:1-3 (primary);
          (Ps 69:28b; Is 4:3-4; cf. Rev 3:5b; 3:12; 21:27); Lk 10:20 (secondary); Phil 3:20; Heb 12:23);
          Rev 3:5b (secondary), 13:8 (primary); 17:8 (secondary); 21:27 (primarily).79

In some passages, dualistic usage can be allowed without conflating the meaning. These matters and passages are
discussed at length elsewhere in my writings. The focus of the present discussion is Dan 12:1-3. The range of
meaning for the BOL is expanded in the present discussion to include v. 3. Logically and grammatically, the three
verses of this Danielic passage make several affirmations regarding those whose names are in the BOL.

      “Now at that time Michael, the great prince who stands guard over the sons of your people, will
      arise. And there will be a time of distress such as never occurred since there was a nation until that
      time; and at that time your people, everyone who is found written in the book,
          [1] will be rescued.
          [2] 2 “And many of those who sleep in the dust of the ground will awake,
             these to everlasting life,

78
   This simplified distinction is being made to avoid the much more complex discussion in MMP3. Satan and the
fallen angels and the lost are presently alien residents in the universal kingdom, but they are not soteriological
members of it. The regenerate are legally and ontologically native members of the universal kingdom.

   Rev 3:5 is split between categories three and four, the first reference to name (onoma) in that verse falling into
category three, while the second usage remains in category four. Rev 21:27 implements a soteric-mistholic (or
mistholic-soteric) antithetical juxtaposition in which believers who qualify morally to enter by the gates into the
City are contrasted with immoral unbelievers outside the City. This verse is not a simple soteriological contrast. It
operates in both soteric and mistholic capacities but primarily in the misthological category. Phil 3:20 is simply
mistholic (not secondarily soteric as previously supposed). Dan 12:1-3 has been added and is considered dualistic,
but primarily mistholic.

            but the others to disgrace and everlasting contempt.
          [3] 3 “And those who have insight will shine brightly like the brightness of the expanse
          of heaven, and those who lead the many to righteousness, like the stars forever and ever.
          (Dan 12:1-3)

Keil and Delitzsch (K&D) give grammatical support for this structure. The and of v. 2 and v. 3 continues the thought
of v. 1, placing both vv. 2 & 3 in the category of those whose names are found written in the book and who participate
in the rescue and resurrection at that time:

      V. 2 is by the copula ‫[ ו‬and] connected with v. 1, and thereby designates the continuance of the
      thought of the second half of v. 1, i.e., the further representation of the deliverance of God’s
      people, namely, of all those who are written in the book of life. Since many of the ‫ ַמ ְשׂ ִכּ ִילים‬who
      know their God (Dan. 11:33) lose their life in the persecution, so in the promise of deliverance a
      disclosure of the lot awaiting those who sealed with their blood their fidelity to God was not to be
      avoided, if the prophecy shall wholly gain its end, i.e., if the promise of the deliverance of all the pious
      shall afford to the people of God in the times of oppression strength and joy in their enduring fidelity
      to God. The appeal to the fact that vv. 2 and 3 contain no designation of time proves nothing at
      all, for this simple reason, that the verses connected by “and” are by this copula placed under
      v. 1, which contains a designation of time, and only further show how this deliverance shall ensue,
      namely thus, that a part of the people shall outlive the tribulation, but those who lose their lives in the
      persecution shall rise again from the dead.

The following summational paraphrase of Dan 12:1-3 is provided to help visualize the grammatical points made by
Keil and Delitzsch. The and in vv. 2 and 3 connects them with v. 1 as occurring at that time and pertaining to those
whose names are found written in the BOL.
                      Illustration 27. Summational Grammatical Paraphrase of Dan 12:1-3
      At that time…everyone who is found written in the book
                   1. will be rescued,
                   2. and many of those dead will be raised,
                                                        • some to everlasting life,
                                                        • others to everlasting shame.
                   3. and those who have insight will shine like the stars forever and ever.

        The grammatical argument by Keil and Delitzsch is supported by logical inferences as well. Although Keil
and Delitzsch do well with part of the logical inference, their inference may need tweaking when they go on to say,
“As with the living, so also with the dead, not all attain to salvation. Also among those that arise there shall be a
distinction, in which the reward of the faithful and of the unfaithful shall be made known.” True, the passage
is talking about a distinction in reward between the faithful and unfaithful. But this is an exclusively misthological
distinction, not a soteriological differentiation. Attaining soteriological salvation is not the issue for those who are
rescued and resurrected. The grammatical analysis by K&D rightly leads to this inference. The names of those in
v. 2 are in the BOL. If Keil and Delitzsch mean to imply otherwise, then they are refuted by their own discussion.
On the other hand, their statement is true as stated in that “as with the living, so also with the dead, not all attain
salvation” in that not all the living are rescued at that time and not all the dead are resurrected at that time. Some of
those living will be killed at that time, and the dead not raised at that time will be raised later and sentenced to
eternal damnation.
        Despite the grammatical argument by K&D, some commentators assume that the only affirmation made
about those who are written in the book is that they will be rescued. Thus, the assumption is that the affirmation is
limited to v. 1. Baldwin (TOTC, 1994) is representative of those who take this view: “The book in this context is
the book of the living, as in Psalm 69:28; that is, there will be survivors, despite heavy loss of life” (emphasis his).
This type of interpretation would be in harmony with those dispensationalists who assert that when Jesus says, “The
one who endures to the end will be saved” (Mt 10:22; 24:13), He simply means that those who endure to the end of
the tribulation will be saved from physical death. Supposedly, the only thing promised is a rescue from physical
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                           Page 72
death. This answer is not satisfying, though, neither for the Danielic nor Matthean contexts. What about the land
promises? What about the kingdom promises? Are they of no import? Surely, more is entailed than mere survival
for those who endure to the end of the tribulation. Such passages cannot be limited to promising physical life. Better
interpretations of Dan 12:1 are not limited to the second of the four meanings of the BOL.
       Goldingay (WBC) treats the BOL in v. 1 as dealing with “the citizen list of true [earthly] Jerusalem. This
would be a combination of the first two of the four meanings of BOL. He proceeds to perceive v. 3 as dealing with
sharing in the glory of New Jerusalem. This assessment suffices for the standard perception of the passage: Living
faithful Jews will be rescued to live in earthly Jerusalem. Resurrected faithful Jews will be resurrected and live in
New Jerusalem. This standard perception is a good place to start for a working premise but a poor place to stay.
Several problems can be posed with its assumptions.
       First, contrary to standard dispensational thought, would not faithful Jews who endure until the end of the
tribulation qualify to live in New Jerusalem? Second, if unfaithful Jewish believers are found alive at the end of the
tribulation, are they not included in this book? Third, must one necessarily suppose that the names of those
resurrected for glory in v. 3 are excluded from the book in v. 1?
       Miller (NAC) cites Goldingay for his discussion of v. 1, but Miller does not limit himself to that perspective:

               The “book” is a common figure of speech in the Scriptures and alludes to the “book of
       life” in which the names of all saints are written (cf. Exod 32:33; Ps 69:28; Mal 3:16; Luke 10:20; Rev
       3:5; 20:12). Evidently this figure comes from the practice of keeping a record of all the citizens of a
       town. Those whose names were listed enjoyed the blessings of community membership, whereas the
       names of those who were excommunicated from fellowship were blotted out. All (Jews or Gentiles)
       who have trusted Jesus Christ as their Savior and Lord have their names written in the book of
       life. Goldingay designates this as “the citizen list of the true Jerusalem.” John the apostle related the
       sad fate of those at the final judgment whose names are not found recorded in this book, “If anyone’s
       name was not found written in the book of life, he was thrown into the lake of fire” (Rev 20:15).
Miller says that the book in Dan 12:1 refers to the Book of Life (BOL). He provides a range of meanings as to what
that could entail. He seems to settle on a soteriological preference for v. 1. This would be the third of the four
interpretations posed above for the BOL in Illustration 26. Concerning the wise who shine in v. 3, Miller (NAC)
appropriately says:
             “Those who are wise” (or “those who make others wise,” cf. 11:33) includes the resurrected
       saints of 12:2. Being in parallel with “those who lead many to righteousness,” these are believers
       generally who are spiritually wise themselves and make others wise through their life and witness.
       This wisdom includes the recognition of the truth of God and willingness to act upon it.
       Verse 3 includes v. 2; however, v. 3 is not limited to v. 2. The shining like stars in v. 3 includes the resurrected
saints of v. 2 but is not limited to it. Per Miller, the shining also includes “messianic age believers” who wisely lead
many to righteousness. Miller takes the soteriological telescopic view of v. 2 and concludes: “The resurrected saints
receive great honor and great reward, whereas the opposite is true for unbelievers.” Aside from the problems already
noted above for this soteriological telescopic view of v. 2, an additional one is deductible from the BOL. Obviously,
those who shine like stars in v. 3 have their name in the BOL. Therefore, the BOL must have misthological
implications. Just as clearly, the BOL has soteriological significance. Yet the soteric ramifications should not be
entangled with the misthological inferences. Dan 12:1-3 has a dual soteric-mistholic intention. The BOL in v. 1
includes (1) the rescued of v. 1, (2) the resurrected of v. 2, and (3) the brightly shining ones in v. 3.
       House (TOTC, 2018) correctly deduces that the promise of v. 1 is not limited to v. 1:
              Daniel is a man of scrolls (see 1:3–20; 9:1–2), and it must resonate with him to hear again (see
       Exod. 32:32–33; Ps. 69:28; Isa. 4:3) that God has a scroll with every believer’s name in it. Not one
       will be forgotten. But in what sense will they be rescued if many of them will be overthrown by wicked
       ones, as 11:35 asserts?
              2. Echoing Isaiah 26:19, the angel moves past the long days of trouble. He states that those who
       are dead (sleeping in the dust of the earth) will awake, some to everlasting life, and some to everlasting
       abhorrence. Regardless of their other disagreements, virtually all scholars conclude that [Dan] 12:2–
       3 describes the resurrection of individuals, ‘something that each individual’ will experience ‘on the
       basis of his or her response to God.’ (Emphasis his.)
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                          Page 73

       A mediating position between the two TOTC commentators is preferable. The rescue is indeed physical (per
Baldwin) and encompasses terrestrial promises (per Goldingay). The BOL encompasses those rescued and those
raised (per House). How can the BOL pertain to both those rescued and raised? Because it is the Book of
Soteriological Life (per Miller). What do those who are rescued and raised have in common? It is not that they are
both physically alive (contra House) or that they will both reside in earthly Jerusalem (contra Goldingay) or that
even they will both reside in New Jerusalem at that time.80 The first, second, and fourth meanings of the BOL are
ruled out as its common denominator. The soteriological understanding of the BOL is left. Miller rightly showed a
preference for this aspect of its meaning in this particular passage. Some of those whose names are soteriologically
written in the BOL will be rescued, namely, those believers who are physically alive at the time. Some of those
whose names are written in the BOL will be raised, namely those believers who are dead at the time.
       Lange (ACHS) appropriately believes that both the living of v. 1 and the raised of v. 2 are in this book, but
he wrongly tries to pigeonhole this range into a misthological limitation:

         The book is the same as that mentioned in the similar passage, Isa. 4:3, and hence, the book of life;
         cf. on chap. 7:10. It is, of course, not to be regarded as a “list of living Israelites” (cf. Psa. 69:29; Ex.
         32:32); nor, probably, as a “record of those who shall be delivered in the decisive hour and be permitted
         to live.” It is rather a record of those who shall inherit eternal life, a “list of the subjects of Messiah’s
         kingdom” (cf. Hitzig on the passage), of those who shall stand approved in the judgment, whether
         they live until it transpires, or are raised from the dead to meet it. (Italics his.)

Lange overlooks the significance of his admission that the BOL in v. 1 includes those alive in v. 1 and those dead
in v. 2. Those raised from the dead in v. 2 are not limited to those who will stand approved in the judgment! Quite
the contrary, v. 2 includes those who will be objects of shame. Consequently, since the BOL includes v. 2, it includes
those resurrected yet shamed. They are included in those who are in the BOL of v. 1. This BOL includes the names
of all believers, not just faithful believers, since it includes both the names of those who will shine and not shine,
the names of faithful believers and unfaithful believers.
        Some commentators argue that many means all in Dan 12:2. Even so, if that position is adopted, a relative
all is still necessitated by two facts. First, the context is only dealing with those who are raised “at that time” (see
K&D, etc.). All those raised at that time will either shine or be objects of shame. The timing, being at the end of the
tribulation and before the millennium, would still be limited to believers. This temporal phrase limits the scope of
the resurrection. Second, those raised at this time is limited by the reference to the book. The only ones raised at
this time are those whose names are written in a book, arguably the soteriological BOL since it includes both those
rescued and resurrected, both those faithful and unfaithful. Hill (EBC) says, “The figurative reference to ‘the book’
is usually equated with ‘the book of life,’ in which all the saints are enrolled (cf. Ex 32:33; Ps 69:28; Mal 3:16;
Php 4:3; Rev 3:5; 20:12).” This assessment is fine if the word saints is taken in the fullest sense to mean all
saints/believers (both faithful and unfaithful). The context requires inclusivity. Radmacher (NIBC) rightly deduces
that it is “God’s record of those who have been justified by faith.” Simply, the BOL is a record of those who have
believed in the Promised Redeemer for eternal life and thereby received it as a free gift by faith. Consequently, they
will live forever. They are rescued and raised since they have eschatological life: “Although the idea of ‘the book’
may be connected here with that of ‘the book of life (or, of the living: Hebrew seper haḥayyîm),’ in which God
keeps listed the names of all who are alive on earth, the outlook in the present context is toward eschatological life
(cf. Isa 4:3: ‘every one marked down for life’)” (Hartman, AYBC). Montgomery (ICC) is correct insofar as he goes:
“The present idea is anticipated by Is. 4:3, ‘those who are written unto life’ in the glorified Jerusalem.” The concept
is anticipated by, not limited to, those who will have life in glorified Jerusalem, whether it be the earthly or heavenly
Jerusalem. However, the concept is also applied by the context to those who are simply rescued or raised regardless
of their qualification to live in the glorified Jerusalem.
        Collins (HERM) rightly deduces that acknowledging that it as the BOL involves complexities:

                The immediate danger from which the people are delivered is the invasion of the king, but the
         deliverance is definitive and extends to all dangers. In accordance with Jewish tradition, salvation is
         envisaged in terms of the people, and the parallel with Jer 30:7 reinforces this point. A significant
80
     Lange says that it is not a book of the living Jews (thus rejecting the second of the four interpretations).
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                          Page 74
      qualification is introduced in Daniel, however; not all the people will be delivered, only those written
      in the book. The book in question is undoubtedly the book of life, as distinct from, though related
      to, the “books” of judgment in Dan 7:10 and the “book of truth” of 10:21. The notion of a “book of
      life” is well attested in the Hebrew Bible, where it seems to refer to membership of the covenantal
      community. A close parallel to this idea is found in the Book of the Heavenly Luminaries (4QDibHam),
      which was found at Qumran but is older than the Essene settlement. There a prayer similar to that in
      Daniel 9 concludes by asking God to “deliver thy people Israel…every man who is inscribed in the
      Book of Life.” 4QDibHam is dated by its editor to the mid-second century B.C.E. and may well be
      older than Daniel. In the context of 4QDibHam, the book of life can be understood on the analogy of
      Isa 4:3 to refer to the community to be restored on earth. The reference in Daniel is more complex.
      The “book” inevitably recalls the books of judgment that are opened in Dan 7:10, and the fact that
      the account of the resurrection follows immediately suggests that it is the book of eternal life.
      “Everyone who is found written in the book” includes the righteous who have not died and so
      are not resurrected in 12:2, but they too are surely destined for eternal life. The association of the
      “book of life” with resurrection radically changes its implications over against the older Hebrew
      tradition.

The BOL in Dan 12:1 is more complex than related usages that serve as a backdrop for the development of the
motif. Among other things, as Collins rightly infers, it includes both the those who have not died (v. 1) and those
who have died (v. 2)—who are “destined for eternal life.” As asserted by K&D grammatically and as deduced by
other commentators logically, the BOL in v. 1 includes the names of both those rescued in v. 1 and raised in v. 2.
Verse 3 refers to those who will shine like stars from both groups: the rescued in v. 1 and the raised in v. 2. However,
not all those rescued or raised will shine like stars. The astral glory is reserved for a limited portion of these groups.
Nevertheless, all those rescued or raised will have their names in the BOL.

                                        Illustration 28. Daniel’s Book of Life
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                     Page 75
        The Danielic book in v. 1 is surely the Book of Eternal Life, as readily deduced by many commentators. This
BOL contains the names of those described in all three verses of Dan 12:1-3, as also inferred both grammatically
and logically by various commentators. What appears to be universally missed by all commentators, surprisingly,
is that those raised to shame must, accordingly, be among those whose names are in this Book of Eternal Life. The
chart has the Book of Life color coded as green to represent life and to note that it is made up of two constituent
colors, blue and yellow, which are separated to represent those physically rescued versus physically raised. The
Danielic focus on those rescued at the conclusion of the tribulation is on the living Jewish believers, though we can
deduce with complete certainty that living Gentiles will also be rescued at this time as well. Gentiles will also be
among the elect gathered: “He will send forth His angels with a great trumpet and they will gather together His elect
from the four winds, from one end of the sky to the other” (Mt 24:31). The living believing Jewish remnant will be
gathered to the wilderness adjacent to the part of the expanded borders of the land of Israel following the Great
Middle East war.
Jacobean Bema
       The following map is adapted from http://trackingbibleprophecy.org/middle_east_war3.php. I have marked
part of the region where the children of Israel wandered in the wilderness for 40 years outside that region in red as
the probable terrestrial location of the Jacobean Bema.
                                    Illustration 29. Terrestrial Jacobean Bema




       This Terrestrial Jacobean Bema (TJB) will deal with all living regenerate Jews who survive the tribulation
and all Jewish children below the age of accountability. In the BOL chart in Illustration 28, Jewish children below
the age of accountability are considered part of the misthologically neutral group. They will enter the Promised
Land in mortal bodies. Others among this misthologically neutral group will include recent Jewish converts who
convert to Christ as their Messiah just before the Lord returns. Having not had time to do much in the way of good
or bad works since their conversion, they also will be allowed to enter the kingdom of Israel in mortal bodies.
Unfaithful Jewish believers, in contrast, will be dispersed throughout the Gentile nations and not allowed to enter
their ancestral kingdom. They will be excluded from the Promised Land. Indeed, Jesus uses the Greek word ekballo
to describe these unfaithful Jewish believers as being cast out from/of the feast (Mt 8:12). They will be cast out
from (out away from) the place where the terrestrial millennial feast will occur in the land of Israel. In fact, the
planet will probably rotate, and the landmasses come back together to form Pangea, with the kingdom of Israel
being at the top. The land on which they are standing for the TJB will probably become part of the PL (Promised
Land). Literally, they will be cast out of (out from inside of) the peripheral part of the land that will comprise the
Promised Land. In one sense, they are cast out from the PL. In another sense, they are cast out of the PL. They are
sent to the terrestrial outer darkness to live in mortal bodies among the Gentiles. Faithful living Jewish believers, in
contrast, will qualify to inherit the heavenly kingdom and thus live in New Jerusalem. They will be translated into
glorified bodies and take their residence in the Heavenly City, which will be in stationary orbit above Earth.
Redeemed Bodies Versus Glorified Bodies                                                                    Page 76
       The typological background for ekballo (cast out) goes all the way back to its usage in the LXX of the Adamic
expulsion: “And he cast out Adam and caused him to dwell over against the garden of Delight, and stationed the
cherubs and the fiery sword that turns about to keep the way of the tree of life” (Gen 3:24; LXE). Adam and Eve
were cast out of and away from the Garden of Eden. The Garden of Eden may have been where earthly Jerusalem
is today. Some would speculate that New Jerusalem is there also, shrouded in another dimension. Personally, I do
not subscribe to that position, though I postulate that it was hovering above the Garden of Eden at the time (see
MMP5). Regardless, the point remains typologically the same. Their expulsion out of/from the Garden of Eden and
the Heavenly City prefigures the expulsion of unfaithful believers out of/from the Promised Land and New
Jerusalem.
                                    Illustration 30. Celestial Jacobean Bema
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                     Page 77

       Resurrected/raised Jewish believers will probably be summoned to the Celestial Jacobean Bema (CJB) atop
the Heavenly City, which is the likely location of the Judgement Seat of Christ Bema (JSOCB), where resurrected
and raptured believers from other time periods and nations are also judged. Just as the TJB (Terrestrial Jacobean
Bema) will be adjacent to the PL (Promised Land), the CJB (Celestial Jacobean Bema) will be atop the PC
(Promised City). Those Jewish believers found faithful will reside in the PC. Those Jewish believers found
unfaithful will be cast out of/from (ekballo) the Heavenly City to live on other planets in the outer realm of the
universal kingdom (see MMP4 and MMP5). Thus, they will live in the celestial outer darkness. They will not shine
like stars. Rather, they will have celestial bodies that fail to shine and are thus objects of misthological shame.
       Not all believers will shine. Not all believers will inherit the kingdom. Not all believers will enter the
Promised Land. Some Jewish believers, who otherwise would have qualified to enter the PL because of their
national heritage, will not be allowed to enter it.81 Even Moses was used typologically to illustrate this point in that
he was not allowed to enter the Promised Land since he struck the rock the second time. The redeemed Israelites
who died in the wilderness during forty years of wandering also illustrate this point. Surely, Daniel knew that not
all redeemed Jews will be allowed into the PL or PC. Such conclusions would be rather initiative to an insightful
OT saint. Mediating upon the implications of the Danielic text and its related OT passages would certainly provide
depth to Paul’s discussion in 1Cor 15:40-58 without requiring that he spell out all the details.
       For example, why would Paul insist “that flesh and blood cannot inherit the kingdom of God; nor does the
perishable inherit the imperishable” (1Cor 15:50) and then quote a passage from Is 25:8 about death being
completely abolished in a context from Isaiah that appears to include enjoying a terrestrial millennial feast in flesh
and blood bodies? In his context, Isaiah affirms a banquet: “And the Lord of hosts will prepare a lavish banquet for
all peoples on this mountain; a banquet of aged wine, choice pieces with marrow, and refined, aged wine” (Is 25:6).
Even if this text is describing a spiritual feast that is applicable to all who enter the millennial kingdom, as
commentators commonly assert, it is not devoid of real eating. Physical eating and drinking will occur by all people
in flesh and blood bodies on Planet Earth. This conclusion cannot be spiritualized away. Those in celestial bodies
will be eating and drinking celestial food in the heavenly realm. This conclusion cannot be spiritualized away. NDEs
confirm that those in New Jerusalem are already literally experiencing this benefit.
       Those in terrestrial bodies will be eating and drinking physical food in the earthly realm. These conclusions
remain valid even if Is 25:6 is interpreted more accurately to represent the misthological enjoyment of the Messianic
kingdom pictured as a feast on God’s mountain, an enjoyment limited to those qualified to be in this misthologically
restricted part of God’s millennial kingdom. Paul is not so shortsighted as to not see that people in flesh and blood
bodies will have limited participation in millennial benefits. He is not so longwinded as to spell out all the
implications of the background material from which he draws his inferences, and he is under a gag order not to
provide details about the heavenly things he has seen. Like Daniel, he must leave it up to those who have insight in
the last days to search out the truth of the matter. Those believers who enter the millennial kingdom in flesh and
blood bodies will enjoy restricted benefits compared to those believers who enter the millennial kingdom in glorified
bodies. Faithful believers will have glorified bodies and inherit the millennial kingdom. They will rule from the
Heavenly City over the kings of the Earth. King David will be resurrected and rule over the kingdom of Israel in a
glorified celestial body. He can choose not to shine in resplendent glory while ruling on his terrestrial thrown from
earthly Jerusalem, just as Jesus did not shine in glory while appearing on Earth after His resurrection. Other kings
will rule over other kingdoms on Earth. These kings will be Gentiles who live until the end of the tribulation and
enter the millennial kingdom in flesh and blood bodies. They will be mortal, terrestrial kings. As Isaiah goes on to
explain, these terrestrial “kings will be your guardians, and their princesses your nurses. They will bow down to
you with their faces to the earth, and lick the dust of your feet; and you will know that I am the Lord; those who
hopefully wait for Me will not be put to shame” (Is 49:23).82

   We need not necessarily assume that all recent Jewish converts will enter the kingdom of Israel. Preconversional

in 4DF and MMP4. Conversely, some living Gentiles who had been semi-faithful might be given low levels of
authority to govern in their communities outside the Promised Land. They could do so in mortal bodies and
eventually even become terrestrial kings, ruling during the millennial kingdom in mortal bodies.

   When Jesus invites the faithful Gentile believers who live through the Tribulation to inherit the kingdom prepared

       Many of these terrestrial kings of the Earth will get tired of licking the dust of the feet of those whom God
has placed in elevated positions. These rebel kings will revolt at the conclusion of the millennium and be destroyed
(Rev 20:7-8). We need not conclude that all those terrestrial kings who rebel or most of their terrestrial subjects
who rebel must necessarily be unregenerate. Most of them will probably be regenerate. Rebel believers are put to
shame and misthologically destroyed regardless of the dispensation in which they live. Just ask Korah and the other
redeemed children of God who perished with him in his rebellion. Just ask the millions of Jewish believers who
were redeemed from the land of Egypt and yet perished in the wilderness because they were denied an inheritance
in the Promised Land. God has been dealing with rebellious believers for thousands of years. The end of the
millennial kingdom will be His misthological finale. He will slay them and then send them to the celestial outer
darkness at the Great White Throne Judgement (GWTJ). Unbelievers will be sent to the Lake of Fire at the GWTJ.
Unfaithful believers raised at that time will be sent to the celestial outer darkness. God will use the battle of Gog
and Magog at the end of the millennium to purge His kingdom of all rebels (Rev 20:8-9). Unregenerate rebels will
be removed from having a spatial alien presence in His universal kingdom and be placed in the Lake of Fire outside
the boundaries of this kingdom. Regenerate rebels will be removed from His terrestrial kingdom and placed in the
outer realm of His universal kingdom. The unregenerate can be removed spatially from the universal kingdom
because they are only within it spatially, not organically indigenous to this region. The regenerate cannot be
removed spatially from the universal kingdom because they organically share the nature of the King. They are
native to this heavenly realm via their sinless regenerate nature. Sin will be removed from the universal kingdom at
the beginning of the eternal state. The unregenerate must be removed from it and placed in the Lake of Fire outside
of the universal kingdom. Those having a regenerate sinless nature must remain within the universal kingdom as an
organic part of the family of believers. NT believers are an organic part of Christ’s body. They cannot be
ontologically severed from Christ and thus cast outside the universal kingdom or into the Lake of Fire.
                                                      Conclusion
       OT believers looked forward to a time of celestial and terrestrial reward. Daniel was informed of a terrestrial
and celestial differentiation that would take place at the end of the tribulation for those whose names are in the Book
of Life. Jewish believers would be rescued. Resurrected Jewish believers would experience one of two celestial
results. The faithful would shine like stars. The unfaithful would be objects of shame. Shining like stars would not
be limited to the faithful who were resurrected but also apply to believers who had faithfully endured to the end of
the tribulation. Daniel would have the background by which to infer that the glorious, righteous believers would
live in the Heavenly City and that those who did not qualify for this reward would not live in the Reward City.
Unfaithful believers would have to live somewhere in the heavenly realm outside the Heavenly City or somewhere
in the terrestrial realm outside of the Promised Land. He could reasonably anticipate that the unfaithful among the
rescued would live in the terrestrial realm outside the Promised Land and that the unfaithful among the resurrected
would live in the celestial realm outside the Heavenly City. NT parables, such as that of the householder and ten
virgins, would later confirm this perspective. Historical precedence, such as the exclusion of Moses and the majority
of the Israelites from the Promised Land, would give grounds to anticipate this to be the case.
       The prophesied removal of rebels at the end of the tribulation would give him reason to anticipate a
misthological application of this separation to unfaithful Jewish believers at that time. Mortals would be excluded
from the Promised Land, immortals from the Promised City. Through Enochian prophecy, he might infer that the
unfaithful resurrected immortal believers might wander somewhere in the darkness outside of the Heavenly City

for them from the foundation of the Earth (Mt 25:34), He is not inviting them to become terrestrial kings of the
Gentile kingdoms. The inheritance of these faithful sheep is eternal (Heb 9:15). Flesh and blood cannot inherit this
imperishable kingdom (1Cor 15:50). Jesus will be inviting these faithful tribulational believers to inherit the Earth
by coming to (1) live in the Celestial City (2) in glorified, immortal, celestial bodies—from which and by which,
respectfully—they will rule the terrestrial kings and kingdoms of the Earth. As ruling members of the Bridal City,
these Gentiles will become part of the Bride of Christ and rule from the Bridal City. In making these assessments,
I am tweaking some previous speculations in my earlier writings that faithful Gentiles might live in the Promised
Land or that the kings of the Earth would be celestial kings. More likely, the kings of the Earth are mortal kings
during the millennial kingdom. Faithful mortal kings of the millennial period will become smortal kings during the
eternal state. See MMP5 for much more detailed assessments and discussions. Smortals, for example, are defined
therein as sinless, semi-transformed, sexually-reproducing, semi-mortal humans in the eternal state. The
conclusions reached herein are in harmony with the much more detailed analysis in that newer book.
Appendix 2. Danielic Stars                                                                                   Page 79
like rogue planets. However, he would be aware that they would not be sent to eternal flames, only dwell in eternal
shame since their names are in the Book of Life. Whatever correlation that might exist between the unregenerate
cast into the darkness of the eternal flame and the regenerate cast into the darkness of eternal shame would not erase
fundamental distinctions in their fates.
       Similarly, Daniel would not need to assume that rebels whom Ezekiel prophesied being removed would all
necessarily suffer identical fates. Regenerate rebels would have their names in the Book of Life and thus not share
the same fate as unregenerate rebels who did not have their names in the Book of Life. Both groups might be
destroyed in some sense of the word (Eze 34:15). Yet this destruction need not mean the same thing in both cases.
For the regenerate fat sheep who were destroyed, Daniel might suppose various possibilities. Their fatness might
be destroyed, and they might become lean through role reversal. After all, Ezekiel prophesied the removal of
regenerate rebels from the land (Eze 20:8,13,21,38). Indeed, Daniel might suspect, given his own prophecy, that
this removal would have both terrestrial and celestial misthological implications. Or he might regard Ezekiel’s
prophecy in Eze 34:15 as limited to the unregenerate and his own prophecy as limited to the regenerate. After all,
Daniel would perceive that the rescue and resurrection of which he himself writes is limited to those Jews whose
names are in the Book of Life. He would have grounds to anticipate similar outcomes for Gentile believers.
       Dispensationalists have posed various theories as to how to deal with Daniel’s prophecy in Dan 12:1-3. Some
take it as including a partial resurrection of unbelievers. Some take it as including a delayed resurrection of
unbelievers. Some deny that it is a resurrection of individuals and insist that it is merely a national resurrection.
Rarer than gold is the dispensationalist who correctly perceives what Daniel would have perceived—that those who
are objects of shame have their names in the Book of Life so that the issue at stake is misthological shame, not
soteriological shame in eternal flames. Not recognizing the misthological nature of the shame has led some
otherwise fine dispensationalists to make some grievous errors, even to the point of making soteriological salvation
conditioned on performance, thereby teaching an accursed gospel at this and similar junctures. Their lack of insight
might prevent them from shining like stars or at least diminish the stellar radiance they otherwise would be expected
to achieve. Paul’s statement that star differs from star in glory will likely have various degrees of misthological
applicability to interpreters who misconstrue the misthological nature of the shame in Dan 12:2.

                        Illustration 31. Celestial versus Terrestrial Outer Darkness
                         Appendix 3: Unglorified Believers
                                                       Introduction
       Based on texts such as Rev 20:6 and Lk 20:35, some misthologists believe that only faithful believers
participate in the resurrection at the end of the tribulation. Unfaithful believers are purportedly not resurrected until
after the millennial kingdom. Such misthologists are kingdom exclusionists (posing that unfaithful believers are
excluded from the millennial kingdom), and some of these kingdom exclusionists are Gehenna misthologists
(posing that unfaithful believers are punished in Gehenna during this 1000-year period). TOD was opposed to
spatial-kingdom-exclusion misthology since, for instance, 1Thess 4:17 affirms that at the point of the rapture, all
believers will always be with the Lord somewhere in His kingdom. Gehenna misthology was found to be even more
objectionable since it not only excludes unfaithful believers from the Lord but places them in Gehenna during the
millennial kingdom. A Gehenna Bema might be posed, but a 1000-year dip in the Lake of Fire should be opposed.
       Nonetheless, a limited form of spatial-kingdom-exclusion misthology is acknowledged in the modified model
depicted herein for Illustration 25. Kingdoms Within the Universal Kingdom (p. 69). At the beginning of the
millennial kingdom, unfaithful resurrected believers are excluded from the misthopological kingdom, the ancestral
kingdom, and the terrestrial kingdom. They are sent to the outer realm of the universal kingdom, which is their
celestial outer darkness. In this sense, I can concur with kingdom exclusionists who insist that unfaithful believers
are excluded from the millennial kingdom—when the millennial kingdom is limited to parts of the universal
kingdom normally associated with Earth: (1) the terrestrial kingdom composing the bulk of the planet for the Gentile
nations, (2) the ancestral kingdom comprised of the Promised Land for the Jewish nation, and (3) the
misthopological kingdom inhabited by overcomers who live in the Heavenly City in a stationary orbit above the
planet. Unfaithful resurrected believers would be excluded from these three kingdoms and thus excluded from the
millennial kingdom when it is defined narrowly in this sense.
       Understandably, some misthologists will ask, Why pose that unfaithful believers are resurrected at the
beginning of the millennial kingdom only to be sent to the outer celestial realm? Why not allow a limited
resurrection to texts such as Rev 20:6 and Lk 20:35? Conceptually, such a proposition is plausible. Theoretically,
the resurrection of unfaithful believers could be delayed for 1000 years. The modified model in Illustration 25
would allow such a possibility if it were deemed exegetically prudent. Indeed, Connell takes such a position and is
worthy of consideration as posing a competing misthological model since his discussion is superior to several
misthologists taking a similar perspective.
                                                  Eternal Rewards
       Some kingdom exclusionists mistakenly think that misthological exclusion from the kingdom is limited to
the millennial kingdom, so rewards, in their opinion, are limited to the millennial kingdom and thus only last 1000
years. To his credit, Connell repeatedly stresses that rewards are eternal. For instance, he correctly states:

      These rewards that He promises - they begin in the millennial reign of Christ (the 1000 years
      when He rules on the earth, literally), and they go on for eternity. When you come to the point
      where that kingdom starts, whatever He decides, regarding you, is irreversible, and
      unchangeable. You are what you are, and you have qualified or disqualified; or qualified to a
      certain level, or whatever.83

Connell’s recurring stress on the eternal, irreversible, and unchangeable nature of rewards throughout his book, in
addition to his overall excellent misthology, makes his book a commendable read. The logical implication of such
a position would be that any limitation placed on unfaithful believers during the millennial kingdom would also
apply to the eternal state in the eternal kingdom. Millennial misthological distinctions are eternal misthological
distinctions. His discussion raises some questions as to how this is possible, however. My model supplies the
necessary details as to how to answer such questions.
